Refuting the Generative Power Hypothesis
Razi refutes the generative power hypothesis with a two-branch argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Razi rejects the idea that a blind 'generative power' within the sperm can account for the complex human form, using arguments from the nature of powers and matter.

Core Idea
Blind forces produce simple shapes, not complex organisms.

Imam al-Razi anticipates an objection: perhaps a 'generative power' (quwwa muwallida) inherent in the sperm is responsible for the transformation. In this lesson, we examine his detailed refutation, which shows that such a blind power would necessarily produce spherical forms, not the intricate structure of a human body.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and refutation
Read the Arabic from 'لا يقال' to the end. The objection posits a generative power. Razi's response has two parts.
Follow the logic carefully.
2
Analyze the first branch: consciousness
Razi first considers if the generative power has consciousness and choice. If so, the sperm would be perfectly wise and powerful—impossible. If not, continue to the next step.
3
Analyze the second branch: simplicity vs. composition
If the power lacks consciousness, the sperm's matter determines the result. Razi argues a simple power on homogeneous matter produces a sphere; composite matter would yield multiple spheres. Neither matches the observed complex body.
This is based on Aristotelian physics.
4
Conclude the necessity of a wise creator
Since both possibilities fail, the generative power cannot be the cause. Therefore, the actual cause must be a wise external influencer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطريق الثالث : حدوث الأعراض مثل ما لشاهده من انقلاب النطفة علقة ثم معنفة ثم لا اودما فلا بد من مور ، وليس المؤثر هو الإنسان ولا أبواه ولابد من شيء آخر .
لا يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يكون المؤثر هو القوة المولدة المركوزة في النطاقة .
لانا نقول : تلك القوة إما أن يكون لها شعور واختيار في التكرين وإما أن لا يكون ، والأول باطل وإلا لكانت النطقة موصوفة بكمال القدرة والحكمة وهو معلوم الفساد بالبديهة ، و الثانى أيضاً باطل لأن النطفة إما أن تكون جسما متشابه الأجزاء في الحقيقة وإما أن لا تكون كذلك ، فإن كان الأول لزم أن يخلق النطفة كرة لأن القوة البسيطة إذا أثرت في المادة البسيطة لابد وأن تفعل فعلا متشابها وهو الكرة وهذا هو الذي عليه تحويل الفلاسفة فى كرية البائط، وإن كان الثانى كانت النطفة مركبة من البسائط وكل واحد من تلك البائط يكون القائم بها قوة بسيطة وذلك يقتضى الكروية فيلزم أن تتخلق النطفة كرات مضموم بعضها إلى البعض ، ولما بطل ذلك علينا أن المؤثر فى خلق أبدان الحيوانات والنبات مؤثر حكيم .
The third way: the occurrence of accidents, like what is witnessed from the transformation of the sperm-drop into a clot, then into a chewed lump, then into flesh and bones? So there must be an influencer, and the influencer is not the human nor his parents, and there must be something else. It cannot be said: why is it not permissible that the influencer be the generative power embedded in the sperm-drop? For we say: that power either has consciousness and choice in the formation or not. The first is false, otherwise the sperm-drop would be described with complete power and wisdom, and that is known to be false intuitively. The second is also false, because the sperm-drop either is a body similar in parts in reality or not. If the first, then the sperm-drop would be created as a sphere, because a simple power, when it acts on a simple matter, must produce a similar action, which is the sphere, and this is what the philosophers have transmitted regarding the sphericity of the simple? If the second, then the sperm-drop is composed of simples, and each of those simples has a simple power subsisting in it, and that necessitates sphericity, so it would follow that the sperm-drop is created as spheres attached to one another. And when that is invalidated, it is incumbent on us that the influencer in the creation of the bodies of animals and plants is a wise influencer.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof (الطريق الثالث) for the existence of a wise Creator, which is based on the occurrence of accidents (حدوث الأعراض). The proof uses the example of the transformation of a sperm-drop (نطفة) into a clot (علقة), then into a chewed lump (مضغة), and then into flesh and bones. This orderly transformation requires an external influencer (مؤثر), and neither the human nor its parents are the cause; therefore, there must be another agent. The author then anticipates an objection: why not attribute this to a 'generative power' (قوة مولدة) inherent in the sperm? He refutes this by analyzing the nature of that power. If it had consciousness and choice, the sperm would have to be endowed with perfect power and wisdom, which is obviously false. If it lacked consciousness, then the sperm, being either homogeneous or composite, would produce a spherical shape (a simple action from a simple power) or multiple spheres, which is not what we observe. Since neither scenario yields the observed complex and orderly forms, the only remaining conclusion is that the actual influencer is a wise external agent.

Key Terms
القوة المولدة
النطفة
الكرة
البسيط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A blind natural force (like a 'generative power') is sufficient to produce complex biological forms.
Correction: Razi shows that a blind force, whether conscious or not, would produce simple spherical shapes, not the intricate structure of a human body.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's refutation, what would happen if the sperm is homogeneous and the generative power lacks consciousness?
A) The sperm would become a perfect sphere.
B) The sperm would become a cube.
C) The sperm would develop into a human directly.
D) The sperm would remain unchanged.

Key Takeaway
A blind generative power cannot produce complex life; only a wise creator can.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine your own body - the way your heart beats without your command, the intricate design of your eye - do you ever stop to consider that blind processes could not have caused this? What would it mean for your life if you truly accepted that a Wise Creator fashioned you?
?
Razi argues that a non-conscious power would only produce simple shapes. Have you ever attributed the complexity of your life circumstances to mere chance or natural forces? How would your response to trials change if you saw them as designed by a Wise Being?
?
The sperm is devoid of intelligence, yet it becomes a human. Have you ever reflected on the absolute power and wisdom required to bring you from such a humble beginning? What gratitude or submission does that demand from you today?

The Fourth Proof: Possibility of Accidents
Imam Razi states the fourth proof and its logical reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's argument that bodies identical in essence require an external cause for their differing attributes, grounded in the principle that possibility necessitates an influencer.

Core Idea
Bodies are equal in essence, so their differences must come from outside.

Imam Razi presents his fourth proof for God's existence, focusing on the accidents (attributes) of bodies. He argues that since all bodies share the same corporeal essence, any specific attribute they possess is merely possible and not necessary. The actual specification therefore demands an external cause. This lesson unpacks that reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Let the rhythm of the argument sink in before analysing.
Notice the phrase 'الإمكان محوج إلى المؤثر' – a key principle from earlier.
2
Understand the key premise
Focus on the statement 'الأجسام متساوية في الجسمية'. Why does equality in essence mean that any specific attribute is merely permissible (جائز)? Think about what it implies for differentiation.
If all bodies are the same in essence, why would one be hot and another cold?
3
Follow the logical conclusion
See how the argument moves from possibility to necessity: since attributes are merely possible, something external must make them actual. This is the principle 'الإمكان محوج إلى المؤثر'.
This proof is a classic kalam argument for a creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطريق الرابع : إمكان الأعراض وتقريره أن نقول الأجسام متساوية في الجسمية فاختصاص كل واحد منها بماله من الصفات يكون جائزا لأن كل ماصح على الشيء صح على مثله والإمكان محوج إلى المؤثر على ما تقدم
The fourth way: the possibility of accidents, and its exposition is that we say: bodies are equal in corporeality, so the specification of each one of them with its attributes is [merely] permissible, because whatever is valid for a thing is valid for its like, and possibility necessitates an influencer, as previously established.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof (الطريق الرابع) for the existence of God, which is based on the possibility of accidents (إمكان الأعراض). Imam Razi states that bodies are equal in their essence (corporeality/جسمية). Since they are essentially the same, any specific attributes that distinguish one body from another (such as shape, color, etc.) are merely possible; they could have been otherwise. The principle is that whatever is valid for one thing is valid for its like (i.e., if one body can have a particular attribute, any other body of the same kind could also have it). Therefore, the actual specification of each body with its particular set of attributes cannot be due to the essence itself, but requires an external influencer (مؤثر). This conclusion is built on the previously established rule: 'possibility necessitates an influencer' (الإمكان محوج إلى المؤثر). Thus, the existence of diverse attributes in otherwise identical bodies points to a cause that chooses which attributes each body receives.

Key Terms
إمكان الأعراض
الجسمية
الصفات
الإمكان محوج إلى المؤثر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Bodies can differentiate themselves by their own essence.
Correction: Razi emphasizes that all bodies are equal in corporeality, so any attribute is equally possible for any body. Therefore, the actual differences cannot come from the bodies themselves but must be assigned by an external cause.

Checkpoint
Why does Imam Razi argue that bodies cannot acquire their specific attributes by themselves?
A) Because bodies are made of different atoms.
B) Because all bodies are equal in their essence (corporeality), so any attribute is merely possible and needs an external cause.
C) Because attributes are eternal and uncreated.
D) Because bodies have free will to choose their attributes.

Key Takeaway
The diversity of attributes in essentially identical bodies proves the need for an external determiner, which is God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you genuinely believed that your very existence and every attribute you have is not self-derived but chosen for you by an external Influencer, how would it change your attitude towards humility and gratitude? Can you point to one specific attribute (talent, health, opportunity) that you have been taking for granted as self-made? What would it mean to trace it back to the Influencer today?
?
The proof shows that any attribute could have been different for any body. When you look at your own body and life circumstances, do you ever feel a sense of contingency—that it could have been otherwise? How does that feeling of 'possibility' drive you to acknowledge the One who actualized one specific possibility over others? Write down one concrete way you will respond to that contingency today.
?
Consider the principle: 'whatever is valid for a thing is valid for its like.' If your neighbor had been born with your same essence, would they deserve your blessings as much as you do? How does this dissolve any sense of entitlement in your heart, and what does it demand from you in terms of thankfulness and service?

The World's Director Must Be the Necessary Being
Imam Razi proves the director of the world must be necessary by refuting circularity and infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's logical proof that the director of the world must be a necessary being, by refuting circular causality and infinite regress.

Core Idea
Every chain of possible beings must terminate in a Necessary Being.

Imam Razi presents a concise yet powerful argument for the existence of a Necessary Being. He begins by considering the director of the world: if it is necessary, we are done; if it is possible, it requires a cause. This then leads to three possibilities: circularity, infinite regress, or termination in a necessary being. Razi shows that the first two are logically impossible, thereby proving the third.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once and its translation to get the overall flow of the argument.
Notice the concise, logical style typical of kalam.
2
Identify the disjunction
The passage begins by splitting the director into two options: necessary or possible. Understand that if possible, it needs a cause.
3
Understand the refutation of circularity
Follow the argument that mutual dependency leads to each being prior to the other, which implies self-priority—a contradiction.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
4
Understand the refutation of infinite regress
See that an infinite chain of possible beings would itself be possible and thus require an external cause, breaking the regress.
The key move is treating the entire series as a whole.
5
Conclude the proof
Realize that the only remaining possibility is termination at a Necessary Being, which is the goal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : مدير العالم إن كان واجب الوجود فهو المطلوب وإن كان جائز الوجود افتقر إلى مؤثر آخر فإما أن يدور أو يتسلسل أو ينتهى إلى واجب الوجود وهو المطلوب .
أما بطلان الدور فلان الشيء إذا احتاج إلى غيره كان المحتاج إليه متقدما في الوجود على المحتاج ، فلو افتقر كل واحد منهما إلى الآخر لمكان كل واحد منهما متقدما في الوجود على الآخر فيلزم أن يكون كل واحد منهما متقدما على المتقدم على نفسه ، ومتقدم المنقدم متقدم ، فالشيء متقدم على نفسه هذا خلف .
وأما بطلان التسلسل فلان مجموع تلك الأمور التي لا نهاية لها مفتقر إلى كل واحد منها وكل واحد منها ممكن والمفتقر إلى الممكن ممكن فالمجموع ممكن وكل ممكن فله مؤثر فالمجموع له مؤثر والمؤثر إما نفس ذلك المجموع أو أمر داخل فيه
Problem: The director of the world—if it is Necessary of Existence, then that is the goal; and if it is possible of existence, it needs another influencer, then either it leads to a circle or an infinite regress or ends at the Necessary of Existence, which is the goal. As for the invalidity of circularity: because a thing, when it needs another, the needed is prior in existence to the needy; so if each of them needs the other, then each would be prior in existence to the other, necessitating that each of them is prior to the one prior to itself, and the prior of the prior is prior, so the thing is prior to itself—this is a contradiction. And as for the invalidity of infinite regress: because the sum of those things which have no end is dependent on each one of them, and each one of them is possible, and that which is dependent on the possible is possible, so the sum is possible, and every possible has an influencer, so the sum has an influencer, and the influencer is either that same sum or something within it.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage is a self-contained problem (مسألة) that examines the nature of the world's director (مدير العالم). The argument is a classic kalam proof for the existence of a Necessary Being. It begins with a disjunction: either the director is necessary (واجب الوجود) or possible (جائز الوجود). If necessary, the conclusion is reached. If possible, it requires another cause. This leads to three possibilities: an infinite regress (تسلسل), a circular dependency (دور), or ending at a Necessary Being. The author then refutes circularity: if A depends on B and B depends on A, then each would be prior to the other, which implies self-priority—a contradiction. Next, he refutes infinite regress: the infinite series as a whole depends on each member, and each member is possible, so the whole is possible. Every possible requires an external influencer. That influencer cannot be the series itself (since then it would depend on its own parts) nor something within it (since that would be part of the series and thus also possible). Therefore, the series must terminate in a cause that is necessary—the Necessary Being.

Key Terms
مدير العالم
واجب الوجود
جائز الوجود
الدور
التسلسل
المجموع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Infinite regress is a valid explanation for the chain of causes.
Correction: Imam Razi shows that an infinite regress of possible beings is impossible because the entire series would itself be possible and require an external cause, thus necessitating a necessary being.

Checkpoint
How does Imam Razi refute infinite regress?
A) He says it leads to a contradiction in priority.
B) He argues the infinite series of possibles is itself possible and needs an external cause.
C) He says infinite regress is forbidden by divine command.
D) He says it is impossible because time is finite.

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi's proof shows that any chain of possible causes must ultimately lead to a Necessary Being, by eliminating circularity and infinite regress as logical possibilities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reflect on your own life: In what ways do you try to be self-sufficient, as if you were a necessary being rather than a contingent one? How does this proof challenge that illusion?
?
The refutation of circularity shows that depending on something that depends on you is logically impossible. Is there any relationship or habit in your life where you are seeking validation from something that ultimately depends on you? How can you break that cycle and turn to the Necessary Being instead?
?
This proof eliminates infinite regress—there must be a First Cause. Are there areas in your life where you keep deferring ultimate accountability, always finding a 'next reason' to avoid facing the ultimate reality of Allah? What would it mean for you today to stop the regress and submit to the Necessary Being?

The Third Proof: From Animal Generation
Razi presents and refutes the generative force hypothesis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument from the generation of animal bodies and why the generative force hypothesis fails to explain it.

Core Idea
No simple force can produce complex forms; a wise creator is necessary.

Razi presents a third way to prove the existence of a wise Creator. He observes the transformation of a sperm into a complex animal body. He then refutes the idea that an inherent generative force could be responsible, using a logical dichotomy to show that such a force would lead to absurd consequences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to understand the flow of the argument.
Notice the concise philosophical style of Razi.
2
Identify the empirical starting point
Razi begins with an observable fact: the transformation of a sperm into a clot, then a chewed-like lump, then flesh. This necessitates a causative agent.
This is the premise of the argument.
3
Trace the first dichotomy
Razi considers the objection that the generative force in the sperm might be the agent. He then splits this possibility: either the force has consciousness and choice, or it does not.
Follow how Razi eliminates both branches.
4
Understand the second dichotomy
For the unconscious case, Razi further splits: the sperm is either perfectly homogeneous or composite. Each leads to spherical shapes, which are not observed.
This is the core refutation.
5
Conclude the need for a wise agent
Since all natural explanations fail, the agent must be a wise creator external to the sperm.
This is the conclusion of the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطريق الثالث : حدوث الأعراض مثل ما نشاهده من انقلاب النطفة علقة ثم مضغة ثم لحما فلا بد من مؤثر ، وليس المؤثر هو الإنسان ولا أبواه ولابد من شيء آخر . لا يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يكون المؤثر هو القوة المولدة المركوزة في النطفة . لانا نقول : تلك القوة إما أن يكون لها شعور واختيار في التكوين وإما أن لا يكون ، والأول باطل وإلا لكانت النطفة موصوفة بكمال القدرة والحكمة وهو معلوم الفساد بالبديهة ، والثاني أيضاً باطل لأن النطفة إما أن تكون جسما متشابه الأجزاء في الحقيقة وإما أن لا تكون كذلك ، فإن كان الأول لزم أن يخلق النطفة كرة لأن القوة البسيطة إذا أثرت في المادة البسيطة لابد وأن تفعل فعلا متشابها وهو الكرة وهذا هو الذي عليه تحويل الفلاسفة في كرية الفلك، وإن كان الثاني كانت النطفة مركبة من البسائط وكل واحد من تلك البسائط يكون القائم بها قوة بسيطة وذلك يقتضي الكروية فيلزم أن تتخلق النطفة كرات مضموم بعضها إلى البعض ، ولما بطل ذلك علمنا أن المؤثر في خلق أبدان الحيوانات والنبات مؤثر حكيم .
The third way: (proof from) the origination of accidents, such as what we observe of the transformation of the sperm into a clot, then a chewed-like lump, then flesh, so a causative agent is necessary. The agent is not the human nor his parents, and there must be something else. It is not to be said: 'Why may the agent not be the generative force embedded in the sperm?' For we say: that force either has consciousness and choice in formation, or it does not. The first is false, otherwise the sperm would be described with perfect power and wisdom, which is known to be false by intuition. The second is also false, because the sperm is either a body whose parts are homogenous in reality, or not so. If the first, then necessarily the sperm would produce a sphere, because a simple force when acting on simple matter must produce a uniform action, which is the sphere; and this is what the philosophers hold regarding the sphericity of the celestial sphere. If the second, then the sperm is composed of simple elements, and each of those simple elements has a simple force inhering in it, which necessitates sphericity, so the sperm would be generated as spheres joined together. When this is false, we know that the agent in the creation of the bodies of animals and plants is a wise agent.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage is a proof (the third way) for the existence of a wise Creator, specifically from the generation of animal bodies. Razi begins by stating the empirical fact: we observe the transformation of a sperm into a clot, then a chewed-like lump, then flesh—thus there must be a causative agent. This agent cannot be the human person or his parents because they are not responsible for the detailed formation. Then Razi presents and refutes the objection that the generative force inherent in the sperm might be the cause. He divides this force into two possibilities: either it has consciousness and choice, or it does not. If it does, then the sperm itself would possess perfect power and wisdom, which is intuitively false. If it does not, then the sperm is either perfectly homogeneous or composite. If homogeneous, a simple force would produce a uniform spherical effect (as per philosophers' view on celestial spheres), which is not observed (the embryo is not a sphere). If composite, each simple element would produce spheres, resulting in a collection of spheres, also not observed. Since both possibilities fail, the agent must be a wise agent external to the sperm. The argument uses a dichotomy to eliminate all natural explanations, leaving a wise Creator.

Key Terms
القوة المولدة
شعور واختيار
مادة بسيطة
كرة
بسائط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is purely empirical and based on observation alone.
Correction: The argument is philosophical: it uses logical dichotomies to refute naturalistic alternatives, not just observation.

Checkpoint
In the third argument, what does Razi conclude about the force generating the sperm?
A) It is a conscious agent because the sperm knows what to become.
B) It cannot be the generative force because either scenario leads to absurdity.
C) It is the parents themselves who form the embryo.
D) The sperm's force is simple and produces a sphere.

Key Takeaway
Razi's third argument refutes naturalistic explanations of animal generation, pointing to a wise Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the intricate formation of your own body from a drop of sperm, are you willing to attribute it to an unconscious force or to a wise Creator? Which is more intellectually honest, and what does your answer reveal about your submission to Allah’s power?
?
Razi argues that if the generative force had consciousness, the sperm would possess perfect wisdom—which is absurd. Yet you often rely on your own wisdom to plan your life. How does this argument challenge your habit of attributing success to your own intelligence rather than to the Creator who formed you?
?
If the simplest natural explanations fail to account for the complexity of even a single organism, how does that unsettle the assumptions you hold about your own independence from Allah? What would it mean for you to truly acknowledge that every detail of your existence is the direct act of a wise Creator?

The Fourth Way: Contingency of Accidents
Razi states the fourth proof: contingency of accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the equality of bodies in their essence and their differing properties proves the necessity of a cause for those properties.

Core Idea
Identical bodies with different properties must have an external cause determining those differences.

Imam Razi presents the fourth of the classical proofs for God's existence, grounded in the observable world of bodies and their attributes. This argument starts from a simple observation: all bodies share the same essential nature (corporeality), yet each body is uniquely characterized by a set of accidental properties. The fact that these properties could have been otherwise points to a cause that particularizes them.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Notice the concise phrasing and the logical flow from premise to conclusion.
The Arabic uses a single sentence with multiple clauses; try to track the argument step by step.
2
Identify the key premises
Razi states two premises: (1) bodies are equal in corporeality, (2) whatever is true of one thing is true of its like. Ensure you see how these lead to the conclusion that particularization is contingent.
3
Understand the role of contingency
The argument relies on the earlier principle that contingent things require a cause. Recognize that 'particularization' here means each body having its specific properties instead of others.
Contingency means it could have been otherwise; hence a cause is needed to choose one possibility.
4
Connect to the conclusion
The cause of this particularization is the Necessary Existent (God). Reflect on how this argument differs from simply pointing out variation—it uses the identity in essence to necessitate an external determinant.
This is a core argument in kalam: the contingency of accidents leads to the existence of a cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطريق الرابع : إمكان الأعراض وتقريره أن نقول الأجسام متساوية في الجسمية فاختصاص كل واحد منها بما له من الصفات يكون جائزا لأن كل ما صح على الشيء صح على مثله والإمكان محوج إلى المؤثر على ما تقدم .
The fourth way: the contingency of accidents. Its formulation is that we say: bodies are equal in corporeality, so the particularization of each one with the properties it has would be contingent, because whatever is true of a thing is true of its like, and contingency necessitates a causative agent, as previously established.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage states the fourth proof for the existence of God, based on the contingency of accidents. Razi asserts that all bodies are equal in their essence (corporeality) but each body has specific properties (e.g., color, shape). Since bodies are identical in corporeality, any property found in one could equally be found in another (principle of like effects). Therefore, the particularization of each body with its specific set of properties is contingent—it could have been otherwise. Contingency, as established earlier, requires an external cause to select one possibility over another. That cause is the Necessary Existent. The argument is concise and relies on the earlier principle that contingent things need a determinant.

جسمية
اختصاص
مثل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument merely points out that bodies have different properties, which is obvious and doesn't prove anything.
Correction: The argument is more precise: it uses the equality of bodies in their essence to show that their differing properties are not necessitated by that essence. This contingency requires an external cause, thus proving the existence of a necessary being.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the fourth way?
A) Bodies are different in their essential nature.
B) Bodies are equal in corporeality but differ in accidents, requiring a cause.
C) Accidents are eternal.
D) The soul is distinct from the body.

Key Takeaway
The equality of bodies in their essence, combined with their differing properties, reveals that those properties are contingent and thus require a cause, which is Allah.

How will you thank Him today for the particular way He made you?
?
Consider something about your appearance or health that you often take for granted. The fact that it is a contingent accident—not necessary to your existence—means it could have been otherwise and is sustained by a Cause. Are you living as if your very existence is a gift held by Allah every moment? What one thought or action can you change today to reflect that awareness?
?
The argument concludes that contingency necessitates a causative agent. Your own life is filled with contingencies—your choices, your circumstances, your very breath. If you truly believed that each contingency is held by Allah, would you still worry about tomorrow as you do? What specific worry can you entrust to Him right now, based on the certainty that He is the Causer of all things?

Proof that the Director of the World Must Be Necessary
Razi proves the world's director must be necessary, refuting circularity and infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that the director of the world must be a Necessary Existent, and how he refutes the alternatives of circularity and infinite regress.

Core Idea
The world's director cannot be contingent; it must be self-sufficient or lead to absurdity.

Razi presents a tight logical trilemma: the director of the world is either necessary (the desired conclusion) or contingent. If contingent, it requires a cause, leading to either a vicious circle or an infinite regress. Both are impossible, so the director must be necessary. This passage is a classic kalam proof for God's necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Note the structure: trilemma, then two refutations, then conclusion.
The Arabic uses concise logical terms; don't get bogged down in vocabulary.
2
Identify the trilemma
Find the three possibilities: the director is necessary, or contingent leading to circle, infinite regress, or termination.
فإما أن يدور أو يتسلسل أو ينتهى إلى واجب الوجود
3
Understand the refutation of circularity
Razi argues that if A depends on B and B depends on A, each would be prior to the other, leading to self-priority, which is impossible.
This is a logical contradiction, not merely undesirable.
4
Understand the refutation of infinite regress
The sum of an infinite chain of contingents is itself contingent, so it needs a cause. That cause cannot be the chain itself or any member, so it must be external and necessary.
The argument does not assume the infinite series is actual; it works with any chain.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Razi concludes that all contingent things end in a Necessary Existent, who is eternal, pre-eternal, and everlasting.
This is the foundational proof for God's existence in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : مدير العالم إن كان واجب الوجود فهو المطلوب وإن كان جائز الوجود افتقر إلى مؤثر آخر فإما أن يدور أو يتسلسل أو ينتهى إلى واجب الوجود وهو المطلوب . أما بطلان الدور فلان الشيء إذا احتاج إلى غيره كان المحتاج إليه متقدما في الوجود على المحتاج ، فلو افتقر كل واحد منهما إلى الآخر لمكان كل واحد منهما متقدما في الوجود على الآخر فيلزم أن يكون كل واحد منهما متقدما على المتقدم على نفسه ، ومتقدم المنقدم متقدم ، فالشيء متقدم على نفسه هذا خلف . وأما بطلان التسلسل فلان مجموع تلك الأمور التي لا نهاية لها مفتقر إلى كل واحد منها وكل واحد منها ممكن والمفتقر إلى الممكن ممكن فالمجموع ممكن وكل ممكن فله مؤثر فالمجموع له مؤثر والمؤثر إما نفس ذلك المجموع أو أمر داخل فيه أو أمر خارج عنه، الأول باطل لأن المؤثر متقدم على الأثر فلو كان المجموع مؤثرا في نفسه يازم كونه متقدما على نفسه وهو محال ، والثانى باطل لأن كل واحد من آحاد ذلك المجموع فإنه لا يكون علة لنفسه ولا لعلته وإلا لزم تقدم الشيء على نفسه ، و إذا لم يكن علة لنفسه ولا لعلته لم يكن علة لذلك المجموع ، فثبت أنه لا بد لذلك المجموع من علة خارجة عنه والخارج عن جميع الممكنات لا يكون ممكنا بل يكون واجبا ، فثبت وجوب انتهاء الممكنات بأسرها إلى الواجب ومتى ثبت كونه واجبا لذاته ثبت أنه أزلي قديم باق أبدي .
A Question: The director of the world—if it is necessary of existence, then it is the sought; if it is contingent of existence, it depends on another cause. Then inevitably a circle or an infinite regress [results], or it ends in a Necessary Existent, which is the sought. As for the impossibility of the circle: because when a thing needs another, the needed is prior in existence to the needy. If each of them depended on the other, each would be prior in existence to the other, entailing that each is prior to the one prior to itself, and what is prior to the prior is prior, so a thing would be prior to itself—a contradiction. As for the impossibility of infinite regress: because the sum of those infinite things depends on each of them, and each is contingent, and that which depends on the contingent is contingent, so the sum is contingent. Every contingent has a cause. So the sum has a cause; the cause is either that same sum, or something internal to it, or something external. The first is false because the cause is prior to the effect; if the sum were a cause of itself, it would be prior to itself, which is impossible. The second is false because each individual of that sum is not a cause of itself nor of its cause—otherwise a thing would be prior to itself—and if it is not a cause of itself nor of its cause, it is not a cause of the sum. So it is established that the sum must have a cause external to it, and what is external to all contingent things cannot be contingent but must be necessary. So it is established that all contingent things must end in the Necessary. And when it is established that He is necessary in Himself, it is established that He is eternal, pre-eternal, everlasting.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage is a formal proof (mas'ala) that the director of the world must be a Necessary Existent. Razi presents a trilemma: the director is either necessary (desired conclusion) or contingent. If contingent, it needs a cause, leading to a circle, an infinite regress, or an ending in a Necessity. He then refutes the circle: if A depends on B and B depends on A, each would be prior to the other, leading to a thing being prior to itself, which is absurd. He refutes infinite regress: the entire infinite series is contingent because each member is contingent. It therefore needs a cause. That cause cannot be the series itself (cause prior to effect) nor any member within it (since no member is self-caused). So the cause must be external to the series, hence a Necessary Existent. This Necessary Existent is then proven eternal and everlasting. The argument is a classic formulation of the impossibility of infinite regress and circular causation.

Key Terms
مدير العالم
واجب الوجود
جائز الوجود
دور
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The infinite regress argument assumes the infinite series is actually existing.
Correction: The argument works even with a potential infinite because each member is contingent and the sum's contingency does not depend on the series being actual; it depends on the dependence of each member.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi argue that the sum of an infinite regress cannot cause itself?
A) Because the sum is not a real thing.
B) Because the cause must be prior to the effect, so the sum would be prior to itself.
C) Because each individual is contingent, so the sum is necessary.
D) Because the sum is external to itself.

Key Takeaway
Razi's proof shows that any contingent chain must terminate in a Necessary Existent, who is the eternal director of the world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the director of the world were contingent, what would that imply about the ultimate foundation of your own existence? Reflect on the fact that every contingent thing needs a cause, and this chain must end in a Necessary Being. How does this change how you view the fragility of your own life?
?
Razi argues that an infinite regress of causes is impossible. Consider the things you rely on for your daily existence—your job, health, relationships. If each of these depended on an infinite chain of contingencies, would you feel secure? How does the certainty of a Necessary First Cause give you a solid foundation?
?
The passage ends by stating that the Necessary Existent is eternal and everlasting. How does the fact that your Creator is not contingent, not dependent on anything, affect your relationship with Him? Are you treating Him as if He needs your worship, or are you aware that He is the self-sufficient Origin of all?

Objections to the Contingency Argument and Razi's Reply
Razi presents and answers objections about contingency, need for cause, and circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key objections raised against the contingency argument for a Director of the world and how Razi refutes them by distinguishing types of causal priority.

Core Idea
Objections to kalam arguments often hinge on mistaking types of priority.

In this passage, Razi presents and answers three objections to the proof that the world has a Director (a necessary being). The objections challenge the need for a cause, the role of contingency versus origination, and the possibility of causal circularity. Razi's responses, though partially cut off, reveal a sophisticated method of distinguishing temporal priority from essential priority—a key tool in kalam dialectics.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the translation. Let the objections and responses stand out.
The passage is dense and contains three separate objections.
2
Identify the three objections
Find each objection. The first suggests suitability dispenses with cause. The second says need is due to origination, not contingency. The third challenges the refutation of circularity.
The first two objections are presented but their full answers are not given in this excerpt.
3
Focus on the third objection and Razi's answer
Razi asks what 'priority' means: time, essence, or something else. He shows that temporal priority is false because cause and effect are simultaneous; essential priority also cannot allow self-priority.
This distinction is crucial: it cuts through the circle fallacy.
4
Reflect on the method
Notice how Razi uses precise definitions to dissolve objections. This is typical of kalam reasoning: clarity on terms is half the answer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يقال مدير العالم ممكن الوجود لكن الوجود به أولى فالأجل هذه الأولوية يستغني عن المؤثر . سلمنا أن الوجوب بالنسبة إليه كالعدم لكن لم قلت إنه يفتقر إلى السبب بيانه : أن علة الحاجة إلى المؤثر هو الحدوث لا الإمكان، فإذا كان ذلك المؤثر قديما لم يحتج إلى المؤثر . سلمنا أنه لابد من سبب فلم قلت أن الدور باطل . قوله : ولأن العلة قبل المعلول فيلزم أن يكون كل واحد منهما قبل نفسه قلنا : تدعى القبلية بالزمان أو بالذات أو بمعنى آخر فإن عنيت به الأول فهو باطل لأنه لا معنى لكون الشيء مؤثرا في الغير إلا صدور الأثر عنه على ما تقدم ، فقبل صدور الأثر عنه يستحيل أن يكون مؤثرا وإذا كان كذلك استحال تقدم العلة على المعلول بالزمان ، وإن عنيت به التقدم بالذات فنقول تعنى بالقدم بالذات .
It was said: Why may it not be said that the director of the world is contingent in existence but existence is more appropriate to it, and due to this appropriateness it dispenses with a cause? We grant that [for it] necessity is like nonexistence, but why do you say it needs a cause? Explanation: the cause of the need for an agent is origination, not contingency; so if that agent is pre-eternal, it does not need an agent. We grant that a cause is necessary; so why do you say the circle is impossible? His statement: 'And because the cause is before the effect, so each would be before itself.' We say: do you claim priority in time, or in essence, or in another sense? If you mean the first, it is false, because there is no meaning to something's being a cause of another except the effect's issuing from it as previously; so before the effect issues from it, it is impossible for it to be a cause; and if so, then the priority of the cause over the effect in time is impossible. If you mean priority in essence, we say: you mean priority in essence?
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage contains objections and answers to the proof of the Director of the World. The first objection claims that a contingent being might be so suited to existence that it needs no cause. Razi's response is cut off in the Arabic but implies that if existence is more appropriate, it still does not remove contingency. The second objection argues that the need for a cause is due to origination (temporal beginning), not contingency; so if the director is pre-eternal, it needs no cause. Razi's answer is implied but not fully given. The third objection challenges the refutation of the circle: why is the circle impossible if causality only implies essential priority? Razi responds by distinguishing priority in time from priority in essence. If priority is temporal, cause and effect cannot be simultaneous, so circle is impossible. If priority is essential, the argument still works because something cannot be essentially prior to itself. The passage ends mid-sentence, likely continuing in the original text. This lesson shows the dialectical nature of kalam: objections are raised and answered in a structured debate.

Key Terms
أولوية
حدوث
إمكان
قبلية بالزمان
قبلية بالذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if causality involves only essential priority, then circular causality (A causes B, B causes A) is logically possible.
Correction: Razi shows that even if priority is essential, something cannot be essentially prior to itself; thus a circle remains impossible.

Checkpoint

B) He argues that origination itself requires a cause.
C) He concedes the point and modifies the argument.
D) He states that contingency alone necessitates a cause regardless of origination.

Key Takeaway
Razi's refutation of the circle objection shows that clarifying the meaning of terms like 'priority' is essential to avoid false paradoxes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Proof that the Totality of Possibles Requires an External Necessary Cause
Imam Razi proves that the totality of possible beings requires an external necessary cause

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's proof that the totality of possible beings cannot be self-causing or caused by its own parts, and therefore requires an external necessary cause, which is then established as eternal.

Core Idea
The collection of possibles cannot cause itself, so it needs a necessary cause outside it.

Imam Razi presents a concise logical proof for the existence of a necessary being. He considers the entire collection of possible things and argues that it cannot be its own cause, nor can it be caused by any of its individual members. This forces the conclusion that there must be a cause external to the whole realm of possibility, which itself must be necessary and eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Get a sense of the flow of the argument.
Notice the concise style and the two false options he refutes.
2
Identify the two false options
Razi presents two possibilities: either the collection is its own cause, or it has an external cause. He refutes the first by the principle that a cause must precede its effect, and the second by showing no individual can cause the whole.
3
Understand the refutation of self-causation
If the collection caused itself, it would have to precede itself, which is impossible. This establishes that the collection cannot be self-caused.
This is a straightforward logical contradiction.
4
Understand why individuals cannot cause the whole
Each individual possible is not a cause for itself (else it would precede itself) nor for its own cause (circular). Therefore, it cannot be a cause for the entire collection.
The argument assumes that a cause must be external to its effect.
5
Conclude the necessity of an external cause and its attributes
Since the collection needs a cause outside it, and nothing outside all possibles can itself be possible, that cause must be necessary. From necessity in essence, it follows that it is eternal, permanent, and everlasting.
These attributes are derived directly from the necessity of existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو أمر خارج عنه، الأول باطل لأن المؤثر متقدم على الأثر فلو كان المجموع مؤثرا في نفسه يازم كرنه منقدما على نفسه وهو محال ، والثانى باطل لأن كل واحد من آحاد ذلك المجموع فإنه لا يكون علة لنفسه ولا لعلته وإلا لزم تقدم الشيء على نفـه ، و إذا لم يكن علة لنفسه ولا لانه لم يكن علة لـ لك المجموع ، فثبت أنه . لا بد لذلك المجموع من علة خارجة عنه والخارج عن جميع الممكنات لا يكون ممكنا بل يكون واجبا ، فثبت وجوب انتهاء الممكنات بأسرها إلى الواجب ومتى ثبت كونه واجبا لذاته ثبت أنه أزلي قديم باق أبدى
Or an external thing, the first is false because the efficient cause precedes the effect, so if the collection were efficient in itself, it would necessarily be preceding itself, which is impossible. The second is false because every single individual of that collection is not a cause for itself nor for its cause, otherwise a thing’s precedence over itself would follow. And when it is not a cause for itself nor for its cause, it is not a cause for that entire collection. So it is established that that collection must have a cause external to it, and what is external to all possible things cannot be possible but must be necessary. So it is established that all possible things must terminate in the Necessary Being, and when it is established that He is necessary in Himself, it is established that He is eternal, pre-eternal, permanent, everlasting.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is a proof by Imam Razi for the existence of a necessary being. It begins by considering a collection of possible things and argues that this collection cannot be self-causing (first option) because a cause must precede its effect, so self-causation would require the collection to precede itself—absurd. Nor can the collection be caused by its own individual members (second option), because each individual possible thing is not a cause for itself (otherwise it would precede itself) nor for its cause (circular). Therefore, no individual can be the cause of the entire collection. Hence the collection needs a cause outside it. Whatever is outside the whole realm of possible things cannot itself be possible, because if it were possible it would belong to the collection. So it must be necessary. This proves that all possible beings ultimately depend on a necessary being. Once it is established that this being is necessary in itself, it follows that it is eternal (azali), pre-eternal (qadim), permanent (baqi), and everlasting (abadi).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the collection of possibles could be caused by one of its members, such as a first possible being.
Correction: No, because each individual possible is not a cause for itself nor for its cause, so it cannot be a cause for the entire collection. The cause must be external to all possibles.

Key Takeaway

If it's the latter, why do you often act as if you control your own destiny? Ask yourself: when was the last time you paused to acknowledge your complete contingency before Allah?

The Dilemma of Existence and Quiddity
Objector argues existence of necessary being is problematic

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational dilemma an objector poses against the necessary being: either its existence is accidental to its quiddity, requiring a cause, or identical, reducing it to a mere description.

Core Idea
Is the necessary being's existence essential or accidental? Both options seem to fail.

This lesson presents an objection from an opponent who grants the existence of a necessary being but argues that its existence leads to a contradiction. The objection sets up a dilemma: existence must either be the same for all beings or different; if the same, then for the necessary being, existence is either accidental or identical to its essence. Both paths are argued to be impossible. Understanding this objection sharpens our grasp of the necessary being's uniqueness.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the structure of the dilemma: two main options, then a sub-dilemma.
The passage is an opponent's argument, not the author's final position.
2
Identify the two main options
The objector says existence of the necessary being either equals existence of possibles or not. Locate these two options in the text.
The second option is dismissed quickly because existence is a single concept.
3
Examine the sub-dilemma
The first option (existence equals) leads to a further question: Is existence accidental to the quiddity of the necessary being or not? Trace the consequences of each.
4
Consider the consequences
If accidental, then existence is possible and needs a cause—either the quiddity (absurd) or something else (contradicts necessity). If not accidental, then reality is pure existence (a description). Understand why both are problematic for the opponent.
The text cuts off here; later refutation is not included.
5
Reflect on the purpose
This is an objection that Razi presents; it does not represent his own view. Ask why such an objection is useful for refining theological arguments.
Common misunderstanding: this might seem to disprove the necessary being, but Razi likely responds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلمنا صحة د ايلكم على وجود واجب الوجود لكنه معارض بوجهين آخرين الأول أنا لو فرضنا موجودا واجب الوجود الكان وجوده إما أن يكون مساويا لوجود الممكنات وإما أن لا يكون ، والقسم الثاني باطل لما تقدم من الدلائل على كون الوجود مفهوما واحداً والأول أيضاً باطل ، لأن ذلك الوجود إما أن يكون عارضا لماهيته أو لا يكون فإن كان الأول كان ذلك الوجود ممكنا وله عله فالعلة إن كانت تلك المساهية كان المعدوم علة للوجود وهو محال ، وإن كان غيرها كان واحب الوجود مفتقرا في وجوده إلى سبب منفصل هذا خلف ، وإن لم يكن ذلك الوجود عارضا الماهيته وهو محال لأنه على هذا التقدير يكون تمام حقيقته مساويا للوجود الذي هو وصف
We grant the validity of your proof for the existence of a necessary existent, but it is opposed by two other aspects. The first: If we suppose an existent that is necessary of existence, its existence is either equal to the existence of possibles or not. The second is invalid due to the previously mentioned proofs that existence is a single concept. The first is also invalid, because that existence is either accidental to its quiddity or not. If it is the first, then that existence is possible and has a cause. So if the cause is that same quiddity, then non-existence would be a cause for existence—impossible. And if it is other than it, then the necessary existent would be dependent in its existence on a separate cause—this is a contradiction. And if that existence is not accidental to its quiddity, that is impossible, because on this estimation its complete reality would be equal to existence which is a description...
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against the concept of a necessary being. The objector grants the proof for a necessary existent but raises two difficulties; only the first is given here (the page cuts off). The objection argues that existence either applies equally (literally: equally) to possibles and the necessary being, or it does not. If not, then existence has different meanings, but the author claims it is a single concept. If it does apply equally, then existence is either accidental to the quiddity of the necessary being or identical with it. If accidental, then the necessary being's existence is possible (since it is not essential) and needs a cause; that cause would have to be either its own quiddity (which is impossible, as non-existence would cause existence) or something else, making the necessary being dependent on another—contradicting its necessity. If existence is not accidental but identical, then its complete reality is existence itself, which is a description (and thus it would be a mere concept without a quiddity). The argument is incomplete in the text, but it aims to show that the concept of a necessary being leads to a dilemma.

Key Terms
واجب الوجود
ممكن
وجود
ماهية
عارض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This objection refutes the existence of a necessary being.
Correction: The objection is an opponent's argument; Razi presents it to refute it later. The objection itself assumes the existence of a necessary being and tries to show it leads to inconsistency, but the defender will have a response.

Checkpoint
What does the objector conclude about the option that the necessary being's existence is accidental to its quiddity?
A) It leads to an infinite regress of causes
B) It leads to a contradiction because then existence would be possible and require a cause
C) It is the correct view and proves the necessary being
D) It is impossible because existence is not a concept

Key Takeaway
The objection attempts to trap the necessary being in a dilemma: its existence is either accidental to its essence (requiring a cause) or identical (making it a mere description)—both presented as impossible, but the objection awaits refutation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that some thinkers argue the necessary being's existence is a mere description, does that disturb your certainty in a personal, self-existent Creator? How will you respond to such intellectual challenges to your faith?
?
The objector claims that if existence is accidental to quiddity, the necessary being would depend on a cause. Have you ever examined whether your own faith rests on inherited doctrine or on a reasoned conviction that Allah is self-sufficient? What will you do to strengthen your understanding?
?
This dilemma attempts to reduce the concept of the necessary being to an impossibility. If you find such objections troubling, are you willing to delve deeper into Islamic theology to resolve them, or will you avoid them? What is your personal responsibility when your beliefs are questioned?

First Objection: Existence as Accident of Essence
First objection: existence as accident of essence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection against the Necessary Existent based on the relationship between existence and essence.

Core Idea
If existence is an accident of essence, the Necessary Existent becomes contingent.

Imam Razi presents a dialectical objection that challenges the proof for the Necessary Existent. The objection examines whether existence is identical to essence or an accident. This lesson walks through the logic that leads to a dilemma.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the dialectical structure: the author concedes the proof then presents objections.
2
Identify the two divisions
The passage first divides the existence of the Necessary Existent into being equal to possibles or not. Then under the equal case, it divides into existence being an accident or not. Map this out.
3
Analyze the accident scenario
If existence is an accident of essence, the essence (non-existent) would cause existence, or an external cause would be needed—both problematic.
4
Analyze the non-accident scenario
If existence is not an accident, the essence becomes identical to existence, which is accidental in our case, so the Necessary Existent would resemble a possible.
5
Conclude the objection
Both branches lead to impossibility or contingency, thus the assumption of a Necessary Existent is problematic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلمنا صحة دليلكم على وجود واجب الوجود لكنه معارض بوجهين آخرين الأول أنا لو فرضنا موجودا واجب الوجود لكان وجوده إما أن يكون مساويا لوجود الممكنات وإما أن لا يكون ، والقسم الثاني باطل لما تقدم من الدلائل على كون الوجود مفهوما واحدا والأول أيضا باطل ، لأن ذلك الوجود إما أن يكون عارضا لماهيته أو لا يكون فإن كان الأول كان ذلك الوجود ممكنا وله علة فالعلة إن كانت تلك الماهية كان المعدوم علة للوجود وهو محال ، وإن كان غيرها كان واجب الوجود مفتقرا في وجوده إلى سبب منفصل هذا خلف ، وإن لم يكن ذلك الوجود عارضا لماهيته وهو محال لأنه على هذا التقدير يكون تمام حقيقته مساويا للوجود الذي هو وصف عارض للماهياتنا ، وكل ما صح على الشيء صح على مثله ، فيلزم أن يصح على ماهيته كل ما يصح على وجودنا فيكون ممكنا وجوده . الثاني : أنه لو كان واجب الوجود لكان قديما والمعقول من القديم هو الذي لا زمان يفرض موجودا فيه إلا وقد كان موجودا قبل ذلك القبلية قبلية زمانية على ما تقدم، بيانه في باب القديم والمحدث فيلزم من قدم الله تعالى قدم الزمان وذلك محال . لا يقال تقدم البارى تعالى على العالم بزمان مقدر لا بزمان محقق ، وتفسيره أن الله تعالى تقدم على العالم بما لو كان هناك زمان لما كان لذلك الزمان أول لأنا نقول تقدم البارى تعالى على العالم إذا كان حاصلا في نفس الأمر محققا وذلك التقدم لا يتحقق إلا بواسطة الزمان استحال كون الزمان مقدرا بل لا بد وأن يكون محققا .
We grant the validity of your proof for the existence of the Necessary Existent, but it is opposed by two other aspects: First: If we assume an existent that is necessary of existence, then its existence is either equal to the existence of possibles or not. The second division is false because of the proofs that existence is a single concept. The first is also false, because that existence is either an accident of its essence or not. If the first, then that existence is possible and has a cause. If that cause is the essence itself, then non-existence would be a cause of existence – impossible. If it is other than it, then the Necessary Existent would depend upon a separate cause – this is a contradiction. And if that existence is not an accident of its essence – which is impossible – because on this assumption the whole of its reality would be equal to existence, which is a description accidental to our essences. And whatever is true of a thing is true of its like, so it follows that everything true of our existence would be true of its essence, and thus its existence would be possible. Second: If there were a Necessary Existent, it would be eternal. The intelligible from the eternal is that which is such that there is no time in which it is supposed to exist except that it existed before that priority – a temporal priority, as previously explained. Its explanation is in the chapter on the eternal and the originated. So from the eternality of God follows the eternality of time – and that is impossible. It cannot be said: The priority of the Creator over the world is by an estimated time, not an actual time; its interpretation is that God is prior to the world in such a way that if there were time, that time would have no beginning. For we say: The priority of the Creator over the world, if it is actual in re, is actual, and that priority cannot be realized except through time; it is impossible for time to be merely estimated – it must be actual.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage presents two objections against the existence of the Necessary Existent (wājib al-wujūd), conceding the opponent's proof for argument's sake. The first objection concerns the relationship between essence (māhiyya) and existence (wujūd). If existence is an accident of the essence, then the essence would be a cause of existence (absurd) or depend on another cause (making it contingent). If existence is not an accident, then the essence equals existence, making it like a contingent's existence, implying contingency. The second objection argues that a Necessary Existent must be eternal (qadīm), and the eternal implies temporal priority and hence the existence of time, but if time is eternal, that is impossible. The text anticipates and rejects a counter-argument that God's priority is merely estimated (muqaddar) time, not actual; the response insists that actual priority requires actual time.

Key Terms
واجب الوجود
وجود
ماهية
عارض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The first objection represents the author's own view about the Necessary Existent.
Correction: It is a dialectical challenge presented to test the opponent's proof.

Checkpoint
What is the core problem with the claim that existence is an accident of the essence in the first objection?
A) It makes existence and essence identical.
B) It leads to either essence causing existence or dependence on an external cause.
C) It implies the essence is eternal.
D) It requires the essence to be possible.

Key Takeaway
The first objection forces the opponent to either make the Necessary Existent dependent on a cause or reduce it to a possible being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter an argument that seems to challenge your belief in God's necessity, do you have the intellectual tools to examine its assumptions? This passage dissects one such challenge. Where in your life have you accepted a surface-level objection without tracing its logical branches?
?
The objection here hinges on whether existence is an accident of essence. Have you ever treated God's attributes as mere additions to His essence, as if He could be defined without them? How does this lesson correct your understanding of divine simplicity?
?
If you were to defend the necessity of God's existence to a skeptic, would your reasoning hold up against this objection? What step in this argument would you need to master to respond confidently? Commit to studying the author's response in the next lesson.

Second Objection: Eternality and Temporal Priority
Second objection: eternality entails temporal priority and eternal time

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection linking the Necessary Existent's eternality to the eternity of time and the response.

Core Idea
An eternal Necessary Existent would imply eternal time, which is impossible.

The second objection argues that a Necessary Existent must be eternal, and that eternality is understood in terms of temporal priority, leading to the conclusion that time itself would be eternal. This lesson examines the objection and the counterargument about estimated versus actual time.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the second objection and the response. Focus on the definition of eternal and the implication.
2
Understand the definition of eternal
The eternal is that which has temporal priority over any time: before any time, it already exists.
3
Follow the implication
If God is eternal, then for any time, God existed before it, meaning time is preceded by more time, so time would be eternal.
4
Examine the counterargument
The counterargument claims God's priority is by estimated time, not actual time. But the author insists that actual priority requires actual time.
5
Understand the response
The objection stands: if priority is real, time must be real; thus eternal time follows, which is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Its explanation is in the chapter on the eternal and the originated. So from the eternality of God follows the eternality of time – and that is impossible. It cannot be said: The priority of the Creator over the world is by an estimated time, not an actual time; its interpretation is that God is prior to the world in such a way that if there were time, that time would have no beginning. Objection: Why Not a Possible Being?
First objection and brief referral.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the mere possibility of existence does not make a being necessary without a determining cause.

Core Idea
Possibility alone cannot explain actual existence—a determining factor is required.

This lesson examines the first objection: why can't the world's director be merely possible, with existence being 'more appropriate'? The response simply refers to an earlier proof, establishing that possibility is insufficient.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objector suggests that a being could be possible (jā'iz al-wujūd) yet existence is simply more fitting for it, implying no need for a Necessary Existent.
2
Understand the response
The responder says 'It has already preceded'—meaning the proof that a possible being needs a determinant has already been given earlier.
This teaches us that earlier arguments are foundational.
3
Reflect on the principle
Think: Does 'possible' mean equal possibility of existence and non-existence? If so, why would existence be 'more appropriate' without a cause?
This exposes the flaw in the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب : قوله لم لا يجوز أن مدير العالم جائز الوجود لكن الوجود به أولى . قلنا : قد تقدم . قوله : هب أنه جائز الوجود على النساوى لكن إنما يحتاج إلى المؤثر لو كان محدثا . قلنا : بينا أن علة الحاجة هي الإمكان فقط . قوله : ما الذي عنيت بتقدم العلة على المعلول . قلنا : العقل مالم يفرض المؤثر وجودا استحال أن يحكم عليه بكونه مؤثرا في الغير ومرادنا من التقدم هذا القدر . قوله : لا يمكن وصفه بكونه كلا ومجموعا إلا إذا ثبت كونه متناهيا . قلنا : مرادنا من الكل والمجموع تلك الأسباب والمسببات بحيث لا يبقى واحد منها خارجا عنها . قوله : المؤثر في حدوث الحوادث اليومية إما القديم أو المحدث . قلنا : قد بينا أن المؤثر هو الصانع القديم المختار ، وأن المختار يصح منه ترجيح أحد الجائزين على الآخر لا مرجح . قوله : واجب الوجود إما أن يكون وجوده عين ماهيته أو غيرها . قلنا : بل عين ماهيته وقد تقدم الجواب عن أدلتهم على أن الوجود مشترك فيه . قوله : يلزم من قدم الله تعالى قدم الزمان . قلنا : إذا جاز أن يكون تقدم بعض أجزاء الزمان على البعض لا بالزمان فلم لا يجوز تقدم ذات الله تعالى على العالم لا بالزمان .
The answer: He said: Why is it not permissible that the director of the world is possible of existence, but existence is more appropriate for him? We said: It has already preceded. He said: Grant that he is possible of existence equally, but he only needs an effect if he is originated. We said: We have shown that the cause of need is only possibility. He said: What do you mean by the priority of the cause over the effect? We said: As long as the intellect does not suppose the effect exists, it is impossible for it to judge it as being an effect in another; and by priority we mean this much. He said: It cannot be described as being each and total unless its being finite is established. We said: We mean by each and total those causes and effects in such a way that not one of them remains outside them. He said: The effect in the origination of daily events is either the eternal or the originated. We said: We have shown that the effect is the eternal, free-choosing Maker, and that the free-chooser can rightly preponderate one of the two possibles over the other without a preponderator. He said: The Necessary Existent is either such that its existence is identical to its essence or other than it. We said: Rather it is identical to its essence, and the answer to their proofs that existence is common has already preceded. He said: From the eternality of God follows the eternality of time. We said: If it is permissible that the priority of some parts of time over others is not by time, then why is it not permissible that the priority of the essence of God over the world is not by time?
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage is a series of short objections and responses. The first objection asks why the world's director could not be merely possible (jā'iz al-wujūd) with existence being more appropriate (awlā) to him. The answer says it has been discussed earlier. The second objection says that even if he is possible equally (on par with non-existence), he only needs a cause if originated. The response reiterates that need is due to possibility (imkān) alone. The third objection questions the meaning of priority (tqaddum) of cause over effect. The response clarifies that priority is only the mind's inability to judge causality without assuming the effect's existence, i.e., conceptual priority. The fourth objection concerns describing a set as a total (kull) and sum (majmū') only if finite. The response explains that 'total' here means the collection of all causes and effects with none excluded. The fifth objection states the cause of daily events is either eternal or originated. The response reaffirms the eternal, free-choosing Maker who can prefer one possibility over another without an external preponderator. The sixth objection presents the dilemma: existence is either identical to essence or not. The response insists it is identical, referring to earlier refutation of the claim that existence is a common concept. The seventh objection argues that God's eternality implies the eternality of time. The response draws an analogy: just as some parts of time can be prior to others without temporal priority (i.e., conceptual or essential), so God's priority over the world can be non-temporal.

Key Terms
جائز الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a possible being can be actual without a cause if existence is 'more appropriate'.
Correction: Possibility implies equal potential for existence and non-existence; any preference requires a determining cause, as proven earlier.

Checkpoint
How does the responder reply to the objection that the world's director could be a possible being with existence more appropriate?
A) By providing a new proof.
B) By stating that the answer has already been given earlier.
C) By agreeing with the objector.
D) By denying the possibility of such a being.

Key Takeaway
A possible being cannot exist without a cause; earlier proofs are sufficient to refute this objection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own existence, do you truly see yourself as a 'possible being' who depends on Allah at every moment, or do you sometimes act as if you are self-sufficient? What would change if you lived with constant awareness of your need for a determinant?
?
The objector suggested that existence could be 'more appropriate' for a possible being without a cause. In your life, are there areas where you assume that because something seems appropriate or natural, it must automatically be right, without examining its true source?
?
The responder quickly referred back to an earlier proof. Are there foundational truths about Allah that you know but fail to apply when doubts arise? Commit now to revisiting the core proofs of Tawheed whenever confusion clouds your heart.
