Objection: Need Only for Originated Beings?
Second objection and correction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the need for a cause is based on possibility (imkān) alone, not on being originated (ḥudūth).

Core Idea
Possibility, not origination, is the ground for needing a cause.

The second objection argues that a possible being only needs an effective cause if it is originated (ḥādith). The responder corrects this by insisting that the sole reason for need is possibility (imkān).

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
The objector concedes the being is possible but says it only needs a cause if it is originated. The response says the cause of need is possibility only.
2
Distinguish between imkān and ḥudūth
Note that possibility is a rational quality inherent in the essence, while being originated is a temporal property. The responder grounds need in the former.
This is a key doctrinal point in kalām.
3
Consider the implication
If need were tied to origination, then an eternal possible being would not need a cause—but that is impossible because possibility entails contingency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب : قوله لم لا يجوز أن مدير العالم جائز الوجود لكن الوجود به أولى . قلنا : قد تقدم . قوله : هب أنه جائز الوجود على النساوى لكن إنما يحتاج إلى المؤثر لو كان محدثا . قلنا : بينا أن علة الحاجة هي الإمكان فقط . قوله : ما الذي عنيت بتقدم العلة على المعلول . قلنا : العقل مالم يفرض المؤثر وجودا استحال أن يحكم عليه بكونه مؤثرا في الغير ومرادنا من التقدم هذا القدر . قوله : لا يمكن وصفه بكونه كلا ومجموعا إلا إذا ثبت كونه متناهيا . قلنا : مرادنا من الكل والمجموع تلك الأسباب والمسببات بحيث لا يبقى واحد منها خارجا عنها . قوله : المؤثر في حدوث الحوادث اليومية إما القديم أو المحدث . قلنا : قد بينا أن المؤثر هو الصانع القديم المختار ، وأن المختار يصح منه ترجيح أحد الجائزين على الآخر لا مرجح . قوله : واجب الوجود إما أن يكون وجوده عين ماهيته أو غيرها . قلنا : بل عين ماهيته وقد تقدم الجواب عن أدلتهم على أن الوجود مشترك فيه . قوله : يلزم من قدم الله تعالى قدم الزمان . قلنا : إذا جاز أن يكون تقدم بعض أجزاء الزمان على البعض لا بالزمان فلم لا يجوز تقدم ذات الله تعالى على العالم لا بالزمان .
The answer: He said: Why is it not permissible that the director of the world is possible of existence, but existence is more appropriate for him? We said: It has already preceded. He said: Grant that he is possible of existence equally, but he only needs an effect if he is originated. We said: We have shown that the cause of need is only possibility. He said: What do you mean by the priority of the cause over the effect? We said: As long as the intellect does not suppose the effect exists, it is impossible for it to judge it as being an effect in another; and by priority we mean this much. He said: It cannot be described as being each and total unless its being finite is established. We said: We mean by each and total those causes and effects in such a way that not one of them remains outside them. He said: The effect in the origination of daily events is either the eternal or the originated. We said: We have shown that the effect is the eternal, free-choosing Maker, and that the free-chooser can rightly preponderate one of the two possibles over the other without a preponderator. He said: The Necessary Existent is either such that its existence is identical to its essence or other than it. We said: Rather it is identical to its essence, and the answer to their proofs that existence is common has already preceded. He said: From the eternality of God follows the eternality of time. We said: If it is permissible that the priority of some parts of time over others is not by time, then why is it not permissible that the priority of the essence of God over the world is not by time?
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage is a series of short objections and responses. The first objection asks why the world's director could not be merely possible (jā'iz al-wujūd) with existence being more appropriate (awlā) to him. The answer says it has been discussed earlier. The second objection says that even if he is possible equally (on par with non-existence), he only needs a cause if originated. The response reiterates that need is due to possibility (imkān) alone. The third objection questions the meaning of priority (tqaddum) of cause over effect. The response clarifies that priority is only the mind's inability to judge causality without assuming the effect's existence, i.e., conceptual priority. The fourth objection concerns describing a set as a total (kull) and sum (majmū') only if finite. The response explains that 'total' here means the collection of all causes and effects with none excluded. The fifth objection states the cause of daily events is either eternal or originated. The response reaffirms the eternal, free-choosing Maker who can prefer one possibility over another without an external preponderator. The sixth objection presents the dilemma: existence is either identical to essence or not. The response insists it is identical, referring to earlier refutation of the claim that existence is a common concept. The seventh objection argues that God's eternality implies the eternality of time. The response draws an analogy: just as some parts of time can be prior to others without temporal priority (i.e., conceptual or essential), so God's priority over the world can be non-temporal.

Key Terms
جائز الوجود
إمكان
مؤثر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That only originated things need a cause; eternal possible beings do not.
Correction: The need for a cause is due to the intrinsic possibility (contingency) of the being, irrespective of whether it is originated or not.

Checkpoint
According to the responder, what is the cause of a being's need for an effective cause?
A) Its being originated.
B) Its being possible.
C) Its being eternal.
D) Its being non-existent.

Key Takeaway
The need for a cause is due to the possibility (contingency) of a being, not its origination in time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you believe that your existence is simply a fact, have you ever contemplated that at every moment you are contingent and in need of the Sustainer? How does this realization change your sense of dependence on Allah in your daily life?
?
The objection suggests that only originated things need a cause. Do you sometimes act as if your current existence is self-sufficient, ignoring that your very possibility requires a cause every moment? What would change if you internalized that you are always in need?
?
This passage establishes that possibility alone necessitates a cause. How does this truth impact your understanding of tawheed – that Allah alone is the Necessary Being, and everything else is always dependent on Him? How can you live in a way that reflects this constant dependence?

Objection: Meaning of Priority (Taqaddum) of Cause
Third objection and clarification of priority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the priority of the cause over the effect is conceptual (mental), not temporal.

Core Idea
Cause's priority over effect is rational, not temporal.

The third objection questions what 'priority of the cause over the effect' means. The responder clarifies that it means the intellect cannot judge the cause as a cause without assuming the effect's existence—it is a conceptual priority.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objector asks for clarification on the term 'priority' (taqaddum) of the cause over the effect.
2
Study the response
The responder explains that the intellect cannot consider the cause as effective without mentally positing the effect. The priority meant is this mental precedence.
This avoids implying temporal priority, which would be problematic for an eternal cause.
3
Distinguish types of priority
In kalām, priority can be temporal, essential, or conceptual. Here it is conceptual—the cause is conceptually prior to the effect.
The effect is co-existent with the cause in the case of an eternal cause and effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

?
If the intellect cannot conceive of an effect without its cause, then your own actions and existence are impossible without Allah’s sustaining will. When you sinned today, did you act as if you were independent of Allah’s control? How will you remind yourself before your next action that your entire being is conceptually dependent on Him—and that this dependence demands gratitude and obedience?

Objection: Totality Requires Finitude?
Fourth objection and clarification on total/sum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a collection of causes and effects can be described as a 'total' without implying finitude.

Core Idea
Describing a set as 'total' does not require finitude if it means comprehensive inclusion.

The fourth objection argues that calling God a 'total' or 'sum' (kull, majmū') presupposes that the set of all things is finite. The responder clarifies that by 'total' they simply mean the comprehensive collection of all causes and effects, none excluded, without implying finitude.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objector says a thing can only be described as 'each' or 'total' if it is finite.
2
Understand the response
The responder says they mean by 'total' the entire collection of causes and effects such that none remain outside—this does not require finitude.
The universe as a whole is infinite but still can be referred to as a totality in the sense of completeness.
3
Contrast with logical use
In logic, 'kull' can refer to a universal concept, not necessarily a finite aggregate. Here it is used in a comprehensive sense.

Objection: Cause of Daily Events – Eternal or Originated?
Fifth objection and response on daily events' cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the cause of daily events is the eternal free-choosing Maker, who can preponderate one possible over another without an external preponderator.

Core Idea
The free-choosing Maker can cause events without requiring an additional cause for His choice.

The fifth objection presents a dilemma: the cause of daily events is either eternal or originated. The responder affirms it is the eternal free-choosing Maker, and explains that the free-chooser can preponderate one possible over another without a preponderator, grounding divine will as the sufficient reason.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objector says the cause of daily events must be either eternal or originated.
2
Analyze the response
The responder states the cause is the eternal Maker, who is free-choosing (mukhtār). He then notes that the free-chooser can prefer one possible over another without needing an external reason for the preference.
This addresses the problem of why God chooses to create at particular times.
3
Explore the concept of preponderance without a preponderator
In kalām, this is a key attribute of divine will: God's choice is not necessitated by any external factor; it is an exercise of free will.
This counters the objection that an eternal cause would produce its effect eternally.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Misconception: That an eternal cause must produce its effect eternally, so no new events can occur.
Correction: An eternal free agent can choose to create events in time without needing a change in His essence.

The eternal free-choosing Maker causes daily events by a free act of preference, requiring no external cause.

Objection: Essence and Existence Identity
Sixth objection and identity of existence and essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that for the Necessary Existent, existence is identical to essence, not an added attribute.

Core Idea
God's existence is His very essence—no distinction.

The sixth objection poses a dilemma: either existence is identical to essence or it is not. The responder affirms identity, referencing an earlier refutation of the idea that existence is a common concept predicated of both God and creatures.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objector offers a disjunction: the Necessary Existent's existence is either the same as its essence or different.
2
Examine the response
The responder says existence is 'identical to its essence' ('ayn māhiyatih) and refers to an earlier answer to arguments that existence is a common concept.
This means God has no composition of essence and existence.
3
Understand the implication
In contingent beings, existence is added to essence; in God, existence is not an accident but His very reality.
This is a core tenet of divine simplicity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب : قوله لم لا يجوز أن مدير العالم جائز الوجود لكن الوجود به أولى . قلنا : قد تقدم . قوله : هب أنه جائز الوجود على النساوى لكن إنما يحتاج إلى المؤثر لو كان محدثا . قلنا : بينا أن علة الحاجة هي الإمكان فقط . قوله : ما الذي عنيت بتقدم العلة على المعلول . قلنا : العقل مالم يفرض المؤثر وجودا استحال أن يحكم عليه بكونه مؤثرا في الغير ومرادنا من التقدم هذا القدر . قوله : لا يمكن وصفه بكونه كلا ومجموعا إلا إذا ثبت كونه متناهيا . قلنا : مرادنا من الكل والمجموع تلك الأسباب والمسببات بحيث لا يبقى واحد منها خارجا عنها . قوله : المؤثر في حدوث الحوادث اليومية إما القديم أو المحدث . قلنا : قد بينا أن المؤثر هو الصانع القديم المختار ، وأن المختار يصح منه ترجيح أحد الجائزين على الآخر لا مرجح . قوله : واجب الوجود إما أن يكون وجوده عين ماهيته أو غيرها . قلنا : بل عين ماهيته وقد تقدم الجواب عن أدلتهم على أن الوجود مشترك فيه . قوله : يلزم من قدم الله تعالى قدم الزمان . قلنا : إذا جاز أن يكون تقدم بعض أجزاء الزمان على البعض لا بالزمان فلم لا يجوز تقدم ذات الله تعالى على العالم لا بالزمان .
The answer: He said: Why is it not permissible that the director of the world is possible of existence, but existence is more appropriate for him? We said: It has already preceded. He said: Grant that he is possible of existence equally, but he only needs an effect if he is originated. We said: We have shown that the cause of need is only possibility. He said: What do you mean by the priority of the cause over the effect? We said: As long as the intellect does not suppose the effect exists, it is impossible for it to judge it as being an effect in another; and by priority we mean this much. He said: It cannot be described as being each and total unless its being finite is established. We said: We mean by each and total those causes and effects in such a way that not one of them remains outside them. He said: The effect in the origination of daily events is either the eternal or the originated. We said: We have shown that the effect is the eternal, free-choosing Maker, and that the free-chooser can rightly preponderate one of the two possibles over the other without a preponderator. He said: The Necessary Existent is either such that its existence is identical to its essence or other than it. We said: Rather it is identical to its essence, and the answer to their proofs that existence is common has already preceded. He said: From the eternality of God follows the eternality of time. We said: If it is permissible that the priority of some parts of time over others is not by time, then why is it not permissible that the priority of the essence of God over the world is not by time?
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage is a series of short objections and responses. The first objection asks why the world's director could not be merely possible (jā'iz al-wujūd) with existence being more appropriate (awlā) to him. The answer says it has been discussed earlier. The second objection says that even if he is possible equally (on par with non-existence), he only needs a cause if originated. The response reiterates that need is due to possibility (imkān) alone. The third objection questions the meaning of priority (tqaddum) of cause over effect. The response clarifies that priority is only the mind's inability to judge causality without assuming the effect's existence, i.e., conceptual priority. The fourth objection concerns describing a set as a total (kull) and sum (majmū') only if finite. The response explains that 'total' here means the collection of all causes and effects with none excluded. The fifth objection states the cause of daily events is either eternal or originated. The response reaffirms the eternal, free-choosing Maker who can prefer one possibility over another without an external preponderator. The sixth objection presents the dilemma: existence is either identical to essence or not. The response insists it is identical, referring to earlier refutation of the claim that existence is a common concept. The seventh objection argues that God's eternality implies the eternality of time. The response draws an analogy: just as some parts of time can be prior to others without temporal priority (i.e., conceptual or essential), so God's priority over the world can be non-temporal.

ماهية
عين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That existence is a common attribute added to essence in all beings, including God.
Correction: For the Necessary Existent, existence is not an added attribute but identical to essence.

Checkpoint
What does the responder say about the existence of the Necessary Existent?
A) It is other than its essence.
B) It is identical to its essence.
C) It is a common concept.
D) It is an accident.

Key Takeaway
The Necessary Existent's existence is not separate from its essence; they are one and the same.

?

Objection: Eternality of God Implies Eternality of Time
Seventh objection and analogy for non-temporal priority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how God's priority over the world can be non-temporal, by analogy with the priority of some parts of time over others, which is not itself temporal.

Core Idea
God's priority is essential, not temporal.

The seventh objection argues that if God is eternal, time must also be eternal, because causal priority implies temporal priority. The responder gives an analogy: some parts of time are prior to others but not by time (i.e., they are prior in a non-temporal sense), so God's priority over the world can also be non-temporal.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objector claims that from God's eternality (qidam) follows the eternality of time (zamān).
2
Follow the analogy
The responder points out that some parts of time are prior to other parts, but this priority is not itself temporal—they are prior in the sense of sequence, not measured by time. Similarly, God's priority over the world is not temporal but essential.
This shows that priority does not necessarily imply time.
3
Apply the principle
Thus, God can be ontologically prior without time existing alongside Him eternally.

Source Text

Proof of the Existence of the Maker of the World
Imam al-Razi proves the existence of the world's Maker against atheists.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Razi's logical proof for the Creator's existence and the main objections he addresses.

Core Idea
The non-existent is pure nothingness and cannot be a candidate for divinity.

Imam al-Razi begins his proof by stating that the Maker of the world must be existent because non-existence is a pure negation lacking any positive attribute. He anticipates objections: first, about an intermediate state (al-ḥāl); second, about distinctions among non-existents; and third, about the implications of existence being univocal. This lesson walks through each part of the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof statement
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to get the overall flow.
2
Understand the first objection (al-ḥāl)
Notice the objection that there might be an intermediate state between existence and non-existence. See how the author concedes the possibility but insists non-existence still lacks distinction.
3
Examine the distinction objection
The second objection claims that non-existents can have distinctions, as in the examples given. See how the author refutes by showing not all non-existences have the same effect.
This is a key move in the argument.
4
Consider the counter-objection about equality
The final objection argues that if God existed, He would be equal to others in existence, leading to absurdities. The author rejects it, implying existence is gradational.
This part is left for further discussion.
5
Review the argument structure
Map out the proof and the three objections. Understand how each objection is handled.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : صانع العالم موجود خلافا للملاحدة لعنهم الله تعالى . لنا : لو لم يكن موجودا لكان معدوما والمعدوم نفي محض لا خصوصية فيه ولا امتياز فلا يصلح للإلهية . فإن قيل : لا نسلم أنه لا واسطة . بيانه تقدم في مسألة الحال . سلمنا لكن لم قلت إنه لا يجوز أن يكون معدوما . قوله : لأن المعدوم لا امتياز فيه . قلنا : لا نسلم فإن عدم السواد عن المحل يصحح حلول البياض فيه ، وعدم الحركة لا يصحح وكذلك عدم اللازم يقتضي عدم الملزوم ، وعدم غيره لا يقتضي ذلك ، وعدم المعارض معتبر في دلالة المعجزة على الصدق ، وسائر العلامات ليس كذلك . سلمنا ما ذكرتموه لكنه معارض بأنه لو كان موجودا لكان مساويا لغيره في الوجود . ووجوب الوجود إن كان مساويا لزم أن يصح عليه ما يصح على الممكنات وهو محال . وإن كان على الوجود ماهية الوجود كان المعدوم علة الموجود وهو باطل .
Issue: The Maker of the world exists, contrary to the atheists, may God curse them. For us: If He were not existent, He would be non‑existent, and the non‑existent is pure negation, having no particularity and no distinction, so it is not suitable for divinity. For if it is said: We do not concede that there is no intermediate. Its explanation preceded in the issue of the state (al-ḥāl). We concede, but why do you say that it is not permissible for Him to be non‑existent? He said: Because the non‑existent has no distinction. We say: We do not concede, for the non‑existence of blackness in a substrate renders the occurrence of whiteness in it possible, but the non‑existence of motion does not do so; likewise, the non‑existence of the necessary entails the non‑existence of the necessitated, but the non‑existence of something else does not entail that; and the non‑existence of a counter‑evidence is considered in the miracle’s indication of truthfulness, but other signs are not so. We concede what you mentioned, but it is countered by the fact that if He were existent, He would be equal to others in existence. And if the necessity of existence is equivalent, then it would entail that what is valid for contingent things would be valid for Him, which is impossible. And if existence is the essence of existence, then the non‑existent would be the cause of the existent, which is false.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is a proof of the existence of the Maker of the world. The author begins by stating the thesis: the Maker exists, contrary to the atheists. The proof is: if He did not exist, He would be non‑existent, but the non‑existent is a pure nothingness with no particularity or distinction, and thus is unfit for divinity. An objection is raised: there may be an intermediate state (the state/ḥāl). The author concedes the existence of an intermediate but insists that the non‑existent cannot have distinction. Another objection: non‑existents can have distinctions, as the non‑existence of blackness enables whiteness, etc. The author refutes this by showing that not all non‑existences have the same effect. Finally, a counter‑objection: if God existed, He would be equal in existence to others, leading to impossible consequences. The argument then discusses whether existence is univocal or not. The author implies that existence is a ‘mushtakk’ (gradational) concept, not univocal, so the objection fails. The passage includes a complete objection-and-answer sequence, though some later objections are left for further discussion.

Key Terms
صانع العالم
معدوم
نفي محض
امتياز
إلهية
حال
وجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Someone might think the proof is circular or that non‑existence can have positive attributes.
Correction: The author clarifies that non‑existence is pure negation; any perceived 'distinction' arises from external relations, not from the non‑existent itself.

Checkpoint
What is the main premise in the proof that the Maker of the world exists?
A) The Maker must be eternal.
B) If the Maker did not exist, He would be non‑existent, and non‑existence is pure negation.
C) The Maker must be one.
D) The world needs a creator.

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Razi argues that non-existence cannot be a candidate for divinity because it is pure negation, and he addresses objections about intermediate states and distinctions among non-existents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Al-Razi refutes the objection that non-existents have different effects. The final objection claims that if God exists, He would be equal to all other existents—yet al-Razi points toward graded existence. The Maker Must Be Existent: Proof and Objection
Razi states the proof for the Maker's existence and answers an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's proof that the Maker must be existent and how he refutes the objection that non-existence can have distinctions.

Core Idea
Non-existence is pure negation, unworthy of divinity.

Razi begins by stating the issue that the Maker of the world exists. He provides a concise logical proof: if the Maker were non-existent, He would be pure negation without any positive reality, and thus unfit to be divine. He then anticipates an objection that non-existence can have specific effects, and answers by explaining that such effects are conditional.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof that existence is necessary for divinity.
Start with Razi's main argument: if the Maker were non-existent, He would be pure negation with no particularity or distinction, and therefore cannot be worthy of divinity.
2
Understand the objection that non-existence can have distinctions.
The objector brings examples where absence (non-existence) has different effects (e.g., absence of blackness vs. absence of motion). Reflect on how these examples challenge the claim that non-existence has no distinctions.
These examples are meant to show that non-existence is not always without specific effects.
3
Grasp Razi's reply using the concept of conditions.
Razi explains that such distinctions are conditional: the non-existence of a prior accident can be a condition for a later effect, but that does not give non-existence positive reality.
The key is that non-existents can be conditions, but not causes or subjects of divinity.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : صانع العالم موجود خلافا للملاحدة لعنهم الله تعالى . لنا لو لم يكن موجودا لكان معدوما والمعدوم نفى محض لا خصوصية فيه ولا امتياز فلا يصلح للإلهية . فإن قيل لا نسلم أنه لا واسطة بيانه تقدم في مسألة الحال ، سلمنا لكن لم قلت إنه لا يجوز أن يكون معدوما. قوله : لأن المعدوم لا امتياز فيه . قلنا : لا نسلم فإن عدم السواد عن المحل يصحح حلول البياض فيه ، وعدم الحركة لا يصحح وكذلك عدم اللازم يقتضى عدم الملزوم ، وعدم غيره لا يقتضى ذلك ، وعدم المعارض معتبر في دلالة المعجزة على الصدق ، وسائر العلامات ليس كذلك ، سلمنا ما ذكرتموه لكنه معارض بما أنه لو كان موجودا لكان مساويا لغيره في . وجوابه الصحيح أن يقال : عدم الحادث السابق شرط به يتم المؤثر فى الحادث اللاحق والعدميات يجوز أن تكون شروطا كما مر بيانه . وقوله في الجواب عن ذلك : أنا بينا أن المؤثر هو الصانع القديم المختار وأن المختار يصح منه ترجيح أحد الجائزين على الآخر لا لمرجح فيه نظر فإنه لم يبين إلى الآن كون المؤثر مختارا وإنما سيبينه فيما بعد بناء على حدوث العالم ، فإن في حدوث العالم على كونه مختارا لزم الدور، وأيضاً ادعاء أن المختار يصح منه ترجيح أحد الجائزين لا أرجح غير مسلم، فإن المختار هو الذى يكون فعله تبعا لإرادته وداعيه لا أن يكون الفعل واقعا منه اتفاقا والداعي يكفي في الترجيح . وقول القدماء : إن الجامع مختار أحد الفرضين المتساويين من غير ترجيح أحدهما على الآخر مردود فإن غاية كلامهم أن الترجيح فى أمثال ذلك غير معلوم وذلك لا يدل على أنه غير موجود فإن المتحير هو الذى لا يترجح أحد دواعيه على الباقية والتحير موجود قطعا في كثير من المختارين من أن البديهة حاكمة بأن الترجيح من غير مرجح محال .
Issue: The Maker of the world exists, contrary to the atheists, may God curse them. For us: if He were not existent, He would be non-existent, and the non-existent is pure negation with no particularity or distinction, so it is not fit for divinity. If it is said: we do not admit that there is no intermediate; its explanation preceded in the issue of state (al-hal). We grant [the denial of intermediate?] but why do you say that it is not permissible for Him to be non-existent? He said: because the non-existent has no distinction. We said: we do not admit that; for the absence of blackness from a substrate renders whiteness in it valid, absence of motion does not make it valid, and likewise the absence of the necessary entails the absence of that which is necessary by it, the absence of other than it does not entail that, and the absence of an impediment is considered in the indication of the miracle of truthfulness, while other signs are not like that. We grant what you mentioned, but it is opposed by the fact that if He were existent, He would be equal to other existing things in... The correct answer is to say: the absence of the prior accident is a condition by which the efficient cause is completed for the later accident, and non-existents can be conditions as its explanation has preceded. And his statement in answer to that: we have shown that the efficient cause is the ancient, free Creator, and that the free agent may validly give preponderance to one of two possibilities over the other without a preponderating reason has a flaw, for he has not yet shown the agent to be free; he will only show it later based on the temporality of the world; for in the temporality of the world and His being free there would be circularity. Also, the claim that the free agent may validly give preponderance to one of two possibilities without a preponderating reason is not admitted; for the free agent is the one whose action is consequent upon his will and motive, not that the action occurs from him by chance, and the motive suffices for preponderance. And the statement of the ancients: the one who makes a choice between two equal alternatives without preponderating one over the other is rejected, for the utmost of their speech is that preponderance in such matters is unknown, and that does not indicate that it is non-existent; for the perplexed one is the one whose motives are not preponderated over the rest, and perplexity certainly exists in many free agents, and intuitive reason judges that preponderance without a preponderating cause is impossible.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the existence of the Maker of the world, with objections and answers. It begins by stating the issue: the Maker exists, contrary to the atheists. The proof: if He were non-existent, He would be pure negation without any distinction, so He could not be worthy of divinity. Then an objection: non-existence can have distinctions (e.g., absence of blackness vs absence of motion have different effects). The answer: non-existents can be conditions, as explained earlier in the issue of state (al-hal). The objector then argues that if the Maker were existent, He would be equal to other existents. The correct answer is that the absence of a prior accident is a condition for the later effect, and non-existents can be conditions. Then Razi criticizes a previous answer that appealed to the Maker being a free agent who can choose without a preponderating reason: that answer is flawed because it assumes the Maker is free (which will only be proved later), and also because the claim that a free agent can choose without any preponderating reason is false; rather, a free agent acts according to will and motive, and the motive suffices for preponderance. Finally, he refutes the ancient argument that someone could choose between two equal alternatives without a preponderating cause: such a case is unknown, but that does not prove impossibility; perplexity exists in many free agents, and intuitive reason judges that preponderance without a preponderating cause is impossible.

Key Terms
صانع العالم
عدم
إلهية
واسطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Non-existence can have positive distinctions that make it a suitable candidate for divinity.
Correction: Razi argues that any distinctions attributed to non-existence are merely conditional (شروط) and do not confer real particularity or existence.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi say the Maker cannot be non-existent?
A) Because non-existence is eternal.
B) Because the non-existent is pure negation with no distinction and thus unworthy of divinity.
C) Because non-existence is impossible for God.
D) Because the Maker is known by intuition.

Key Takeaway
Non-existence is pure negation; only existence has the particularity required for divinity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi argues that if the Maker were non-existent, He would be pure negation with no distinction—unworthy of divinity. Have you ever thought of God as a mere concept or idea rather than a necessary existent? What would be lost if your faith rested on a non-existent deity?
?
The objector claims non-existence can have distinctions, like absence of blackness enabling whiteness. Do you treat non-existence in your own life as a positive force—perhaps by placing hope in things that do not exist yet, or by fearing what is absent? How does Razi's response that non-existence is only a condition challenge you to seek what truly exists and matters?
?
Razi's proof reduces atheism to an absurdity: the Maker cannot be non-existent because non-existence is pure negation. If you truly internalized this, how would it change your conversations with someone who doubts God's existence? Would you rely on this logical argument or on something else? What would it take for you to be as certain of God's existence as Razi is?

The Free Agent and the Problem of Choice Without Cause
Razi critiques the free agent argument and refutes choice without cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's critique of the claim that a free agent can choose without a preponderating reason, and his positive argument that choice requires a motive.

Core Idea
Free choice is always based on a motive, not on arbitrary preference without cause.

Razi examines a previous argument that the Maker is a free agent who can give preponderance without a preponderating reason. He finds this argument flawed, pointing out that it relies on a yet-unproven premise (that the Maker is free) and also misrepresents the nature of free will. He then refutes the ancient notion that one can choose between equals without a cause, arguing that such a case is unknown and that intuitive reason rejects it.

Guided Path
1
Read the flawed argument and Razi's critique of its circularity.
Identify the argument that the Maker is free and can choose without cause. Note Razi's criticism: this assumes freedom, which will only be proved later based on the world's temporality, leading to circularity.
2
Understand Razi's positive account of free agency.
Razi defines the free agent as one whose action follows from his will and motive (داعي), not from chance. This implies that preponderance is always accompanied by a motive.
3
Examine the refutation of the ancient claim of choice without cause.
Analyze Razi's response to the statement that one can choose between two equal alternatives without a preponderating reason. He argues that such a case is unknown and does not prove possibility, and that intuitive reason rejects it.
Razi also introduces the concept of perplexity (تحير) as an example of a state where motives are balanced, but that is not a case of arbitrary choice.

?

God's Existence Is Not Univocal with Creatures
Razi answers the first objection concerning univocal existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's existence is not the same as that of possible beings, and the distinction between univocal and analogical terms.

Core Idea
Existence is analogical, not univocal—God’s existence is unique.

The first objection argues that if God’s existence is like ours, He would share our limitations. Razi answers by showing that ‘existence’ is a term with gradations, not a single kind. This lesson unpacks that distinction and its implications for divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic and translation of the first objection and Razi’s reply. Notice how he identifies the key issue: the meaning of existence.
2
Understand univocal vs analogical
Razi introduces two types of terms: mutawāṭi'a (univocal) and mushakkaka (analogical). Reflect on examples: 'human' is univocal; 'healthy' is analogical. Existence is like 'healthy'.
This is a crucial distinction in Islamic theology.
3
Consider the quiddity argument
Razi refutes a subtler point: if quiddity and existence were identical, then the non-existent would have a quiddity. He says quiddity itself is neutral—neither existent nor non-existent.
This shows the complexity of the debate.
4
Apply to God
Ask: why does this answer protect God’s transcendence? Because it prevents drawing conclusions about God from creatures.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المعارضة الأولى لإثبات واجب الوجود بأن وجود واجب الوجود إن كان مساويا بالوجود الممكنات لزم أن يصح عليه ما يصح على الممكنات ليس بشيء ، فإن من فهم الفرق بين المعاني المتواطئة والمعانى المشككات عرف أن الوجود على الواجب وعلى غيره لا يقع بالتساوى، وإن كان المفهوم من الوجود شيئا واحدا وحينئذ لا يلزم منه أن يصح على الواجب ما يصح على الممكنات من غير أن يذهب إلى أن الوجود ليس بمشترك وقوله : إن كانت على الوجود ماهية الوجود كان المعدوم ماهية الموجود فباطل ، لأن الماهية وحدها لا تكون موجودة ولا معدومة وهذا هو عين مذهبه الذي ذكره في سائر المواضع وأبطاله ههنا وأما المعارضة الثانية بوجوب قدم الزمان وجوابه بأن تقدم البارى على العالم كتقدم بعض أجزاء الزمان على البعض الآخر فقد سبق ما يرد عليه، والحق أن البارى تعالى ليس بزمان والزمان من مبدعاته والوهم يقيس ما لا يكون في الزمان على ما فى الزمان كما مر فى المكان ، والعقل كما يأبى عن إطلاق التقدم المكاني على البارى كذلك يأبى عن إطلاق التقدم الزمانى عليه ، بل ينبغي أن يقال إن للبارى تعالى تقدما خارجا عن القسمين ، وإن كان الوهم خارجا عن توهمه .
As for the first objection against the proof of the Necessary Being: that the existence of the Necessary Being, if it were equal in existence to possible beings, would entail that what applies to possible beings applies to Him. This is nothing, for whoever understands the difference between univocal (mutawāṭi'a) and analogical (mushakkaka) terms knows that existence for the Necessary and for other than Him does not occur equally. Even if the concept of existence were one thing, then this does not entail that what applies to possible beings applies to the Necessary, without going so far as to say that existence is not common. And his statement: if the quiddity of existence were [the same as] existence, then the non-existent would be the quiddity of the existent is false, because the quiddity alone is neither existent nor non-existent, and this is exactly his doctrine which he mentioned in other places and refuted it here. As for the second objection concerning the necessity of the pre-eternity of time, and the answer: that the priority of the Creator over the world is like the priority of some parts of time over others, there has already preceded what refutes it. The truth is that the Creator, exalted, is not temporal, and time is among His creations. The imagination (al-wahm) compares what is not in time to what is in time, as preceded in the case of space. Intellect ('aql) just as it refuses to apply spatial priority to the Creator, so too it refuses to apply temporal priority to Him. Rather, it should be said that the Creator, exalted, has a priority outside both categories, even if the imagination is incapable of imagining it.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage presents two objections against the proof of the Necessary Being and their answers. The first objection: if the existence of the Necessary Being is univocally the same as that of possible beings, then He would be subject to the same accidents as possibles. Razi answers by distinguishing between univocal terms (المعاني المتواطئة) which apply equally to all instances, and analogical terms (المعاني المشككات) which apply in a graded manner. Existence is not univocal; it applies to the Necessary Being in a different way. Even if the concept of existence were one, that does not entail that all properties apply equally. He also refutes the claim that if quiddity were existence, then non-existence would be the quiddity of existence—that is false because quiddity itself is neither existent nor non-existent. The second objection: if the Creator is pre-eternal, then time must also be pre-eternal. The answer: the priority of the Creator is not temporal; time is created. The imagination wrongly compares God to temporal things. Intellect refuses to apply temporal priority to God; His priority is beyond both spatial and temporal categories.

Key Terms
واجب الوجود
متواطئة
مشككات
ماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God’s existence is the same kind as creatures’ existence.
Correction: Existence is analogical (mushakkak), not univocal (mutawāṭi'), so God’s existence is unique and necessary, while creatures’ is contingent.

Checkpoint
How does Razi answer the objection that if God's existence is the same as that of creatures, then He must share their properties?
A) By saying existence is not a real attribute.
B) By distinguishing between univocal and analogical terms.
C) By denying that God exists.
D) By claiming existence is an accident.

Key Takeaway
Existence is an analogical term; God’s existence is unique and cannot be compared to that of creatures.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How does the realization that God's existence is fundamentally different from your own affect the way you understand your absolute dependence on Him? Are there areas of your life where you subtly act as if your existence is self-sufficient?
?
If existence is analogical and God's existence is necessary while yours is contingent, what does that mean for your sense of security? In what specific ways does this truth demand that you rely on God rather than on your own strength?
?
When you reflect on the fact that quiddity is neutral and does not determine existence, does it humble you to know that your being is a gift, not a given? How should this reshape your gratitude and worship today?

The Priority of the Creator is Beyond Time
Razi answers the second objection regarding the pre-eternity of time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Creator’s priority is not temporal and how intellect refrains from applying spatial or temporal categories to God.

Core Idea
God’s priority is beyond both space and time.

The second objection claims that if God is pre-eternal, then time must also be pre-eternal. Razi answers by clarifying that God’s priority is not temporal; time is created. This lesson explains why the imagination misleads us and how intellect must affirm a priority outside our categories.

Guided Path
1
Read the second objection and answer
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the core issue: how can God be prior if time is created?
2
Understand the answer: time is created
Razi states that time is among God’s creations. Therefore, God is not in time; His priority is not temporal.
This avoids the regress.
3
The role of imagination and intellect
Imagination (wahm) wrongly compares God to temporal things. Intellect (‘aql) must refuse to apply temporal or spatial priority to God.
Imagination is a lower faculty.
4
Affirm a unique priority
Razi concludes that God has a priority outside both categories, even if imagination cannot grasp it.
This is a key point of transcendence.

وأما المعارضة الأولى لإثبات واجب الوجود بأن وجود واجب الوجود إن كان مساويا بالوجود الممكنات لزم أن يصح عليه ما يصح على الممكنات ليس بشيء ، فإن من فهم الفرق بين المعاني المتواطئة والمعانى المشككات عرف أن الوجود على الواجب وعلى غيره لا يقع بالتساوى، وإن كان المفهوم من الوجود شيئا واحدا وحينئذ لا يلزم منه أن يصح على الواجب ما يصح على الممكنات من غير أن يذهب إلى أن الوجود ليس بمشترك وقوله : إن كانت على الوجود ماهية الوجود كان المعدوم ماهية الموجود فباطل ، لأن الماهية وحدها لا تكون موجودة ولا معدومة وهذا هو عين مذهبه الذي ذكره في سائر المواضع وأبطاله ههنا وأما المعارضة الثانية بوجوب قدم الزمان وجوابه بأن تقدم البارى على العالم كتقدم بعض أجزاء الزمان على البعض الآخر فقد سبق ما يرد عليه، والحق أن البارى تعالى ليس بزمان والزمان من مبدعاته والوهم يقيس ما لا يكون في الزمان على ما فى الزمان كما مر فى المكان ، والعقل كما يأبى عن إطلاق التقدم المكاني على البارى كذلك يأبى عن إطلاق التقدم الزمانى عليه ، بل ينبغي أن يقال إن للبارى تعالى تقدما خارجا عن القسمين ، وإن كان الوهم خارجا عن توهمه .
As for the first objection against the proof of the Necessary Being: that the existence of the Necessary Being, if it were equal in existence to possible beings, would entail that what applies to possible beings applies to Him. This is nothing, for whoever understands the difference between univocal (mutawāṭi'a) and analogical (mushakkaka) terms knows that existence for the Necessary and for other than Him does not occur equally. Even if the concept of existence were one thing, then this does not entail that what applies to possible beings applies to the Necessary, without going so far as to say that existence is not common. And his statement: if the quiddity of existence were [the same as] existence, then the non-existent would be the quiddity of the existent is false, because the quiddity alone is neither existent nor non-existent, and this is exactly his doctrine which he mentioned in other places and refuted it here. As for the second objection concerning the necessity of the pre-eternity of time, and the answer: that the priority of the Creator over the world is like the priority of some parts of time over others, there has already preceded what refutes it. The truth is that the Creator, exalted, is not temporal, and time is among His creations. The imagination (al-wahm) compares what is not in time to what is in time, as preceded in the case of space. Intellect ('aql) just as it refuses to apply spatial priority to the Creator, so too it refuses to apply temporal priority to Him. Rather, it should be said that the Creator, exalted, has a priority outside both categories, even if the imagination is incapable of imagining it.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage presents two objections against the proof of the Necessary Being and their answers. The first objection: if the existence of the Necessary Being is univocally the same as that of possible beings, then He would be subject to the same accidents as possibles. Razi answers by distinguishing between univocal terms (المعاني المتواطئة) which apply equally to all instances, and analogical terms (المعاني المشككات) which apply in a graded manner. Existence is not univocal; it applies to the Necessary Being in a different way. Even if the concept of existence were one, that does not entail that all properties apply equally. He also refutes the claim that if quiddity were existence, then non-existence would be the quiddity of existence—that is false because quiddity itself is neither existent nor non-existent. The second objection: if the Creator is pre-eternal, then time must also be pre-eternal. The answer: the priority of the Creator is not temporal; time is created. The imagination wrongly compares God to temporal things. Intellect refuses to apply temporal priority to God; His priority is beyond both spatial and temporal categories.

Key Terms
قدم الزمان
وهم
عقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's existence is temporally prior to the world.
Correction: God's priority is not temporal; time is created, and God transcends time.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, why is God not temporally prior to the world?
A) Because time is eternal.
B) Because time is created by God.
C) Because God is inside time.
D) Because priority is only spatial.

Key Takeaway
God’s priority over creation is neither spatial nor temporal; it is a unique priority beyond human categories.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's existence before creation, do you unconsciously imagine Him as existing 'in time'? What steps can you take today to train your intellect to reject that temporal assumption?
?
How often do you rely on your imagination (wahm) to conceptualize God, even when the intellect ('aql) tells you He is beyond such categories? What does this reveal about your spiritual discipline?
?
If time is created and God is not bound by it, what does that mean for your anxieties about the future or regrets about the past? How can this truth transform your trust in Him this week?

Refuting Objections on Divine Existence and Non-Existence
Razi refutes objections about existence being composite and non-existence having distinctions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's dialectical defense of the simplicity and uniqueness of divine existence against objections based on composition and distinctions in non-existence.

Core Idea
The Necessary Being's existence cannot be composite, and non-existents' distinctions do not imply reality.

Theologians argued that God's existence must be either identical to contingent existence or different. If identical, God would be contingent; if different, His essence would be composite and thus contingent. Others claimed that distinctions among non-existents (e.g., absence of blackness vs. absence of motion) give them some reality. In this passage, Razi dismantles both objections and clarifies the nature of descriptions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the complete Arabic passage once to get a feel for Razi's style, then read the literal translation. Let the argument unfold before analyzing.
Note the concise, dialectical style typical of kalam.
2
Analyze the first objection and answer
Identify the dilemma: if divine existence is like contingent existence, it is contingent; if different, it is composite and thus contingent. Find Razi's answer: the denial of an intermediate (between being alike and different) is known necessarily and by proof.
This answer refutes the need to accept either horn of the dilemma.
3
Analyze the second objection and answer
The opponent says non-existents are distinct. Razi responds that if mere distinction sufficed for being a creator, then a non-existent human with existent attributes could be a creator, which is sophistry.
This shows that distinctions in non-existence are merely negative, not positive.
4
Understand the final point on descriptions
The passage ends with Razi's response to the claim that 'existence' is a shared description: he refuses that it is shared in the way alleged, and states that descriptions are either negative or positive.
This reinforces that existence is not a genus or shared attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجود، فإن لم يخالف غيره في وجه آخر كان مثلا للممكن مطلقا فيكون ممكنا مطلقا وإن خالفه كانت حقيقته مركبة وكل مركب مفتقر إلى أجزائه وغيره، وكل مركب فهو مفتقر إلى غيره وكل مفتقر إلى الغير ممكن، فالواجب ممكن . هذا خلف . والجواب : بينا أن نفى الواسطة معلوم بالضرورة وبالبرهان على ما تقدم . قوله : العدمات متميزة قلنا : لو كفى ذلك في أن يكون خالقا فليجوز أن يكون الإنسان معدوما وإن كانت الصفات القائمة به موجودة ، وذلك عين السفسطة أما المعارضة فجوابها لا نسلم كون الشيء وصفا مشتركا فيه بين الموجودات . وهى إما سلبية أو ثبوتية .
Existence: if it does not differ from other [existents] in some respect, then it would be absolutely like the possible [being], so it would be absolutely possible; if it differs, then its reality is composite, and every composite is in need of its parts and other than itself, and every composite is in need of other than itself, and every being in need of another is possible, so the Necessary would be possible. This is a contradiction. The answer: we have shown that the denial of the intermediate is known necessarily and by proof as preceded. His statement: non-existents are distinct. We said: if that sufficed for being a creator, then it would be permissible for a human to be non-existent while the attributes subsisting in him are existent, and that is sheer sophistry. As for the objection, its answer: we do not admit that a thing is a shared description among existents [in that way]. And it is either negative or positive.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and answer about the nature of existence and distinctions in non-existence. The objection: if existence of the Necessary Being does not differ from other existents, then He is like a possible being; if it differs, then His reality is composite, and every composite is contingent, so the Necessary would be possible. That is a contradiction. Razi's answer: the denial of an intermediate (between being alike and being different) is known necessarily and by proof. Then there is an objection: non-existents are distinct (e.g., absence of blackness vs absence of motion). Razi answers: if mere distinction sufficed to be a creator, then a human could be non-existent while his attributes exist, which is sophistry. Finally, regarding the objection that a thing is a shared description among existents, the answer is that we do not admit that, and such descriptions are either negative or positive.

ممكن
واسطة
عدم
سفسطة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Distinctions among non-existents (e.g., absence of blackness vs. absence of motion) indicate that non-existents have some positive reality or attribute.
Correction: Razi shows that if that were so, then a non-existent human could be a creator if his attributes exist, which is absurd. Distinctions in non-existence are merely negative, not positive.

Checkpoint
What is Razi's answer to the objection that non-existents are distinct?
A) He agrees that non-existents are distinct but argues they are not real.
B) He says that if distinction sufficed for being a creator, then a non-existent human could be a creator, which is sophistry.
C) He claims that non-existents cannot be distinguished at all.
D) He says that distinctions among non-existents are positive attributes.

Key Takeaway
Razi defends the simplicity of divine existence by denying an intermediate between likeness and difference, and refutes the idea that distinctions among non-existents imply reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you think of God, do you ever mistakenly imagine Him as having parts or being like other beings in any way? How does Razi’s rejection of an intermediate between likeness and difference challenge you to purify your concept of God? What specific step will you take today to guard against that subtle error?
?

In your daily life, when you say 'God exists,' do you feel it is just another label like 'I exist'? How does this lesson push you to affirm that His existence is absolutely unique and necessary? Make a concrete commitment to remember that in your next prayer.

The Proof that God is Powerful
Proof that God is powerful via exhaustive division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the contingency of the world necessitates that its Creator is powerful (qadir) with the ability to choose.

Core Idea
God's power is proven by the fact that the world could have not existed.

In this passage, Imam Razi presents the core argument for God's power. He begins by establishing that the world requires an agent, and then demonstrates that this agent cannot be a necessitating cause, but must be one that can choose to act or not act. This is the definition of 'powerful'.

Guided Path
1
Context of the issue
The issue is that all agree God is powerful, but the majority of philosophers deny it. Read the opening lines to understand the disagreement.
Philosophers hold a different concept of divine agency.
2
Follow the bifurcation
The proof splits the possibilities: either the agent's effect issues inevitably (necessitating) or with possibility (powerful). Identify these two categories.
This is a classic kalam approach: exhaustive division.
3
Refutation of the first possibility
The first possibility leads to either pre-eternity of the world (if no condition) or an infinite regress (if condition is originated). Both are impossible. Understand why.
The impossibility of infinite regress is a key principle.
4
Conclusion that God is powerful
Since the first possibility is false, the second is established. The agent must be powerful, meaning He can act or refrain. This is the definition of qadir.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القول في الصفات الثبوتية) مسألة : اتفق الكل على أنه تعالى قادر خلافا لجمهور الفلاسفة ) لنا أنه ثبت افتقار العالم إلى مؤثر فذلك المؤثر إما أن يقال صدر الأثر عنه مع امتناع أن لا يصدر أو صدر مع جواز أن لا يصدر ، والأول باطل لأن تأثيره في وجود العالم إن لم يتوقف على شرط لزم من قدمه قدم العالم وقد أبطلناه ، وإن توقف على شرط فذلك الشرط إن كان قديما عاد الإلزام ، فإن كان حادثا كان الكلام في حدوثه كالكلام فى الأول ويلزم التسلسل إما إلى ما لا نهاية وهو محال أو لا إلى أول فيلزم منه حوادث لا أول لها وهو محال ، ولما بطل هذا القسم ثبت الثانى ولا نعنى القادر إلا ذلك (١) فإن قيل لم لا يجوز أن يكون المؤثر موجبا قوله يلزم من قدمه قدم العالم . قلنا إما أن يكون صحيح الوجود فى الأزل أو لا يكون ، فإن كان الأول لم يكن قدم العالم محالا فنحن نلتزمه وإن كان الثاني كان لصحة وجوده بذاته ، وإذا كان كذلك لم يلزم من قدم المؤثر قدم العالم ، لأن صدور الأثر عن المؤثر كما يعتبر فيه وجود المؤثر يعتبر فيه إمكان الأثر ، والذى يؤيده وهو أن القادر عندك هو الذي منه الإيجاد ، والله تعالى كان قادرا في الأزل ولم يلزم من أزلية قدرته صحة الإيجاد أزلا فلما لم يصح يلزم من القدرة الأزلية حصول الصحة في الأزل، فلم لا يجوز أن لا يلزم من وجود المؤثر وجود العالم في الأزل، سلمنا أنه لو لم يتوقف تأثيره في العالم على شرطه لزم من قدمه قدم العالم، فلم لا يجوز أن يقال تأثيره في وجود العالم كان موقوفا على شرط حادث وحدوث ذلك الشرط على شرط آخر لا إلى أول ، والكلام فيه يرجع إلى مسألة حوادث لا أول لها سلمنا أنه لابد من القادر ، لكن لم قلت إنه واجب الوجود ولم لا يجوز أن يقال واجب الوجود اقتضى لذاته موجودا قديما ليس مجسم ولا جسمانى ، وذلك المعلول كان قادرا وهو الذى خلق العالم . سلمنا أن ما ذكرتموه يدل على القادر لكنه معارض بنوعين من الكلام الأول أن يبين أن حقيقة القادر على الوجه الذي قلتموه محال وبيانه من وجوه
Section on Positive Attributes. Issue: All agreed that He, may He be exalted, is powerful (qadir), contrary to the majority of philosophers. For us: It is established that the world is in need of an agent. That agent either the effect issues from Him with impossibility of not issuing, or issues with possibility of not issuing. The first is invalid because if His influence in the existence of the world does not depend on a condition, then from His pre-eternity would follow the pre-eternity of the world, which we have refuted. And if it depends on a condition, then if that condition is pre-eternal, the same objection returns. If it is originated, then the discussion about its origination is like the discussion about the first, and an infinite regress follows either to infinity, which is impossible, or to no first, which entails events without a beginning, which is impossible. When this category is invalidated, the second is established, and we mean by 'powerful' except that. If it is said: Why is it not permissible that the agent be necessitating (mujib) such that from its pre-eternity follows the pre-eternity of the world? We say: Either it is correct in existence in pre-eternity or not. If the first, then the pre-eternity of the world is not impossible, so we accept it. If the second, then its existence is due to itself. If so, then pre-eternity of the agent does not necessitate pre-eternity of the world, because the issue of the effect from the agent considers both the existence of the agent and the possibility of the effect. What supports this is that the powerful one in your view is the one from whom bringing-into-existence issues. God was powerful in pre-eternity, and from the pre-eternity of His power the validity of bringing-into-existence pre-eternally did not follow. Since the pre-eternal power did not entail the occurrence of validity in pre-eternity, why is it not permissible that the existence of the agent does not entail the existence of the world in pre-eternity? We concede that if His influence in the world did not depend on a condition, then from His pre-eternity would follow the pre-eternity of the world. So why is it not permissible to say that His influence in the existence of the world was dependent on an originated condition, and the origination of that condition on another condition without a first? The discussion about this returns to the issue of events without a beginning. We concede that there must be a powerful being, but why do you say He is necessary of existence? Why is it not permissible to say that the Necessary of Existence entails by His essence a pre-eternal existent that is neither a body nor corporeal, and that effect was powerful and it created the world? We concede that what you have mentioned indicates the powerful, but it is opposed by two types of discourse: the first is to show that the reality of the powerful in the sense you said is impossible, and its exposition is from several aspects...
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage is a complex proof and objection-response. It begins by stating the consensus that God is powerful (قادر), against the philosophers who deny this in the sense of contingent will. The proof is based on the world's need for an agent, and that agent must have the possibility of action or non-action, because a necessitating agent would require the world's pre-eternity, which is rejected. The objection that the agent could be a necessitating cause (موجب) is answered by examining whether the agent's existence in pre-eternity is possible or actual. Further objections consider the possibility of a condition chain and the possibility of a mediated power. The text then moves to the objection that the proof only shows a powerful being, not a necessary being, and that the first effect could be powerful. Finally, the objector argues that the concept of powerful as defined may be impossible. The passage ends with the beginning of that objection.

Key Terms
القادر
الموجب
حادث العالم
التسلسل
الحوادث لا أول لها

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the proof assumes the world is originated, but it actually argues from the world's need for an agent independent of its temporal origination.
Correction: The proof only uses the fact that the world depends on an agent, not necessarily that it is originated in time. However, the impossibility of pre-eternity of the world is established elsewhere.

Checkpoint
Why is the first possibility (necessitating agent) rejected?
A) Because it leads to the pre-eternity of the world or an infinite regress.
B) Because philosophers deny it.
C) Because the agent must be free.
D) Because the world is originated.

Key Takeaway
The proof that God is powerful relies on the impossibility of a necessitating cause, which would entail either a pre-eternal world or an infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The proof shows that God's power is defined by possibility of both action and inaction. Have you ever limited God's power in your mind by assuming He 'must' do something for you? How will you trust His free choice now?

Refuting Objections to the Proof of God's Power
Responses to objections against the proof of God's power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key objections raised against the proof of God's power and how Imam Razi refutes them.

Core Idea
Objections fail because they either lead to absurdities or misunderstand the nature of power.

After presenting the proof, Imam Razi anticipates several objections from the philosophers. He addresses each in turn, showing that they do not undermine the proof. These objections include the possibility of a necessitating agent, infinite regress of conditions, and the claim that the powerful being might not be necessary.

Guided Path
1
First objection: Why not a necessitating agent?
The objector asks why the agent cannot be a nécessitating cause (mujib). Razi responds by examining whether the agent's existence in pre-eternity is possible or actual.
This response uses the same principle of the world's contingency.
2
Second objection: Infinite regress of conditions
The objector suggests that the agent's influence may depend on an originated condition, which itself depends on another, without a first. Razi notes this returns to the issue of events without a beginning.
This is a known debate about the impossibility of an infinite regress of contingent events.
3
Third objection: The powerful being need not be necessary
The objector argues that the proof only shows a powerful being, not that the being is necessary. Razi's response is not fully given here, but the passage implies that the proof for power can be combined with other proofs for necessity.
This connects to the broader discussion of divine attributes.
4
Fourth objection: The definition of powerful is impossible
The objector claims that the concept of 'powerful' as defined is impossible, and begins to list aspects. However, the passage only mentions this objection without full exposition.
This shows that not all objections are fully addressed in this section.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القول في الصفات الثبوتية) مسألة : اتفق الكل على أنه تعالى قادر خلافا لجمهور الفلاسفة ) لنا أنه ثبت افتقار العالم إلى مؤثر فذلك المؤثر إما أن يقال صدر الأثر عنه مع امتناع أن لا يصدر أو صدر مع جواز أن لا يصدر ، والأول باطل لأن تأثيره في وجود العالم إن لم يتوقف على شرط لزم من قدمه قدم العالم وقد أبطلناه ، وإن توقف على شرط فذلك الشرط إن كان قديما عاد الإلزام ، فإن كان حادثا كان الكلام في حدوثه كالكلام فى الأول ويلزم التسلسل إما إلى ما لا نهاية وهو محال أو لا إلى أول فيلزم منه حوادث لا أول لها وهو محال ، ولما بطل هذا القسم ثبت الثانى ولا نعنى القادر إلا ذلك (١) فإن قيل لم لا يجوز أن يكون المؤثر موجبا قوله يلزم من قدمه قدم العالم . قلنا إما أن يكون صحيح الوجود فى الأزل أو لا يكون ، فإن كان الأول لم يكن قدم العالم محالا فنحن نلتزمه وإن كان الثاني كان لصحة وجوده بذاته ، وإذا كان كذلك لم يلزم من قدم المؤثر قدم العالم ، لأن صدور الأثر عن المؤثر كما يعتبر فيه وجود المؤثر يعتبر فيه إمكان الأثر ، والذى يؤيده وهو أن القادر عندك هو الذي منه الإيجاد ، والله تعالى كان قادرا في الأزل ولم يلزم من أزلية قدرته صحة الإيجاد أزلا فلما لم يصح يلزم من القدرة الأزلية حصول الصحة في الأزل، فلم لا يجوز أن لا يلزم من وجود المؤثر وجود العالم في الأزل، سلمنا أنه لو لم يتوقف تأثيره في العالم على شرطه لزم من قدمه قدم العالم، فلم لا يجوز أن يقال تأثيره في وجود العالم كان موقوفا على شرط حادث وحدوث ذلك الشرط على شرط آخر لا إلى أول ، والكلام فيه يرجع إلى مسألة حوادث لا أول لها سلمنا أنه لابد من القادر ، لكن لم قلت إنه واجب الوجود ولم لا يجوز أن يقال واجب الوجود اقتضى لذاته موجودا قديما ليس مجسم ولا جسمانى ، وذلك المعلول كان قادرا وهو الذى خلق العالم . سلمنا أن ما ذكرتموه يدل على القادر لكنه معارض بنوعين من الكلام الأول أن يبين أن حقيقة القادر على الوجه الذي قلتموه محال وبيانه من وجوه
Section on Positive Attributes. Issue: All agreed that He, may He be exalted, is powerful (qadir), contrary to the majority of philosophers. For us: It is established that the world is in need of an agent. That agent either the effect issues from Him with impossibility of not issuing, or issues with possibility of not issuing. The first is invalid because if His influence in the existence of the world does not depend on a condition, then from His pre-eternity would follow the pre-eternity of the world, which we have refuted. And if it depends on a condition, then if that condition is pre-eternal, the same objection returns. If it is originated, then the discussion about its origination is like the discussion about the first, and an infinite regress follows either to infinity, which is impossible, or to no first, which entails events without a beginning, which is impossible. When this category is invalidated, the second is established, and we mean by 'powerful' except that. If it is said: Why is it not permissible that the agent be necessitating (mujib) such that from its pre-eternity follows the pre-eternity of the world? We say: Either it is correct in existence in pre-eternity or not. If the first, then the pre-eternity of the world is not impossible, so we accept it. If the second, then its existence is due to itself. If so, then pre-eternity of the agent does not necessitate pre-eternity of the world, because the issue of the effect from the agent considers both the existence of the agent and the possibility of the effect. What supports this is that the powerful one in your view is the one from whom bringing-into-existence issues. God was powerful in pre-eternity, and from the pre-eternity of His power the validity of bringing-into-existence pre-eternally did not follow. Since the pre-eternal power did not entail the occurrence of validity in pre-eternity, why is it not permissible that the existence of the agent does not entail the existence of the world in pre-eternity? We concede that if His influence in the world did not depend on a condition, then from His pre-eternity would follow the pre-eternity of the world. So why is it not permissible to say that His influence in the existence of the world was dependent on an originated condition, and the origination of that condition on another condition without a first? The discussion about this returns to the issue of events without a beginning. We concede that there must be a powerful being, but why do you say He is necessary of existence? Why is it not permissible to say that the Necessary of Existence entails by His essence a pre-eternal existent that is neither a body nor corporeal, and that effect was powerful and it created the world? We concede that what you have mentioned indicates the powerful, but it is opposed by two types of discourse: the first is to show that the reality of the powerful in the sense you said is impossible, and its exposition is from several aspects...
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage is a complex proof and objection-response. It begins by stating the consensus that God is powerful (قادر), against the philosophers who deny this in the sense of contingent will. The proof is based on the world's need for an agent, and that agent must have the possibility of action or non-action, because a necessitating agent would require the world's pre-eternity, which is rejected. The objection that the agent could be a necessitating cause (موجب) is answered by examining whether the agent's existence in pre-eternity is possible or actual. Further objections consider the possibility of a condition chain and the possibility of a mediated power. The text then moves to the objection that the proof only shows a powerful being, not a necessary being, and that the first effect could be powerful. Finally, the objector argues that the concept of powerful as defined may be impossible. The passage ends with the beginning of that objection.

Key Terms

الموجب
التسلسل
الحوادث لا أول لها
وجوب الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the proof of God's power is complete without addressing objections, but Razi shows that the proof withstands scrutiny.
Correction: The objections are serious but are answered, though some responses are only sketched.

Checkpoint
How does Razi respond to the objection that the agent could be a necessitating cause (mujib)?
A) He says it is impossible because the agent must be free.
B) He examines whether the agent's existence in pre-eternity is possible or actual, showing neither requires the world to be pre-eternal.
C) He accepts the objection and modifies the proof.
D) He says the agent's power is eternal, so the world must be eternal.

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi addresses multiple objections to the proof of God's power, showing that the proof remains valid against attempts to reintroduce necessitarian causation or infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face delays in your duas being answered, do you ever doubt God's power? How does Razi's argument that power does not necessitate immediate effect help you trust His timing?
?
The objectors argued that an eternal agent would necessitate an eternal world, but Razi showed that possibility also matters. In your life, what 'possibilities' might God be waiting for before He acts? Are you patient enough to wait for His wisdom?
?
Razi refutes the notion of an infinite regress of conditions. Are there areas in your life where you keep adding conditions before obeying God? What first step can you take today to break that chain?

The Objection: A Necessary Being Could Produce a Qadir
Razi presents an objection to the First Qadir being the Necessary Being.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand a key objection to identifying the first Qadir with the necessarily existent being, and why this objection is not a polytheistic proposal but a logical challenge.

Core Idea
A necessary being might produce a Qadir, so why must God be the first Qadir?

This lesson examines an objection raised against the kalam argument that the first Qadir must be the Necessary Being (God). The objector concedes that a Qadir is required, but challenges the conclusion that this Qadir is the necessarily existent. Instead, they propose that a necessary being could produce an eternal, non-corporeal Qadir, which then creates the world. Understanding this objection helps sharpen the theological reasoning for God's unique role as the ultimate Agent.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic text and its translation carefully. Notice the structure: first a concession ('we grant'), then a question, then a proposed alternative.
The objector is not denying that a Qadir exists; they are denying that it must be the first cause.
2
Identify the two parts of the objection
The objection has two parts: (1) the concession that a Qadir is necessary, and (2) the proposal that a necessary being produces a Qadir who then creates. Identify each part in the text.
3
Consider the implied challenge
Think about why this objection is problematic for the standard kalam argument. If a necessary being can produce a Qadir, then God may not be directly responsible for creation. How would a kalam theologian respond?
The typical response involves infinite regress: if the Qadir itself is an effect, it needs another cause, leading to an endless chain.
4
Reflect on the misunderstanding
Some readers mistakenly think this objection proposes multiple gods. But it is a logical challenge about causal chains. Discuss why this distinction matters.
The core issue is about identifying the first agent, not about accepting polytheism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلمنا أنه لابد من القادر، لكن لم قلت إنه واجب الوجود ولم لا يجوز أن يقال واجب الوجود اقتضى لذاته موجودا قديما ليس مجسم ولا جسمانى ، وذلك المعلول كان قادرا وهو الذى خلق العالم
We grant that a Qadir is necessary, but why do you say it is necessarily existent? And why is it not permissible to say that the necessarily existent required by its essence an eternal existent that is neither a body nor corporeal, and that effect was Qadir, and it is the one that created the world?
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage is an objection. The objector accepts that a Qadir is needed but questions why that Qadir must be the necessarily existent (God). Perhaps a necessary being (واجب الوجود) could produce an eternal, non-corporeal being that is Qadir, and that being creates the world. The text does not include an explicit response, but the implication is that this leads to an infinite regress because the Qadir would itself be an effect, requiring another agent, etc.

Key Terms
واجب الوجود
قادر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think this objection is a proposal of polytheism, i.e., multiple gods.
Correction: The objection is not about multiple gods but about the causal chain: it suggests that a necessary being produces a Qadir, which is a logical challenge to identify the first agent, not a claim of multiple deities.

Checkpoint
What does the objector propose as an alternative to God being the first Qadir?
A) The world is self-sufficient.
B) A necessary being produces a Qadir that creates the world.
C) There are multiple first qadirs.
D) The Qadir is the world itself.

Key Takeaway
This objection challenges the identification of the first Qadir with the Necessary Being, proposing instead that the Necessary Being could produce a Qadir, which shifts the causal chain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God could have delegated creation to a created agent, does that weaken your sense of His direct involvement in your life? In what ways do you currently rely on 'intermediaries' instead of turning directly to the First Cause?
?
The objector's proposal seems to push the ultimate cause further away. Do you ever find yourself satisfied with secondary explanations for events, avoiding the uncomfortable reality that everything traces back to God? What would it look like to live acknowledging Him as the immediate Qadir over every detail?
?
If the chain of causes must end in a self-sufficient Qadir, what does that demand of you when you feel your own power is insufficient? Are you willing to admit that your own ability to act is entirely dependent on the First Qadir, or do you cling to an illusion of independence?
