The Impossibility of an Eternal Obstacle
Imam Razi argues that an eternal obstacle to creation is impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why positing an eternal obstacle to creation leads to a logical contradiction.

Core Idea
An eternal obstacle is impossible because it creates an absurdity.

Imam Razi presents a concise dilemma that refutes any claim that an eternal obstacle prevented the creation of the world. By examining whether such an obstacle can cease to exist or not, he shows that both options lead to contradictions. This argument demonstrates the power of logical reasoning in theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the logical structure.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
Notice that the argument splits into two cases: either the obstacle can cease by itself or it cannot. Each case leads to an unacceptable consequence.
3
Understand the conclusion
Realize that the only consistent position is that no eternal obstacle can exist, affirming that God's power to create is not hindered.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلت المانع ان كان ممكن الزوال لذاته فليفرض إرتفاعه وحينئذ يصح الفعل الأزلي هذا خلف وإن كان ممتنع الزوال لذاته فليفرض وجب أن يكون كذلك أبداً ، إذ لو جاز أن ينقلب ممكنا لجاز أن يقال العالم كان ممتنعاً لذاته ثم انقلب واجباً .
I say: if the obstacle is such that it is possible for it to cease by itself, then let its removal be assumed, and then the eternal act would be valid—this is a contradiction. And if it is impossible for it to cease by itself, then it must be that it is thus forever, for if it were possible for it to become possible, then it could be said that the world was impossible by itself and then became necessary.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage presents a dilemma against the opponent who posits an eternal obstacle preventing the world's creation. Imam Razi argues: either the obstacle can cease to exist by its own nature, or it cannot. If it can, we can imagine its removal, and then the world would have existed eternally—which is false (the world is not eternal). If it cannot cease, then it is permanently impossible for the world to come into existence, which is also false. Therefore, the notion of an eternal obstacle is absurd.

Key Terms
مانع
أزلي
خلف
ممتنع
واجب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that God's power can be limited by an eternal obstacle.
Correction: Imam Razi shows that an eternal obstacle is logically impossible; it is not a matter of power but of logical consistency.

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion of the dilemma about the obstacle?
A) The obstacle can be removed so the world is eternal.
B) The obstacle cannot be removed so the world is impossible.
C) The obstacle cannot be eternal because it leads to contradiction.
D) The obstacle is irrelevant to creation.

Key Takeaway
Positing an eternal obstacle to creation leads to an unavoidable contradiction, proving it cannot exist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Does your life reveal a belief that there are obstacles that can delay or frustrate God's will? What would it mean for your trust in Him if you truly accepted that no eternal obstacle can stand in His way?
?
The argument shows that the world's existence itself disproves any eternal barrier to creation. If your own existence is a sign of God's unhindered power, what does that demand of you in terms of gratitude and obedience?
?
If the idea of an eternal obstacle is logically impossible, what 'obstacles' do you mentally erect that prevent you from fully submitting to God's commands? Are they real, or are they illusions your soul clings to?

General Answer on the World's Non-Existence in Eternity
General response to objection about world's non-existence in eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the world's non-existence in eternity does not imply a limit on God's power, and how the necessity of the cause differs from voluntary choice.

Core Idea
The cause's necessity, not power, explains why the world is not eternal.

Imam Razi begins by addressing a key objection: if God is all-powerful, why didn't He create the world earlier in eternity? The answer lies in distinguishing between what is impossible due to power/choice and what is impossible due to a necessitating cause. This lesson unpacks that distinction and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the general answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the logical flow of the response.
Pay attention to the terms 'قدرة' (power) and 'اختيار' (choice).
2
Identify the key distinction
Notice that Imam Razi distinguishes between impossibility from power/choice and impossibility from a necessitating cause. Understand why this distinction is crucial.
3
Consider the 'day argument'
Reflect on the hypothetical: if the world had existed one day earlier, it would not be eternal. This shows that eternal existence is not required, and the cause being present does not force immediate creation.
4
Review refutations of beginningless events and intermediaries
Understand why beginningless temporal events and intermediaries are dismissed: the former is already refuted, the latter rejected by Muslim consensus.
This reinforces the Islamic position on creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب : قوله إنما لم يوجد العالم في الأزل لإستحالة وجوده أولا . قلنا وقوع العالم بالقدرة والاختيار فى الأزل محال ، أما استناده إلى العلة الموجبة غير محال فلم يصلح هذا عن صدوره عن الملة القديمة في الأزل ، سلمنا كونه محالا فى الأزل لكن لو وجد قبل أن وجد بمقدار يوم لم يصر بسبب ذلك أرليا ، فكان يجب أن يوجد قبل أن وجد ، لأن العلة قائمة والمانع المذكور مفقود وأما حوادث لا أول لها فقد تقدم إبطالها، وأما الواسطة فقد أجمع المسلمون على إبطالها . أنا المعارضة الأولى: الجوابها أنه لم لا يجوز أن يكون المؤثر المستجمع مجميع جهات المؤثرية تارة يكون مصدراً الأثر وتارة لا يكون ، ونحن قد بينا أن المختار هو الذي يمكنه الترجيح لا لمرجح . وأما الثانية الجوابها : إما التمكن ثابت بالنسبة إلى المندور قبل دخوله في الموجود حوله لامكنة في الحال علي الشيء الذي سيوجد في الاستقبال

قلنا لا نسلم . ولم لا يجوز أن يقال حصل في الحال التمكن من إيجاده في المستقبل.
وأما الثالثة : فجوابها أن القادر هو الذي يصح أن يصدر عنه ما يكون فى نفسه ممكنا ، والفعل إنما يصح فيما لا يزال ، فلا جرم كان الله قادراً فى الأزل على التكوين فيما لا يزال . وأما الرابعة : فجوابها أن النسبة التي أدعيتموها وبنيتم عليها الإمتياز منوعة، فليس في الوجود إلا القدرة والمقدور. وأما الخامسة : فجوابها أن التعاق إضافة ولا وجودها في الأعيان فلا يلزم عدم القديم . وأما السادسة : فجوابها أن الموجدية إضافة الذات إلى الأثر والإضافات لا وجود لها في الأعيان (1)
The answer: His statement 'the world did not exist in eternity only because its existence is impossible.' We say: the occurrence of the world by power and choice in eternity is impossible, but its being based on the necessitating cause is not impossible; so this does not refute its emanation from the eternal cause in eternity. Granted it is impossible in eternity, but if it had existed before it existed by a day, it would not thereby become eternal; so it should have existed before it existed, because the cause is present and the mentioned obstacle is absent. As for beginningless temporal events, they have already been refuted. As for the intermediary, the Muslims have unanimously refuted it. As for the first objection: its answer is that it is not permissible that the agent who possesses all aspects of agency sometimes becomes a source of effect and sometimes not, and we have shown that the voluntary agent is the one who can give preponderance without a preponderant. As for the second objection: its answer is that ability is established in relation to what is intended before it enters into existence, because there is no ability in the present moment with respect to something that will exist in the future. We do not accept that. Why not say that ability is obtained in the present to bring it into existence in the future? As for the third: its answer is that the powerful is the one from whom what is possible in itself can proceed, and the act is only valid in the future; thus God was powerful in eternity to create in the future. As for the fourth: its answer is that the relation you claim and upon which you built distinction is imaginary; in existence there is only power and the object of power. As for the fifth: its answer is that the connection is a relation and has no existence in external objects, so the perishing of the eternal does not follow. As for the sixth: its answer is that 'causing existence' is a relation of the essence to the effect, and relations have no existence in external objects.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This lesson contains a general answer and six specific answers to objections. The general answer addresses the claim that the world's non-existence in eternity is due to impossibility; the author distinguishes between impossibility by power/choice and impossibility by necessitating cause. Then he addresses objections: 1) The agent who possesses all necessary aspects of agency is not forced to always act; the voluntary agent can choose without a preponderant. 2) Ability (tamakkun) is related to future acts, not present ones. 3) God's power is eternal, but the act is in the future. 4) The supposed relation that implies distinction is an imagination; only power and its object exist. 5) The connection (ta‘alluq) is a relation, not an entity, so it cannot perish. 6) Causing existence is a relation, not an entity. These responses protect God's eternity and power.

Key Terms
قدرة
اختيار
إيجاد
معارضة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's omnipotence requires that He must create at the earliest possible moment, making the world eternal.
Correction: God's power is not constrained by a necessitating cause; He can choose to create at any time. The world's non-existence in eternity is due to His choice, not impossibility.

Checkpoint
According to the general answer, why is the world's non-existence in eternity not attributable to impossibility?
A) Because the world's existence in eternity is impossible absolutely.
B) Because impossibility only applies to power/choice, not to necessitating cause.
C) Because the world did exist in eternity.
D) Because God's power is limited.

Key Takeaway
The world's non-existence in eternity is due to God's choice, not a limitation of power; the necessitating cause does not force eternal creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face delays in your prayers being answered, do you ever doubt God's power? How does this lesson's distinction between power/choice and necessity reshape your patience?
?
Is there an area of your life where you demand God to act immediately, as if He were a necessitating cause? What would it mean for you to truly trust His choice and wisdom over your timeline?
?
If God's will is not bound by necessity, then your waiting is not a sign of His weakness but of His deliberate plan. How will you respond to the next unanswered prayer with that certainty?

Answers to Objections 1-3: Power and Choice
Responses to three objections on divine agency, ability, and power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the responses to three objections: that a fully capable agent must always act, that ability requires present existence, and that God's power is limited to the present.

Core Idea
A voluntary agent can choose without a preponderant, and ability extends to future acts.

The objectors raise specific points: if God has all aspects of agency, He must act; ability is only relevant to present objects; and God's power is confined to the present. Imam Razi's answers defend divine freedom and the scope of power. Let's examine each.

Guided Path
1
Read objections 1-3 and their answers
Read the Arabic and translation for the first three objections carefully. Note the structure: each begins with 'أما المعارضة ... فجوابها'.
Objection 1 concerns agency, objection 2 ability, objection 3 power.
2
Understand the first answer: voluntary agency
The answer asserts that the voluntary agent can give preponderance without a preponderant. This means God's choice is not forced by conditions.
3
Understand the second answer: ability for future acts
Imam Razi argues that ability (tamakkun) is established for the intended act before it exists, and we can affirm ability now for future existence.
The objection claimed ability only for present objects.
4
Understand the third answer: God's power is eternal, act is future
The answer states that the powerful is the one from whom possible acts can proceed, and acts only valid in the future. Thus God was powerful in eternity to create in the future.
This preserves God's eternal power while maintaining temporality of creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب : قوله إنما لم يوجد العالم في الأزل لإستحالة وجوده أولا . قلنا وقوع العالم بالقدرة والاختيار فى الأزل محال ، أما استناده إلى العلة الموجبة غير محال فلم يصلح هذا عن صدوره عن الملة القديمة في الأزل ، سلمنا كونه محالا فى الأزل لكن لو وجد قبل أن وجد بمقدار يوم لم يصر بسبب ذلك أرليا ، فكان يجب أن يوجد قبل أن وجد ، لأن العلة قائمة والمانع المذكور مفقود وأما حوادث لا أول لها فقد تقدم إبطالها، وأما الواسطة فقد أجمع المسلمون على إبطالها . أنا المعارضة الأولى: الجوابها أنه لم لا يجوز أن يكون المؤثر المستجمع مجميع جهات المؤثرية تارة يكون مصدراً الأثر وتارة لا يكون ، ونحن قد بينا أن المختار هو الذي يمكنه الترجيح لا لمرجح . وأما الثانية الجوابها : إما التمكن ثابت بالنسبة إلى المندور قبل دخوله في الموجود حوله لامكنة في الحال علي الشيء الذي سيوجد في الاستقبال

قلنا لا نسلم . ولم لا يجوز أن يقال حصل في الحال التمكن من إيجاده في المستقبل.
وأما الثالثة : فجوابها أن القادر هو الذي يصح أن يصدر عنه ما يكون فى نفسه ممكنا ، والفعل إنما يصح فيما لا يزال ، فلا جرم كان الله قادراً فى الأزل على التكوين فيما لا يزال . وأما الرابعة : فجوابها أن النسبة التي أدعيتموها وبنيتم عليها الإمتياز منوعة، فليس في الوجود إلا القدرة والمقدور. وأما الخامسة : فجوابها أن التعاق إضافة ولا وجودها في الأعيان فلا يلزم عدم القديم . وأما السادسة : فجوابها أن الموجدية إضافة الذات إلى الأثر والإضافات لا وجود لها في الأعيان (1)
The answer: His statement 'the world did not exist in eternity only because its existence is impossible.' We say: the occurrence of the world by power and choice in eternity is impossible, but its being based on the necessitating cause is not impossible; so this does not refute its emanation from the eternal cause in eternity. Granted it is impossible in eternity, but if it had existed before it existed by a day, it would not thereby become eternal; so it should have existed before it existed, because the cause is present and the mentioned obstacle is absent. As for beginningless temporal events, they have already been refuted. As for the intermediary, the Muslims have unanimously refuted it. As for the first objection: its answer is that it is not permissible that the agent who possesses all aspects of agency sometimes becomes a source of effect and sometimes not, and we have shown that the voluntary agent is the one who can give preponderance without a preponderant. As for the second objection: its answer is that ability is established in relation to what is intended before it enters into existence, because there is no ability in the present moment with respect to something that will exist in the future. We do not accept that. Why not say that ability is obtained in the present to bring it into existence in the future? As for the third: its answer is that the powerful is the one from whom what is possible in itself can proceed, and the act is only valid in the future; thus God was powerful in eternity to create in the future. As for the fourth: its answer is that the relation you claim and upon which you built distinction is imaginary; in existence there is only power and the object of power. As for the fifth: its answer is that the connection is a relation and has no existence in external objects, so the perishing of the eternal does not follow. As for the sixth: its answer is that 'causing existence' is a relation of the essence to the effect, and relations have no existence in external objects.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This lesson contains a general answer and six specific answers to objections. The general answer addresses the claim that the world's non-existence in eternity is due to impossibility; the author distinguishes between impossibility by power/choice and impossibility by necessitating cause. Then he addresses objections: 1) The agent who possesses all necessary aspects of agency is not forced to always act; the voluntary agent can choose without a preponderant. 2) Ability (tamakkun) is related to future acts, not present ones. 3) God's power is eternal, but the act is in the future. 4) The supposed relation that implies distinction is an imagination; only power and its object exist. 5) The connection (ta‘alluq) is a relation, not an entity, so it cannot perish. 6) Causing existence is a relation, not an entity. These responses protect God's eternity and power.

Key Terms
معارضة
جواب
تمكين
قدرة
اختيار
إيجاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God is able to do something, He must do it immediately; otherwise His power is limited.
Correction: God's ability is not constrained by time; He can choose to act at a future time, and His power is eternal even if the act is temporal.

Checkpoint
In the answer to the third objection, what is said about God's power?
A) God is powerful only in the present.
B) God is powerful in eternity to create in the future.
C) God's power is limited to the past.
D) God cannot create temporal things.

Key Takeaway
God's voluntary choice means He can act or not without a preponderant, and His power extends to future acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Imam Razi argues that God can choose to act or not without any external reason. Do your supplications reflect that you truly believe God's decisions are utterly free and not forced by your actions? If you were more convinced of this, how would your reliance on Him change in your daily worries?
?
The lesson says ability can be present now for a future act. When you plan for tomorrow, do you implicitly assume God's power is limited to the present moment? How can this understanding shape your anxiety about the future?
?
The third answer states God was powerful in eternity to create in the future. This means His power is not bound by time. In what area of your life do you most need to remember that God's power already exists for things you haven't even imagined? What specific step will you take today to trust that power over your own planning?

Answers to Objections 4-6: Relations and Divine Power
Responses to three objections on relations, connection, and causing existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes objections that rely on imaginary relations, showing that only power and its object exist externally, and that connections and causing existence are mere relations without external existence.

Core Idea
Relations like connection and causing existence are mental, not real, so they do not affect God's eternity.

The final three objections attempt to undermine divine power by appealing to relations: a supposed distinction between power and its object, the connection (ta‘alluq) as an entity, and causing existence (mujidiyya) as something real. Imam Razi's answers cut through by denying the external reality of these relations.

Guided Path
1
Read objections 4-6 and their answers
Read the Arabic and translation for the fourth, fifth, and sixth objections carefully. Notice the recurring theme of 'إضافة' (relation).
2
Understand the fourth answer: no real relation
The answer denies that there is any real relation that implies distinction. Only power (qudra) and its object (maqdur) exist.
3
Understand the fifth answer: connection is a relation
The answer states that connection (ta‘alluq) is a relation (idafa) with no external existence, so it cannot perish and affect the eternal.
4
Understand the sixth answer: causing existence is a relation
Similarly, 'causing existence' (mujidiyya) is a relation of the essence to the effect, and relations have no existence in external objects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Second Objection: Animals' Complex Acts and Razi's Response
Razi presents the objection from animal behaviors and the role of intermediaries, then responds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi responds to the objection that animals perform complex acts without knowledge, and why he considers such acts as evidence of specific knowledge.

Core Idea
Animal precision implies knowledge of that precise act.

The second objection against the design argument points to bees and spiders building intricate structures without apparent knowledge. Razi responds by noting that the very precision of their acts shows they have knowledge specific to those acts. This lesson unpacks the objection and its answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection about bees and spiders
Read the first part of the Arabic passage that describes how bees build hexagonal houses with wisdom that only engineers know, and spiders weave webs with utmost precision.
Notice the claim: animals perform these acts without knowledge or wisdom.
2
Read Razi's response
Read the second part: 'As for animals, whoever performs a well-ordered act is knowledgeable only of that act.'
Razi affirms that the act itself indicates knowledge of that act's requirements.
3
Consider the implication
Ask yourself: Does animal instinct refute the need for a wise designer? Razi says no—the act shows knowledge, albeit limited to that specific action.
This preserves the link between design and knowledge.
4
Reflect on the phrase 'عالم بذلك الفعل فقط'
This phrase is key: 'knowledgeable only of that act'. It limits the knowledge but does not deny it. Reflect on how this answers the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيها أن قبل النحلة في غاية الاحكام وهو بناء البيوت المسدسة مع كثرة ما فيها من الحكمة التي لا يعرفها إلا المهندسون ، وكذا العنكبوت تبنى بيتها في غاية الاحكام ، وكذلك نرى كل واحد من الحيوانات تأتى بالأفعال المرافقة لها بحيث يعجز عن تحصيلها أكثر الأذكياء مع أنه ليس بشيء منها علم ولا حكمة .
وأما الحيوانات فكل من فعل فعلا محكما فهو عالم بذلك الفعل فقط
The second: that the bee [?] is in the utmost precision—building hexagonal houses with much wisdom that only engineers know. Likewise the spider builds its web in utmost precision. Similarly we see every animal performs acts fitting to it such that most intelligent people are incapable of achieving them, while there is no knowledge nor wisdom in any of them. As for animals, whoever performs a well-ordered act is knowledgeable only of that act.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection against the proof from design, and a brief response. The objector points to animals like bees building perfect hexagonal hives and spiders weaving intricate webs—acts of great precision that even smart humans cannot replicate—yet these animals have no knowledge or wisdom. If design does not require knowledge in animals, why must it in God? Razi responds: each animal that performs a well-ordered act (محكم) is indeed knowledgeable specifically of that act (عالم بذلك الفقط فقط). So animals do have a kind of knowledge for their specific actions.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think animal instinct implies no knowledge at all, thus design does not require a knowing designer.
Correction: Razi argues that the very precision of the act shows the animal has knowledge of that act's requirements, so design still entails knowledge, albeit limited.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's response to the animal objection, what is true of animals that perform well-ordered acts?
A) They have no knowledge at all.
B) They are knowledgeable only of that specific act.
C) They are knowers like human engineers.
D) Their acts are actually God's acts.

Key Takeaway
Razi maintains that any well-ordered act requires knowledge of that act, so animal behaviors do not undermine the design argument—they actually demonstrate specific knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How does your answer affect your gratitude to Allah for placing such wisdom in creation? Commit to reflecting on one natural phenomenon today and thanking Allah for the knowledge He has embedded in it.
?
Razi argues that animals have knowledge only of their specific act. In your own life, are you content with knowing just enough for your daily routines, or do you strive to understand the deeper wisdom behind them? This week, pick one aspect of your deen that you have neglected and learn its deeper purpose, not just its surface practice.

Refutation of 'Abbad on Divine Power
Al-Razi refutes 'Abbad's claim that known events are necessary and not within power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Razi refutes 'Abbad's claim that divine knowledge of an event makes that event necessary and beyond divine power, by distinguishing between epistemic judgment and ontological modality, and by clarifying the causal order.

Core Idea
Divine knowledge follows reality; it does not create necessity in the thing known.

This passage presents Al-Razi's refutation of a theologian named 'Abbad, who argued that whatever God knows must happen becomes necessary and thus not within God's power. If taken to its conclusion, this would negate all divine power. Al-Razi offers several concise but powerful objections that defend both God's power and the sound relationship between knowledge and reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: 'Abbad's claim, then Al-Razi's answer starting with 'al-jawab'.
The marginal note is part of the passage and clarifies a key point.
2
Identify 'Abbad's claim
'Abbad claims that what God knows will happen is obligatory (wajib) and what He knows will not happen is impossible (mumtani'), and both are beyond power. Identify these terms in the Arabic.
3
First counter: elimination of all power
Al-Razi first argues that this would eliminate all divine power, since everything is either known to exist or not. Reflect on this logical consequence.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Second and third counters: possibility in itself and causal order
Next, Al-Razi states that the event is possible in itself, so it is within power. Also, knowledge follows occurrence, which follows power; the later does not invalidate the earlier. Understand the distinction between epistemic and ontological necessity.
The marginal note explains that the later does not necessitate the earlier either; causation moves forward.
5
Reflect on the marginal note
The marginal note clarifies that the later (knowledge) does not invalidate or necessitate the earlier (power). It emphasizes that causation flows from earlier to later. This reinforces the previous point.
This note is likely from a commentator, but it is included as part of the source.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما عباد فإنه زعم أن ما علم الله أنه يكون فهو واجب وما علم أنه لا يكون فهو ممتنع والواجب والمتنع غير مقدور والجواب: أن هذا يقتضى أن لا يكون لله تعالى مقدور أصلا لأن كل شيء فهو إما معلوم الوجود أو معلوم العدم ثم نقول إنه وإن كان واجبا نظراً إلى العلم لكنه ممكن في نفسه فكان مقدورا، ولأن العلم بالوقوع تبع الوقوع الذي هو تبع القدرة والمتأخر لا يبطل المتقدم (1). (1) أقول: المتأخر لا يبطل المتقدم لا يوجبه أيضا بل المتقدم هو الذي يوجب المتأخر إذا كان المتقدم بالعلية، وأصل هذا الجواب ما مر في المذهب المتقدم
As for 'Abbad, he claimed that what God knows will occur is obligatory, and what He knows will not occur is impossible, and the obligatory and the impossible are not within power. The answer: this necessitates that there is no power for God at all, because everything is either known to exist or known not to exist. Then we say: although it is obligatory with respect to knowledge, it is possible in itself, so it is within power. And because knowledge of occurrence follows occurrence which follows power, and the later does not invalidate the earlier (1). (1) I say: The later does not invalidate the earlier nor does it necessitate it; rather the earlier is what necessitates the later if the earlier is by causation. And the origin of this answer is what was mentioned in the preceding school.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage is a refutation. The author states 'Abbad's claim that whatever God knows will happen is necessary and whatever He knows will not happen is impossible; both categories being beyond God's power. Then the answer: (1) This would eliminate all divine power, since everything is either known to exist or known not to exist. (2) Although something is necessary relative to divine knowledge, in itself it is possible and thus within power. (3) Divine knowledge follows the actual occurrence, which follows power; the later (knowledge) does not invalidate the earlier (power). The marginal note clarifies that the later does not necessitate the earlier either; causation flows from the earlier to the later. The core distinction is between epistemic judgment and ontological modality.

Key Terms
علم
واجب
ممتنع
مقدور
تبع
متقدم
متأخر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That divine knowledge of an event makes that event necessary in itself, removing it from divine power.
Correction: Divine knowledge does not cause the event to be necessary in itself; the event is possible in itself, and knowledge merely reflects what will occur through power.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation of 'Abbad, why does 'Abbad's claim not eliminate divine power?
A) Because God's knowledge is not real knowledge.
B) Because the event is possible in itself despite being necessary in knowledge.
C) Because 'Abbad only meant moral necessity.
D) Because the event is impossible in itself.

Key Takeaway
Divine knowledge does not make events necessary in themselves; they remain possible and subject to divine power, and knowledge follows power in the causal order.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that because God knows everything you will do, your choices are meaningless and you are just acting out a script? How does Al-Razi's distinction between epistemic necessity and ontological possibility challenge that feeling and restore your sense of responsibility before God?
?
When you sin, do you secretly think, 'God knew I would do this, so it was inevitable'? If so, how does this lesson's correction—that knowledge follows power, not the other way—call you to repentance and a fresh reliance on God's power to change?
?
If God's power is truly prior to His knowledge (in causal order), then your every action is a moment where God's power makes it happen, and His knowledge simply registers it. Does this make you more aware of your dependence on Him in every moment? What would change in your daily life if you truly internalized that?

Refuting Al-Balkhi: God's Power Over the Servant's Acts
Al-Razi refutes Al-Balkhi's argument that God cannot do the like of the servant's action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi refutes Al-Balkhi's argument by distinguishing the intrinsic nature of an act from its accidental moral qualities.

Core Idea
Moral qualities are agent-dependent accidents; God can do the same act without them.

Al-Balkhi argued that because the servant's actions are either obedience, folly, or absurd, God cannot perform the like of them. Al-Razi's refutation hinges on a crucial distinction: what an act is in itself (e.g., motion) is separate from the moral labels that attach due to the agent. This lesson unpacks that distinction and its implications for divine omnipotence.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the structure: first Al-Balkhi's claim, then Al-Razi's answer.
2
Identify both arguments
Summarize Al-Balkhi's reasoning in one sentence, then summarize Al-Razi's reply.
Al-Balkhi says God cannot do the servant's act because it's either obedience, folly, or absurd. Al-Razi says the act's essence is neutral, and moral qualities are accidental.
3
Analyze the key distinction
Focus on the phrase 'أحوال عارضة' (accidental states). Why does this distinction matter for the argument?
The act in itself (e.g., movement) is the same; the moral label depends on the doer, so God can do the act without those labels.
4
Reflect on theological significance
Consider why this refutation is important for defending God's omnipotence and the concept of qadar (divine decree).
It shows that God's power includes the ability to do the same actions as humans, but without unworthy motives or consequences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما البلخي فقد زعم أن الله تعالى لا يقدر على مثل مقدور العبد لأن مقدور العبد إما طاعة أو سفه أو عبث وذلك على الله محال. والجواب: أن الفعل في نفسه حركة أو سكون مثلا وكونه طاعة وسفها أو عبثا أحوال عارضة له من حيث كونه صادرا عن العبد والله تعالى قادر على مثل ذات ذلك الفعل
As for Al-Balkhi, he claimed that God is not capable of the like of the servant's action because the servant's action is either obedience, folly, or absurd, and that is impossible for God. The answer: the act in itself is, for example, movement or stillness, and its being obedience, folly, or absurd are accidental states that attach to it by virtue of its originating from the servant. God is capable of the like of the essence of that act.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage is a refutation. Al-Balkhi argues that God cannot perform an act similar to the servant's act because the servant's act is either obedience, folly, or absurd, which are unworthy of God. The answer distinguishes between the act's intrinsic nature (e.g., motion or rest) and its moral quality (obedience, folly, absurdity). The moral quality is accidental, arising from the agent (the servant). God, as a different agent, can perform the same intrinsic act without those accidental qualities. Thus God is capable of the essence of the act.

Key Terms

عبث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The moral quality of an act (obedience, folly, absurd) is intrinsic to the act itself, so God cannot perform any act that a human can perform if that act is classified as disobedience or folly.
Correction: The moral quality is accidental and agent-dependent. An act's essence (e.g., movement) is neutral; its moral value depends on the intention and context of the doer. God can perform the same essence without the moral quality.

Checkpoint
How does al-Razi refute Al-Balkhi's argument?
A) By denying that the servant's actions are obedience, folly, or absurd.
B) By distinguishing the act's essence from its accidental moral qualities.
C) By claiming God cannot do any action at all.
D) By arguing that God's power is limited to spiritual acts.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi shows that the moral labels of actions depend on the agent, not the act itself, so God can perform the same essential acts without unworthy qualities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see someone commit a sin, do you immediately label the act as evil in itself, forgetting that its moral weight depends on the agent's intention and circumstances? How can this lesson help you avoid judging others unfairly?
?
God can perform the same physical action as a human without any moral deficiency. Does this truth comfort you or challenge your assumptions about divine justice? How does it affect your understanding of God's actions in your life, even those that seem difficult?
?
If the moral quality of your actions depends on your intention and relationship with God, not just the outward act, then what does this lesson demand you examine in your own daily movements and stillness? Will you commit to pausing before each action to consider its inner quality?

Refutation of Shared Power: God's Power Over the Servant's Action
Al-Razi refutes the claim that God cannot act on the very same action as the servant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the claim that God cannot act on the same action as the servant and al-Razi's refutation based on independent causes.

Core Idea
An action can be attributed to two agents alternatively, not simultaneously under the same attribution.

Al-Razi takes on a key Mu'tazilite objection to the Ash'ari view of divine power. Abu Ali and Abu Hashim argued that if God and the servant both have power over the same action, conflicting wills would make the action impossible. Al-Razi responds by appealing to the principle of independent causes and the role of attribution.

Guided Path
1
Understand the opposing claim
Read the opening sentence to grasp the position of Abu Ali and Abu Hashim: God can create a similar action but not the exact same action.
Notice the key terms 'مقدور' (object of power) and 'دواعي' (motives).
2
Identify the proposed contradiction
Examine their reasoning: if the same action were under both powers, God's will to create and the servant's will to avoid would conflict, leading to logical impossibility.
The contradiction arises from assuming that motives and repelling factors are absolute.
3
Analyze al-Razi's refutation
Al-Razi denies that a repelling factor (صارف) always prevents existence; it only does so if no independent cause (سبب مستقل) intervenes. God's will is such an independent cause.
This answer preserves God's all-powerfulness while acknowledging the servant's power.
4
Grasp the marginal note on attribution
The marginal note clarifies that an action can be shared only when un-attributed; once attributed to one agent, it cannot be shared under that aspect. The essence can be shared alternatively.
Key concept: 'اشتراك' (sharing) and 'إضافة' (attribution).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما أبو علي وأبو هاشم وأتباعهما فقد زعموا أن الله تعالى قادر على مثل مقدور العبد لكنه غير قادر على نفس مقدوره، لأن المقدور من شأنه أن يوجد عند توفر دواعي القادر وأن يبقى على العدم عند توفر صارفه فلو كان مقدور العبد مقدورا لله تعالى لكان إذا أراد الله تعالى وقوعه وكره العبد وقوعه يلزم أن يوجد لتحقق الداعي وأن لا يوجد لتحقق الصارف وهو والجواب: أن البقاء على العدم عند تحقق الصارف ممنوع مطلقا بل ذلك إنما يجب إذا لم يقم مقامه سبب آخر مستقل، وهذا أول المسألة (2). (2) أقول: إنما يمكن كون المقدور مشتركا إذا أخذ غير مضاف إلى أحدهما بعد الإضافة إلى أحدهما امتنع الاشتراك فيه من حيث تلك الإضافة والمقدور غير المصاف ممكن إضافته إلى كل واحد على سبيل البدل، والمراد من كون مقدور أحدهما مقدور الآخر
As for Abu Ali and Abu Hashim and their followers, they claimed that God is capable of the like of the servant's action but not capable of the very same action, because the object of power is such that it exists when the motives of the powerful agent are present, and remains non-existent when the repelling factor is present. So if the servant's object of power were God's object of power, then if God willed its occurrence and the servant disliked its occurrence, it would necessitate that it exist because the motive is realized, and that it not exist because the repelling factor is realized. The answer: staying non-existent when the repelling factor is present is not absolutely necessary; rather that is only necessary if no other independent cause takes its place. This is the beginning of the issue (2). (2) I say: It is possible for the object of power to be shared only if it is taken without being attributed to one of them; after attribution to one of them, sharing is impossible in that attribution. The un-attributed object of power can be attributed to each in an alternative manner, and the meaning of 'one's object of power being the other's object of power' is that.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage is a refutation. Abu Ali and Abu Hashim argue that God can do a similar action to the servant's but not the exact same action, because the servant's action depends on his motives and repelling factors. If the same action were within both God's and the servant's power, then if God wills it and the servant does not, there would be contradiction (existence from God's will, non-existence from the servant's repelling factor). The answer denies that the repelling factor necessarily prevents existence; an independent cause (God's will) can supersede it. The marginal note adds that an action can be shared if unspecified; once specified as 'servant's action', it is uniquely attributed, but the essence can be shared in the alternative.

Key Terms
مقدور
دواعي
صارف
سبب مستقل
اشتراك
إضافة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An action cannot be simultaneously willed by two agents with conflicting intentions.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that an independent cause (God's will) can override a repelling factor, so the same action can be within two powers when considered without specific attribution.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi's refutation, why does the presence of a repelling factor in the servant not prevent God from creating the same action?
A) Because God's will overrides the servant's will.
B) Because the repelling factor is not effective when an independent cause exists.
C) Because the servant has no real power.
D) Because the action is impossible by nature.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi argues that an independent cause (God's will) can override a repelling factor, so the same action can be within two powers when considered without attribution.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you truly believe that God's will can override all your desires and aversions? When you face a situation where your heart is torn between what you want and what God commands, which 'cause' do you treat as independent and decisive?
?
Al-Razi insists that God's power acts on the very same action you perform. Does this thought make you more humble about your own achievements, or does it unsettle you? Are you willing to attribute every success and failure to God's ultimate control, even when it challenges your sense of autonomy?
?
The marginal note says an action can be 'shared' between you and God only when it is not attributed to you. When you take credit for a good deed, are you attributing it exclusively to yourself, thereby denying God's share? How can you shift your internal attribution without falling into fatalism?

The Dispute and the Majority Argument
Opening statement of the issue and the majority's proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two opposing positions on knowing God's essence and the majority's proof that knowing His existence entails knowing His essence.

Core Idea
Knowing God's existence means knowing His essence, because they are identical.

The question of whether we can know the reality of God's essence divides theologians. Some say yes, others no. This lesson presents the basic dispute and the central proof from the majority, which hinges on the identity of existence and essence in God.

Guided Path
1
Read the two positions
Identify who holds each view: Dirar and al-Ghazali (with philosophers) deny knowledge; the majority (Ash'aris and Mu'tazila) affirm it.
Note that 'majority' includes both Ash'aris and Mu'tazila here.
2
Examine the majority's proof
The proof states we know God's existence, and existence is identical to His essence, so we must know the essence. Rejecting this leads to contradiction: one thing would be both known and unknown.
This argument assumes existence and essence are one in God, not in created things.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider what 'knowing the essence' means here—the majority claim we know it in some way, not necessarily comprehensively.
This nuance is often misunderstood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : ذهب ضرار من المتكلمين والغزالي من المآخرين إلى أنا لا نعرف حقيقة ذات الله تعالى وهو قول الحكماء) وذهب جمهور المتكلمين منا ومن المعتزلة إلى أنها معلومة . حجة المتكلمين منا ومن المعتزلة أنا نعرف وجوده ووجوده عين ذاته فلا بد وأن نعلم ذاته ، وإلا لكان الشيء الواحد بالاعتبار الواحد معلوما مجهولا . حجة الفريق الثانى من وجهين الأول : أما المعلوم عندنا منه سبحانه ، إما السالب كقولنا ليس مجسم ولا جوهر ولا عرض ، ولا شك أن الماهية مغايرة لسلب ما عداها عنها ، وإما الإضافات كقولنا قادر عالم فلاشك أن الماهية مغايرة لهذه الإضافات لأن المعلوم عندنا من قدرة الله تعالى أنه أمر مستلزم التأثير فى الفعل على سبيل الصحة فماهية القدرة مجهولة والمعلوم ليس إلا هذا اللازم وهو التأثير المخصوص ، وكذلك المعلوم عندنا من علم الله تعالى ليس إلا أنه أمر يازمه الإحكام والإتقان فى الفعل فراهية ذلك العلم غير هذا الأثر ، والمعلوم ليس إلا هذا الأثر ، فظهر أن ماهية صفات الله تعالى غير معلومة لنا ، وبتقدير أن تكون معلومة لكن العلم بالصفة لا يستلزم العلم بماهية الموصوف على التفصيل ، ولمادل الاستقراء على سبيل الإنصاف أنا لا نعلم من الله تعالى إلا المسلوب والإضافات، وثبت أن العلم بهما لا يستلزم العلم بالماهية ثبت أنا لا نعلم حقيقة الله تعالى الثاني : أنا قد بينا فى أول هذا الكتاب أنه لا يمكننا أن نتصور شيئا إلا الذى ندركه بحواسنا أو نجده من نفوسنا أو نتصوره من عقولنا أو ما يتركب عن أحد هذه الثلاثة ، فالماهية الإلهية خارجة عن هذه الاقسام الثلاثة فهي غير معلومة لنا (۲) أقول : مثبتو الحال القائلون بأن العالمية صفة لا يقولون أن العلم صفة بل يقولون العالمية معللة بالعلم والعلم معنى فلا يزيدون على صفة واحدة من باب العلم وكذلك في سائر الصفات، والذين يقولون بالصفات الزائدة لا يقولون إن إثبات الصفات يكون من جهة الأفعال أو التنزيه فقط ، بل يقولون السمع أيضاً طريق آخر في أمثالها وإنما أثبتوها لورود النص بها وكونها غير مرادفة لسائر الصفات . (۲) أقول : القول بأن المعلوم منه تعالى أما اللوب والإضافات ليس بمسلم عند المتكلمين ، لأنهم يقولون وجود الله تعالى معلوم وليس هو صفة سلبية ولا إضافية والحكماء يقولون فى الجواب عنه إن الموجود المعلوم هو المشترك الذي يحمل عليه تعالى وعلى غيره لا بالسواء بل بالتشكيك والموضوع بهذا المحمول هو حقيقته تعالى الواجب وجودها لذاته التى لا يعبر عنها إلا يوصف سلى أو إضافى ، فيقال مثلا الوجود القائم بذاته الذي ليس يعارض الماهية ولو نعتا هذا هو الأمر المشترك المقارب المقارن الأسلوب ، أو تلك الحقيقة فغير معلومة لغيره تعالى
Issue: Dirar among the mutakallimun and al-Ghazali among the later scholars held that we do not know the reality of the essence of God, and that is the view of the philosophers. The majority of the mutakallimun, both from us (Ash'aris) and from the Mu'tazila, held that it is known. The proof of the mutakallimun from us and the Mu'tazila: we know His existence, and His existence is identical to His essence, so necessarily we know His essence; otherwise one thing under one consideration would be known and unknown. The proof of the second group is from two aspects: First: what is known to us of Him, exalted, is either negations (as-salib), like our saying 'He is not a body, not a substance, not an accident'—and there is no doubt that the quiddity (al-mahiya) is other than the negation of what is other than it—or relations (al-idafat), like our saying 'powerful, knowing'—and there is no doubt that the quiddity is other than these relations, because what is known to us of God's power is that it is an entailing influence upon action in the manner of validity; so the quiddity of power is unknown, and what is known is only this necessary consequent, which is the specific influence. Likewise, what is known to us of God's knowledge is only that it is a thing that necessitates perfection and precision in action; so the reality of that knowledge is other than this effect, and what is known is only this effect. Thus it is clear that the quiddity of God's attributes is unknown to us, and even if it were known, knowledge of the attribute does not entail detailed knowledge of the quiddity of the described. Since induction (al-istiqra') fairly indicates that we know of God only negations and relations, and it is established that knowledge of them does not entail knowledge of the quiddity, it is established that we do not know the reality of God. Second: We have shown at the beginning of this book that we cannot conceive anything except what we perceive by our senses, or find in our souls, or conceive from our intellects, or what is composed of one of these three. The divine quiddity is outside these three divisions, so it is unknown to us. (2) I say: The affirmers of the state (muthbitū al-ḥāl) who say that 'being knowing' is an attribute do not say that knowledge is an attribute; rather, they say that 'being knowing' is caused by knowledge, and knowledge is a meaning, so they do not add more than one attribute in the category of knowledge, and similarly for other attributes. Those who affirm additional attributes do not say that the affirmation of attributes comes only from actions or transcendence; rather, they say that audition (al-sam') is also another path in similar cases, and they only affirmed them due to the textual evidence and their being non-synonymous with other attributes. (2) I say: The statement that what is known of Him, exalted, is only negations and relations is not conceded by the mutakallimun, because they say that the existence of God is known, and it is neither a negative nor a relational attribute. The philosophers say in response that the known existent is the common term that applies to Him and to others not univocally but by analogy (tashkīk); and the subject of this predicate is His reality, the necessary existence in itself, which is only expressed by a negative or relational description. For example, 'existence subsisting by itself which does not oppose quiddity' – even if this is a description, it is a common concept, or that reality is unknown to other than Him, exalted.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage is a theological dispute on whether we can know the essence (dhāt) of God. It begins by stating two positions: (1) Dirar and al-Ghazali, along with the philosophers, deny knowledge of God's essence; (2) the majority of mutakallimun (Ash'aris and Mu'tazila) affirm it. The majority's proof: we know God's existence, and existence is identical to His essence (for those who hold that existence and essence are one in God), so knowing existence entails knowing essence. Otherwise, one thing would be both known and unknown under the same consideration, which is contradictory. The opposing group (the deniers) offer two proofs. First proof: what we know of God is either negations (e.g., 'He is not a body') or relations (e.g., 'He is powerful, knowing'). Negations do not inform us of the quiddity, and relations only tell us about effects, not the quiddity of the attributes themselves. Induction shows we only know negations and relations, and that does not entail knowledge of the essence. Second proof: all human conceptions derive from senses, introspection, or intellect, or combinations thereof. The divine essence is outside these categories, hence unknown. The teacher should note that the text then includes two responses introduced by 'أقول' (I say). The first response explains that those who affirm a 'state' (ḥāl) do not multiply attributes unnecessarily, while those who affirm additional attributes also rely on revelation (sam'), not just reason. The second response argues that the claim that we only know negations and relations is rejected because existence itself is known and is neither a negation nor a relation; the philosophers counter that existence is a common analogical term and the reality remains unknown to others. This commentary section is dense and represents a debate between different schools, so each nuance should be unpacked.

Key Terms
ذات
ماهية
وجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The majority claim we know God's essence comprehensively in all details.
Correction: The passage only asserts that we know the essence in some way (through existence). The deniers' objection also shows that detailed knowledge is not claimed.

Checkpoint
What is the core premise of the majority's proof?
A) God's essence is known through creation
B) Existence and essence are identical in God
C) We know God's attributes, not essence
D) Reason cannot know God

Key Takeaway
The majority of theologians affirm knowledge of God's essence by equating it with His existence, which we know.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to know God through His existence. But do you live each day with the awareness that His essence—His very self—is not hidden from you? What area of your life would change if you truly believed you already know Him intimately?
?
If knowing God's essence is tied to knowing His existence, then your worship should reflect certainty. Examine your heart: do you sometimes act as if God is a distant, unknown entity? How can you use this proof to strengthen your conviction that He is knowable?
?
The majority's argument depends on the identity of existence and essence. Does your daily acknowledgment of God's oneness (tawhid) truly reflect that He is simple, with no parts or additions? Confess honestly: do you ever subconsciously imagine God as having a 'hidden essence' separate from His known attributes?

The Deniers' First Proof: Negations and Relations
First proof of the deniers: knowledge limited to negations and relations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the deniers' argument that what we know of God are only negations and relations, not the quiddity of His essence or attributes.

Core Idea
We only know negations and relations about God, not His real quiddity.

The deniers of knowing God's essence offer two proofs. The first examines what we actually know about God. It argues that our knowledge is limited to negative statements (what God is not) and relational descriptions (what God does). Since these do not reveal the inner reality of either the attributes or the essence, we cannot claim to know God's essence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of what we know
The passage divides our knowledge of God into negations (as-salib) and relations (al-idafat). Negations are like 'He is not a body'; relations are like 'He is powerful' (power implies relation to action).
These are the only kinds of knowledge we have about God, according to the deniers.
2
Understand why negations don't reveal quiddity
Negations tell us what God is not, not what He is. The quiddity (mahiya) is distinct from the negation of what is other than it.
3
Understand why relations don't reveal quiddity
Relational attributes like power and knowledge are known only by their effects (influence on action, perfection in act). The quiddity of the attribute itself remains unknown. Similarly, knowing attributes doesn't entail knowing the essence of the described.
For example, we know God's power only as 'entailing influence on action' but not what power is in itself.
4
Follow the conclusion
Since induction shows we only know negations and relations, and knowledge of them does not entail knowledge of quiddity, we do not know the reality of God.
This is a direct challenge to the majority position.

مسألة : ذهب ضرار من المتكلمين والغزالي من المآخرين إلى أنا لا نعرف حقيقة ذات الله تعالى وهو قول الحكماء) وذهب جمهور المتكلمين منا ومن المعتزلة إلى أنها معلومة . حجة المتكلمين منا ومن المعتزلة أنا نعرف وجوده ووجوده عين ذاته فلا بد وأن نعلم ذاته ، وإلا لكان الشيء الواحد بالاعتبار الواحد معلوما مجهولا . حجة الفريق الثانى من وجهين الأول : أما المعلوم عندنا منه سبحانه ، إما السالب كقولنا ليس مجسم ولا جوهر ولا عرض ، ولا شك أن الماهية مغايرة لسلب ما عداها عنها ، وإما الإضافات كقولنا قادر عالم فلاشك أن الماهية مغايرة لهذه الإضافات لأن المعلوم عندنا من قدرة الله تعالى أنه أمر مستلزم التأثير فى الفعل على سبيل الصحة فماهية القدرة مجهولة والمعلوم ليس إلا هذا اللازم وهو التأثير المخصوص ، وكذلك المعلوم عندنا من علم الله تعالى ليس إلا أنه أمر يازمه الإحكام والإتقان فى الفعل فراهية ذلك العلم غير هذا الأثر ، والمعلوم ليس إلا هذا الأثر ، فظهر أن ماهية صفات الله تعالى غير معلومة لنا ، وبتقدير أن تكون معلومة لكن العلم بالصفة لا يستلزم العلم بماهية الموصوف على التفصيل ، ولمادل الاستقراء على سبيل الإنصاف أنا لا نعلم من الله تعالى إلا المسلوب والإضافات، وثبت أن العلم بهما لا يستلزم العلم بالماهية ثبت أنا لا نعلم حقيقة الله تعالى الثاني : أنا قد بينا فى أول هذا الكتاب أنه لا يمكننا أن نتصور شيئا إلا الذى ندركه بحواسنا أو نجده من نفوسنا أو نتصوره من عقولنا أو ما يتركب عن أحد هذه الثلاثة ، فالماهية الإلهية خارجة عن هذه الاقسام الثلاثة فهي غير معلومة لنا (۲) أقول : مثبتو الحال القائلون بأن العالمية صفة لا يقولون أن العلم صفة بل يقولون العالمية معللة بالعلم والعلم معنى فلا يزيدون على صفة واحدة من باب العلم وكذلك في سائر الصفات، والذين يقولون بالصفات الزائدة لا يقولون إن إثبات الصفات يكون من جهة الأفعال أو التنزيه فقط ، بل يقولون السمع أيضاً طريق آخر في أمثالها وإنما أثبتوها لورود النص بها وكونها غير مرادفة لسائر الصفات . (۲) أقول : القول بأن المعلوم منه تعالى أما اللوب والإضافات ليس بمسلم عند المتكلمين ، لأنهم يقولون وجود الله تعالى معلوم وليس هو صفة سلبية ولا إضافية والحكماء يقولون فى الجواب عنه إن الموجود المعلوم هو المشترك الذي يحمل عليه تعالى وعلى غيره لا بالسواء بل بالتشكيك والموضوع بهذا المحمول هو حقيقته تعالى الواجب وجودها لذاته التى لا يعبر عنها إلا يوصف سلى أو إضافى ، فيقال مثلا الوجود القائم بذاته الذي ليس يعارض الماهية ولو نعتا هذا هو الأمر المشترك المقارب المقارن الأسلوب ، أو تلك الحقيقة فغير معلومة لغيره تعالى
Issue: Dirar among the mutakallimun and al-Ghazali among the later scholars held that we do not know the reality of the essence of God, and that is the view of the philosophers. The majority of the mutakallimun, both from us (Ash'aris) and from the Mu'tazila, held that it is known. The proof of the mutakallimun from us and the Mu'tazila: we know His existence, and His existence is identical to His essence, so necessarily we know His essence; otherwise one thing under one consideration would be known and unknown. The proof of the second group is from two aspects: First: what is known to us of Him, exalted, is either negations (as-salib), like our saying 'He is not a body, not a substance, not an accident'—and there is no doubt that the quiddity (al-mahiya) is other than the negation of what is other than it—or relations (al-idafat), like our saying 'powerful, knowing'—and there is no doubt that the quiddity is other than these relations, because what is known to us of God's power is that it is an entailing influence upon action in the manner of validity; so the quiddity of power is unknown, and what is known is only this necessary consequent, which is the specific influence. Likewise, what is known to us of God's knowledge is only that it is a thing that necessitates perfection and precision in action; so the reality of that knowledge is other than this effect, and what is known is only this effect. Thus it is clear that the quiddity of God's attributes is unknown to us, and even if it were known, knowledge of the attribute does not entail detailed knowledge of the quiddity of the described. Since induction (al-istiqra') fairly indicates that we know of God only negations and relations, and it is established that knowledge of them does not entail knowledge of the quiddity, it is established that we do not know the reality of God. Second: We have shown at the beginning of this book that we cannot conceive anything except what we perceive by our senses, or find in our souls, or conceive from our intellects, or what is composed of one of these three. The divine quiddity is outside these three divisions, so it is unknown to us. (2) I say: The affirmers of the state (muthbitū al-ḥāl) who say that 'being knowing' is an attribute do not say that knowledge is an attribute; rather, they say that 'being knowing' is caused by knowledge, and knowledge is a meaning, so they do not add more than one attribute in the category of knowledge, and similarly for other attributes. Those who affirm additional attributes do not say that the affirmation of attributes comes only from actions or transcendence; rather, they say that audition (al-sam') is also another path in similar cases, and they only affirmed them due to the textual evidence and their being non-synonymous with other attributes. (2) I say: The statement that what is known of Him, exalted, is only negations and relations is not conceded by the mutakallimun, because they say that the existence of God is known, and it is neither a negative nor a relational attribute. The philosophers say in response that the known existent is the common term that applies to Him and to others not univocally but by analogy (tashkīk); and the subject of this predicate is His reality, the necessary existence in itself, which is only expressed by a negative or relational description. For example, 'existence subsisting by itself which does not oppose quiddity' – even if this is a description, it is a common concept, or that reality is unknown to other than Him, exalted.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage is a theological dispute on whether we can know the essence (dhāt) of God. It begins by stating two positions: (1) Dirar and al-Ghazali, along with the philosophers, deny knowledge of God's essence; (2) the majority of mutakallimun (Ash'aris and Mu'tazila) affirm it. The majority's proof: we know God's existence, and existence is identical to His essence (for those who hold that existence and essence are one in God), so knowing existence entails knowing essence. Otherwise, one thing would be both known and unknown under the same consideration, which is contradictory. The opposing group (the deniers) offer two proofs. First proof: what we know of God is either negations (e.g., 'He is not a body') or relations (e.g., 'He is powerful, knowing'). Negations do not inform us of the quiddity, and relations only tell us about effects, not the quiddity of the attributes themselves. Induction shows we only know negations and relations, and that does not entail knowledge of the essence. Second proof: all human conceptions derive from senses, introspection, or intellect, or combinations thereof. The divine essence is outside these categories, hence unknown. The teacher should note that the text then includes two responses introduced by 'أقول' (I say). The first response explains that those who affirm a 'state' (ḥāl) do not multiply attributes unnecessarily, while those who affirm additional attributes also rely on revelation (sam'), not just reason. The second response argues that the claim that we only know negations and relations is rejected because existence itself is known and is neither a negation nor a relation; the philosophers counter that existence is a common analogical term and the reality remains unknown to others. This commentary section is dense and represents a debate between different schools, so each nuance should be unpacked.

Key Terms

الإضافات
ماهية
قادر
عالم
التأثير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that knowing God is powerful gives direct knowledge of His essence.
Correction: The passage argues that knowing 'powerful' only tells us about an effect (influence on action), not the quiddity of power itself, let alone the essence.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, why do relational attributes (like power) not reveal the quiddity of God?
A) Because they are known only by their effects, not their essence
B) Because they are not real attributes
C) Because they are identical to the essence
D) Because they are negative statements

Key Takeaway
The deniers' first proof argues that our knowledge of God is confined to negations and relations, which do not reveal the quiddity of His attributes or essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that we cannot know the quiddity of God or His attributes. How does this affect the way you have been describing God in your heart? Have you been presuming to know more than you actually do, and what will you now do to correct that presumption?
?
If all your knowledge of God is limited to negations and relations, what does that say about the certainty of your statements about His essence? What one step can you take today to humbly acknowledge this limit before Him?
?
Even the attributes you affirm (like mercy) are known only by their effects in your life. Does your attachment to those effects reveal a deeper longing for the unknown reality of God Himself? What is one concrete action you can take this week to seek that reality more earnestly, while accepting that its quiddity remains hidden?

Allah's Existence
intermediate
6 min
The Deniers' Second Proof: Limits of Human Conception
Second proof of the deniers: epistemological limits preclude knowledge of God's essence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the epistemological argument that all human conceptions come from senses, introspection, or intellect, and that the divine essence lies beyond these categories.

Core Idea
The divine essence is outside the three sources of human conception.

The second proof from the deniers shifts to epistemology: how do we form concepts? Everything we can conceive comes from sensory perception, inner experience, or intellectual deduction (or combinations). The divine essence does not fall into any of these, so it is unknowable.

Guided Path
1
Understand the epistemological framework
The passage asserts a trichotomy: we conceive things either through senses (external), introspection (internal), or intellect (a priori). Everything is reducible to these or their compounds.
This is a common theory of concept formation in Islamic philosophy and theology.
2
Identify where the divine essence falls
The divine essence is not perceivable by senses, not an inner state, and not an intellectual abstraction. Therefore it is outside the three categories.
The deniers argue it is thus necessarily unknown.
3
Connect to the overall argument
This second proof complements the first by showing that even in principle, we cannot form a concept of God's essence.

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, what are the three sources of human conception?
A) Intellect, revelation, and senses
B) Senses, soul (introspection), and intellect
C) Reason, experience, and testimony
D) Senses, imagination, and intuition

Key Takeaway
The divine essence lies outside the three categories of human concept formation, so it cannot be known.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to know God, but does your conception of Him come from your senses, your feelings, or your intellect? Which of these is inadequate, and why? Will you let the fact that God's essence is beyond your conception lead you to awe and submission, or to prideful disregard?
?
If the very essence of God is beyond your grasp, what does that mean for your prayer when you try to address Him? Are you comfortable with a God you cannot fully conceive, or does that discomfort reveal a hidden desire to limit Him to your own understanding?
?
The deniers argue that the divine essence is unknown. You might dismiss this as sophistry, but have you ever actually tried to conceive of God's essence? Did you succeed? Let that failure humble you before the One who is beyond all thought, and commit to worshipping Him as He deserves, not as you imagine.

Responses to the Deniers: Defending Knowledge of God
Two responses from the author defending the possibility of knowing God

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two responses to the deniers: one clarifying the nature of attributes, and another arguing that existence is known and not a negation or relation.

Core Idea
We know God's existence, which is neither negation nor relation, and attributes are affirmed through revelation.

The author (Imam Razi) offers two responses to the deniers. The first clarifies that not all theologians multiply attributes unnecessarily; some see attributes as states (ahwal). The second challenges the claim that we only know negations and relations, pointing out that existence is known and is neither. The philosophers' counter-response is also mentioned.

Guided Path
1
Understand the first response
The author notes that some theologians (muthbitū al-ḥāl) do not posit additional attributes; they say 'being knowing' is caused by knowledge, which is a meaning. Others who affirm additional attributes rely on revelation (sam') as a source, not just reason.
This response addresses possible confusion about attribute theories.
2
Understand the second response
The claim that we only know negations and relations is rejected because existence is known and is neither. The philosophers respond that existence is a common analogical term; the reality behind it remains unknown.
The second response is a debate between mutakallimun and philosophers.
3
Reflect on the complexity
These responses show the issue is not settled; different schools have different positions on what can be known.

مسألة : ذهب ضرار من المتكلمين والغزالي من المآخرين إلى أنا لا نعرف حقيقة ذات الله تعالى وهو قول الحكماء) وذهب جمهور المتكلمين منا ومن المعتزلة إلى أنها معلومة . حجة المتكلمين منا ومن المعتزلة أنا نعرف وجوده ووجوده عين ذاته فلا بد وأن نعلم ذاته ، وإلا لكان الشيء الواحد بالاعتبار الواحد معلوما مجهولا . حجة الفريق الثانى من وجهين الأول : أما المعلوم عندنا منه سبحانه ، إما السالب كقولنا ليس مجسم ولا جوهر ولا عرض ، ولا شك أن الماهية مغايرة لسلب ما عداها عنها ، وإما الإضافات كقولنا قادر عالم فلاشك أن الماهية مغايرة لهذه الإضافات لأن المعلوم عندنا من قدرة الله تعالى أنه أمر مستلزم التأثير فى الفعل على سبيل الصحة فماهية القدرة مجهولة والمعلوم ليس إلا هذا اللازم وهو التأثير المخصوص ، وكذلك المعلوم عندنا من علم الله تعالى ليس إلا أنه أمر يازمه الإحكام والإتقان فى الفعل فراهية ذلك العلم غير هذا الأثر ، والمعلوم ليس إلا هذا الأثر ، فظهر أن ماهية صفات الله تعالى غير معلومة لنا ، وبتقدير أن تكون معلومة لكن العلم بالصفة لا يستلزم العلم بماهية الموصوف على التفصيل ، ولمادل الاستقراء على سبيل الإنصاف أنا لا نعلم من الله تعالى إلا المسلوب والإضافات، وثبت أن العلم بهما لا يستلزم العلم بالماهية ثبت أنا لا نعلم حقيقة الله تعالى الثاني : أنا قد بينا فى أول هذا الكتاب أنه لا يمكننا أن نتصور شيئا إلا الذى ندركه بحواسنا أو نجده من نفوسنا أو نتصوره من عقولنا أو ما يتركب عن أحد هذه الثلاثة ، فالماهية الإلهية خارجة عن هذه الاقسام الثلاثة فهي غير معلومة لنا (۲) أقول : مثبتو الحال القائلون بأن العالمية صفة لا يقولون أن العلم صفة بل يقولون العالمية معللة بالعلم والعلم معنى فلا يزيدون على صفة واحدة من باب العلم وكذلك في سائر الصفات، والذين يقولون بالصفات الزائدة لا يقولون إن إثبات الصفات يكون من جهة الأفعال أو التنزيه فقط ، بل يقولون السمع أيضاً طريق آخر في أمثالها وإنما أثبتوها لورود النص بها وكونها غير مرادفة لسائر الصفات . (۲) أقول : القول بأن المعلوم منه تعالى أما اللوب والإضافات ليس بمسلم عند المتكلمين ، لأنهم يقولون وجود الله تعالى معلوم وليس هو صفة سلبية ولا إضافية والحكماء يقولون فى الجواب عنه إن الموجود المعلوم هو المشترك الذي يحمل عليه تعالى وعلى غيره لا بالسواء بل بالتشكيك والموضوع بهذا المحمول هو حقيقته تعالى الواجب وجودها لذاته التى لا يعبر عنها إلا يوصف سلى أو إضافى ، فيقال مثلا الوجود القائم بذاته الذي ليس يعارض الماهية ولو نعتا هذا هو الأمر المشترك المقارب المقارن الأسلوب ، أو تلك الحقيقة فغير معلومة لغيره تعالى
Issue: Dirar among the mutakallimun and al-Ghazali among the later scholars held that we do not know the reality of the essence of God, and that is the view of the philosophers. The majority of the mutakallimun, both from us (Ash'aris) and from the Mu'tazila, held that it is known. The proof of the mutakallimun from us and the Mu'tazila: we know His existence, and His existence is identical to His essence, so necessarily we know His essence; otherwise one thing under one consideration would be known and unknown. The proof of the second group is from two aspects: First: what is known to us of Him, exalted, is either negations (as-salib), like our saying 'He is not a body, not a substance, not an accident'—and there is no doubt that the quiddity (al-mahiya) is other than the negation of what is other than it—or relations (al-idafat), like our saying 'powerful, knowing'—and there is no doubt that the quiddity is other than these relations, because what is known to us of God's power is that it is an entailing influence upon action in the manner of validity; so the quiddity of power is unknown, and what is known is only this necessary consequent, which is the specific influence. Likewise, what is known to us of God's knowledge is only that it is a thing that necessitates perfection and precision in action; so the reality of that knowledge is other than this effect, and what is known is only this effect. Thus it is clear that the quiddity of God's attributes is unknown to us, and even if it were known, knowledge of the attribute does not entail detailed knowledge of the quiddity of the described. Since induction (al-istiqra') fairly indicates that we know of God only negations and relations, and it is established that knowledge of them does not entail knowledge of the quiddity, it is established that we do not know the reality of God. Second: We have shown at the beginning of this book that we cannot conceive anything except what we perceive by our senses, or find in our souls, or conceive from our intellects, or what is composed of one of these three. The divine quiddity is outside these three divisions, so it is unknown to us. (2) I say: The affirmers of the state (muthbitū al-ḥāl) who say that 'being knowing' is an attribute do not say that knowledge is an attribute; rather, they say that 'being knowing' is caused by knowledge, and knowledge is a meaning, so they do not add more than one attribute in the category of knowledge, and similarly for other attributes. Those who affirm additional attributes do not say that the affirmation of attributes comes only from actions or transcendence; rather, they say that audition (al-sam') is also another path in similar cases, and they only affirmed them due to the textual evidence and their being non-synonymous with other attributes. (2) I say: The statement that what is known of Him, exalted, is only negations and relations is not conceded by the mutakallimun, because they say that the existence of God is known, and it is neither a negative nor a relational attribute. The philosophers say in response that the known existent is the common term that applies to Him and to others not univocally but by analogy (tashkīk); and the subject of this predicate is His reality, the necessary existence in itself, which is only expressed by a negative or relational description. For example, 'existence subsisting by itself which does not oppose quiddity' – even if this is a description, it is a common concept, or that reality is unknown to other than Him, exalted.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage is a theological dispute on whether we can know the essence (dhāt) of God. It begins by stating two positions: (1) Dirar and al-Ghazali, along with the philosophers, deny knowledge of God's essence; (2) the majority of mutakallimun (Ash'aris and Mu'tazila) affirm it. The majority's proof: we know God's existence, and existence is identical to His essence (for those who hold that existence and essence are one in God), so knowing existence entails knowing essence. Otherwise, one thing would be both known and unknown under the same consideration, which is contradictory. The opposing group (the deniers) offer two proofs. First proof: what we know of God is either negations (e.g., 'He is not a body') or relations (e.g., 'He is powerful, knowing'). Negations do not inform us of the quiddity, and relations only tell us about effects, not the quiddity of the attributes themselves. Induction shows we only know negations and relations, and that does not entail knowledge of the essence. Second proof: all human conceptions derive from senses, introspection, or intellect, or combinations thereof. The divine essence is outside these categories, hence unknown. The teacher should note that the text then includes two responses introduced by 'أقول' (I say). The first response explains that those who affirm a 'state' (ḥāl) do not multiply attributes unnecessarily, while those who affirm additional attributes also rely on revelation (sam'), not just reason. The second response argues that the claim that we only know negations and relations is rejected because existence itself is known and is neither a negation nor a relation; the philosophers counter that existence is a common analogical term and the reality remains unknown to others. This commentary section is dense and represents a debate between different schools, so each nuance should be unpacked.

الصفات الزائدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All theologians who affirm attributes are multiplying attributes without need.
Correction: The first response shows that some (muthbitū al-ḥāl) do not multiply attributes; they use a single meaning for each category.

Checkpoint
What is the mutakallimun's objection in the second response?
A) Attributes are known through revelation
B) The existence of God is known and is not a negation or relation
C) The philosophers' argument is circular
D) Negations are sufficient for knowledge

Key Takeaway
The responses show that the deniers' claim is contested: existence is known and attributes can be affirmed via revelation, but the philosophers maintain the deeper reality remains unknown.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
After reading the deniers' claim that we only know negations and relations of God, do you feel your own knowledge of God is sufficient? Are you content with knowing that He exists, or do you long to know His reality? What action will you take today to deepen your knowledge of Him through His revelation?
?
The philosophers argue that the true reality of God is unknown except through negative and relational descriptions. Does this disturb you? If so, why? Commit to reciting one of God's attributes from the Qur'an and ask yourself whether you truly understand it or merely repeat the words.
?
The passage shows a disagreement among scholars about how we know God. Does your own faith rely more on rational arguments or on texts? Challenge yourself to identify one belief you hold about God and trace whether it comes from revelation or reason. Would you still hold it if rational arguments alone could not prove it?
