The Dilemma and First Horn: When Gods Can Differ
Razi presents the first horn of his dilemma for tawhid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first horn of Imam Razi's dilemma: that assuming two gods who can differ leads to three impossible outcomes.

Core Idea
Two gods who can differ produce impossible contradictions in every scenario.

Imam al-Razi begins his proof of tawhid by setting up a powerful dilemma. If there were two gods, either they could will contrary acts or they could not. In this lesson we explore the first branch—what happens if they can differ. The argument shows that every possible outcome when they differ is logically impossible, thereby forcing us to reject the assumption.

Guided Path
1
Read the first horn passage
Read from 'لانا لو قدرنا إلهين' to 'وذلك يقتضي عدم القديم وهو محال' carefully. Note the structure: a dilemma within a dilemma.
This passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the three subcases
When difference occurs, Razi considers three possibilities: both wills fulfilled, neither fulfilled, or only one fulfilled. Write them down.
Each subcase ends with 'وهو محال' (which is impossible).
3
Understand the impossibility of each subcase
For each subcase, ask: why is it impossible? The commentary will help you see the logical contradictions.
The third subcase has two separate arguments.
4
Reflect on the method
Notice how Razi uses a reductio ad absurdum: assuming two gods leads to contradictions, so the assumption is false.
This is a cornerstone of kalam reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: الإله تعالى واحد. لانا لو قدرنا إلهين لمكان إما أن يصح من أحدهما أن يفعل فعلا على خلاف الآخر أو لا يصح، فإن صح فلنذر ذلك لأن ما ليس يمتنع لا يلزم من فرض وقوعه محال، وإلا لكان ممتنعا لا يمكننا، وعند وقوع ذلك الاختلاف فإما أن يحصل مرادهما فيكون الجسم الواحد متحركا ساكنا وهو محال، أو لا يحصل مرادهما وهو أيضا محال، لأن المانع من وجود مراد كل واحد منهما وجود مراد الآخر فامتناع مراد كل واحد منهما متوقف على حصول مراد الآخر، فلو امتنعا معا لوجدا معا وهو محال، أو يحصل مراد أحدهما دون مراد الثاني وهو أيضا محال لأن كل واحد منهما قادر على ما لا نهاية له فلا يكون أحدهما أولى بالرجحان، ولأن الذي يحصل مراده يكون عاجزا فجزاء جزيته إن كانت أزلية فهو محال، لأن العجز إنما يعقل عما يصح وجوده، ووجود المخلوق الأزلي محال، فالعجز عنه أزلا محال، وإن كانت حادثة فهو محال، لأن هذا إنما يعقل لو كان قادرا في الأزل ثم زالت قادريته وذلك يقتضي عدم القديم وهو محال. وأما إن امتنعت المخالفة فهو باطل. لأنه إذا كان كل واحد منهما قادرا على جميع المقدورات والقادر يصح منه فعل مقدوره فحينئذ يصح من فعل الحركة لولا الآخر ومن الآخر فعله السكون، لولا هذا فما لم يقصد أحدهما إلى الفعل لا يتعذر على الآخر القصد إلى فعله، لكن ليس تقدم قصد أحدهما على الآخر أولى من العكس، فإذا يستحيل أن يصير أحدهما مانعا للآخر من القصد وصحة المخالفة.
Issue: God, exalted, is one. For if we were to suppose two gods, it would either be possible for one of them to do an act contrary to the other, or not possible. If it is possible, then let us grant that, because that which is not impossible does not necessitate that its hypothetical occurrence is impossible; otherwise it would be impossible and not possible for us. And when that difference occurs, then either both of their wills are fulfilled, so one body would be moving and still, which is impossible; or neither of their wills is fulfilled, which is also impossible, because the impediment to the existence of the will of each of them is the existence of the will of the other, so the prevention of the will of each of them depends on the fulfillment of the will of the other. If both were prevented together, they would both exist together, which is impossible. Or the will of one of them is fulfilled without the will of the second, which is also impossible, because each of them is capable of what has no end, so one of them is not more worthy of preponderance. And because the one whose will is fulfilled would be incapable; if his recompense [for being incapable] is eternal, it is impossible, because incapability is conceived only concerning what is possible of existence, and the existence of an eternal creature is impossible, so incapability regarding it eternally is impossible. And if it is temporal, it is impossible, because this is only conceivable if he was capable in eternity then his capability ceased, and that necessitates the non-existence of the eternal, which is impossible. And if difference is impossible, then it is false. Because if each of them is capable of all possible things, and the capable one can perform his possible act, then it is possible for one to perform movement were it not for the other, and for the other to perform stillness, were it not for this. So as long as one of them does not intend the act, the other is not prevented from intending his act. But the precedence of the intention of one over the other is not more likely than the opposite. Thus it is impossible that one of them become an impediment to the other from intention and the validity of difference.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage presents a rational proof for the oneness of God (tawhid) based on the impossibility of two gods. The argument is structured as a dilemma. First, suppose there are two gods. Either it is possible for them to differ (i.e., one wills something contrary to the other), or it is not possible. If it is possible, then consider the case when they actually differ. There are three subcases: (1) Both wills are fulfilled—this leads to a body being simultaneously moving and still, an impossible contradiction. (2) Neither will is fulfilled—this is impossible because the prevention of each will depends on the other's will being fulfilled, leading to a circular dependency: both wills would have to exist and not exist together. (3) Only one will is fulfilled—this is impossible because both gods are equally powerful (capable of infinite things), so there is no reason for one to prevail over the other. Moreover, the god whose will is not fulfilled would be weak; his weakness would either be eternal (impossible, because weakness is only conceived regarding something possible, and an eternal creature is impossible) or temporal (impossible, because it would imply that the god was capable then lost capability, which requires change in the eternal). If, on the other hand, difference between the two gods is impossible (i.e., they cannot will contrary things), then this is also false, because each is capable of all possible acts; they could potentially will opposite acts, and there is no pre-established impediment. Since one's intention does not necessarily precede the other's, neither can prevent the other from intending. Thus the assumption of two gods leads to contradictions in all scenarios, proving that God is one.

Key Terms
إلهان
الاختلاف
الامتناع
المحال
القدرة
الإرادة
المراد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof shows that two gods would conflict, but they might still exist if they always agree.
Correction: The proof covers all possibilities: if they can differ, each scenario is impossible; if they cannot differ, that also leads to a contradiction (to be seen in Lesson 2). So no consistent scenario exists.

Checkpoint
In the first horn, what is one of the impossible consequences if both wills are fulfilled when two gods differ?
A) One body would be both moving and still
B) Neither will is fulfilled
C) One god becomes weak
D) The universe ceases to exist

Key Takeaway
If two gods can differ, every possible outcome leads to an impossibility, so the assumption of two gods must be false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly worship only One God, or do you find yourself seeking approval from people, status, or desires as if they were rivals? This passage shows that any difference between gods leads to absurdity. How can you eliminate the 'other gods' in your heart that compete for your obedience?
?
If it is impossible for two equally powerful wills to coexist without contradiction, what does that say about your own divided loyalties? Are you trying to serve both God and your own whims? Which will ultimately prevails in your life when they conflict? Commit today to submitting fully to the One whose will alone is worthy.
?
Razi's argument forces you to confront the logical necessity of tawhid. But is your belief in God's oneness merely intellectual, or does it shape how you live each moment? When did you last act in a way that would be impossible if you truly believed there is only one God with absolute power and will?

The Second Horn: When Gods Cannot Differ
Razi completes his dilemma by refuting the second horn.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the second horn of the dilemma—that two gods cannot differ—also leads to a contradiction, completing the proof that God is one.

Core Idea
Two gods who cannot differ are still impossible because each remains capable of independent action.

Imam Razi now examines the other branch of his dilemma: what if two gods cannot will contrary acts? He argues that this too is absurd, because each god is fully capable of all possible acts, and there is no reason for one to be impeded by the other. Thus the assumption of two gods leads to contradiction in every branch, leaving tawhid as the only rational conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the second horn passage
Read from 'وأما إن امتنعت المخالفة فهو باطل' to the end. Note the concise reasoning.
This passage is shorter but dense.
2
Understand the reasoning
Each god is capable of all possible acts. If they cannot differ, it means one is prevented from acting. But why? There's no reason for one's intention to precede the other's, so difference would be possible—contradicting the assumption.
The key is 'ليس تقدم قصد أحدهما على الآخر أولى من العكس'.
3
Connect to the conclusion
Since both horns are impossible, the original assumption of two gods is false. Therefore God is one.
See the overall proof structure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: الإله تعالى واحد. لانا لو قدرنا إلهين لمكان إما أن يصح من أحدهما أن يفعل فعلا على خلاف الآخر أو لا يصح، فإن صح فلنذر ذلك لأن ما ليس يمتنع لا يلزم من فرض وقوعه محال، وإلا لكان ممتنعا لا يمكننا، وعند وقوع ذلك الاختلاف فإما أن يحصل مرادهما فيكون الجسم الواحد متحركا ساكنا وهو محال، أو لا يحصل مرادهما وهو أيضا محال، لأن المانع من وجود مراد كل واحد منهما وجود مراد الآخر فامتناع مراد كل واحد منهما متوقف على حصول مراد الآخر، فلو امتنعا معا لوجدا معا وهو محال، أو يحصل مراد أحدهما دون مراد الثاني وهو أيضا محال لأن كل واحد منهما قادر على ما لا نهاية له فلا يكون أحدهما أولى بالرجحان، ولأن الذي يحصل مراده يكون عاجزا فجزاء جزيته إن كانت أزلية فهو محال، لأن العجز إنما يعقل عما يصح وجوده، ووجود المخلوق الأزلي محال، فالعجز عنه أزلا محال، وإن كانت حادثة فهو محال، لأن هذا إنما يعقل لو كان قادرا في الأزل ثم زالت قادريته وذلك يقتضي عدم القديم وهو محال. وأما إن امتنعت المخالفة فهو باطل. لأنه إذا كان كل واحد منهما قادرا على جميع المقدورات والقادر يصح منه فعل مقدوره فحينئذ يصح من فعل الحركة لولا الآخر ومن الآخر فعله السكون، لولا هذا فما لم يقصد أحدهما إلى الفعل لا يتعذر على الآخر القصد إلى فعله، لكن ليس تقدم قصد أحدهما على الآخر أولى من العكس، فإذا يستحيل أن يصير أحدهما مانعا للآخر من القصد وصحة المخالفة.
Issue: God, exalted, is one. For if we were to suppose two gods, it would either be possible for one of them to do an act contrary to the other, or not possible. If it is possible, then let us grant that, because that which is not impossible does not necessitate that its hypothetical occurrence is impossible; otherwise it would be impossible and not possible for us. And when that difference occurs, then either both of their wills are fulfilled, so one body would be moving and still, which is impossible; or neither of their wills is fulfilled, which is also impossible, because the impediment to the existence of the will of each of them is the existence of the will of the other, so the prevention of the will of each of them depends on the fulfillment of the will of the other. If both were prevented together, they would both exist together, which is impossible. Or the will of one of them is fulfilled without the will of the second, which is also impossible, because each of them is capable of what has no end, so one of them is not more worthy of preponderance. And because the one whose will is fulfilled would be incapable; if his recompense [for being incapable] is eternal, it is impossible, because incapability is conceived only concerning what is possible of existence, and the existence of an eternal creature is impossible, so incapability regarding it eternally is impossible. And if it is temporal, it is impossible, because this is only conceivable if he was capable in eternity then his capability ceased, and that necessitates the non-existence of the eternal, which is impossible. And if difference is impossible, then it is false. Because if each of them is capable of all possible things, and the capable one can perform his possible act, then it is possible for one to perform movement were it not for the other, and for the other to perform stillness, were it not for this. So as long as one of them does not intend the act, the other is not prevented from intending his act. But the precedence of the intention of one over the other is not more likely than the opposite. Thus it is impossible that one of them become an impediment to the other from intention and the validity of difference.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage presents a rational proof for the oneness of God (tawhid) based on the impossibility of two gods. The argument is structured as a dilemma. First, suppose there are two gods. Either it is possible for them to differ (i.e., one wills something contrary to the other), or it is not possible. If it is possible, then consider the case when they actually differ. (2) Neither will is fulfilled—this is impossible because the prevention of each will depends on the other's will being fulfilled, leading to a circular dependency: both wills would have to exist and not exist together. (3) Only one will is fulfilled—this is impossible because both gods are equally powerful (capable of infinite things), so there is no reason for one to prevail over the other. Moreover, the god whose will is not fulfilled would be weak; his weakness would either be eternal (impossible, because weakness is only conceived regarding something possible, and an eternal creature is impossible) or temporal (impossible, because it would imply that the god was capable then lost capability, which requires change in the eternal). Thus the assumption of two gods leads to contradictions in all scenarios, proving that God is one.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two gods cannot differ, they could coexist in harmony, so the proof only rules out conflict, not coexistence.
Correction: The proof shows that the very claim that they cannot differ is itself contradictory: if they were truly two independent deities, each would have the capacity to act differently, making difference possible. The impossibility of difference actually undermines the assumption of two gods.

Checkpoint
Why does Imam Razi argue that the second horn (difference impossible) is false?
A) Because both gods are equally powerful and there is no reason for one to be impeded
B) Because they would always agree
C) Because one god would be weaker
D) Because the universe would be chaotic

Key Takeaway
The second horn of the dilemma also fails, proving that the only consistent belief is that God is one.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that there is no other power that can rival Allah, would you still fear anyone or anything as you do? What does your heart's inclination towards certain people or things reveal about your tawhid?
?
Razi argues that two equal gods cannot exist because there is no reason for one to defer to the other. In your life, do you ever act as if your will is independent of Allah's? How can you consciously submit your intentions to Him today?
?
This passage shows that the very concept of multiple gods is logically impossible. Yet, many people live as if they serve multiple masters. Which 'gods' of your own making (status, comfort, relationships) have you allowed to vie with Allah for your ultimate trust? Will you actively depend on Him alone from now on?

The Dilemma: Can Two Gods Act Against Each Other?
Al-Razi enumerates three subcases of the first branch of his dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first horn of al-Razi's argument for oneness, where he shows that if two gods can disagree, it leads to three impossible consequences.

Core Idea
If two gods can disagree, each subcase leads to an absurdity.

In this passage, al-Razi starts his proof by assuming two gods and asks whether it is possible for one to act against the other. He first explores the branch where such opposition is possible, and he breaks it down into three subcases. Each subcase will be shown to be impossible, thus eliminating the first branch of the dilemma.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how al-Razi sets up the dilemma and then lists three subcases.
The subcases are: both wills fulfilled, neither fulfilled, one fulfilled.
2
Identify the first subcase
Find where al-Razi considers that both desires are fulfilled. What contradiction does he point out?
A single body cannot be both moving and stationary.
3
Identify the second subcase
Find where neither desire occurs. Why does al-Razi say this leads to an impossibility?
It creates a vicious circle where each depends on the other.
4
Identify the third subcase
Find where only one god's desire is fulfilled. Why is this impossible according to al-Razi?
Because both are equally powerful, no reason for one to prevail; also the failing god would have inability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : الإله تعالى واحد
لانا لو قدرنا إلهين لمكان إما أن يصح من أحدهما أن يفعل فعلا على خلاف الآخر أو لا يصح، فإن صح فلنظر ذلك لأن ما ليس يمتنع لا يلزم من فرض وقوعه محال، وإلا لكان ممتنعا لا يمكننا، وعند وقوع ذلك الاختلاف فإما أن يحصل مرادهما فيكون الجسم الواحد متحركا ساكنا وهو محال، أو لا يحصل مرادهما وهو أيضاً محال، لأن المانع من وجود مراد كل واحد منهما وجود مراد الآخر فامتناع مراد كل واحد منهما متوقف على حصول مراد الآخر، فلو امتنعا معا لوجدا معا وهو محال، أو يحصل مراد أحدهما دون مراد الثاني وهو أيضا محال لأن كل واحد منهما قادر على مالا نهاية له فلا يكون أحدهما أولى بالرجحان، ولأن الذي يحصل مراده يكون عاجزا فاعجزيته إن كانت أزلية فهو محال، لأن العجز إنما يعقل عما يصح وجوده، ووجود المخلوق الأزلي محال، فالعجز عنه أزلا محال، وإن كانت حادثة فهو محال، لأن هذا إنما يعقل لو كان قادرا في الأزل ثم زالت قادريته وذلك يقتضي عدم القديم وهو محال، وأما إن امتنعت المخالفة فهذا باطل، لأنه إذا كان كل واحد منهما قادرا على جميع المقدورات والقادر يصح منه فعل مقدوره فحينئذ يصح من فعل الحركة لولا الآخر ومن الآخر فعل السكون لولا هذا، فما لم يقصد أحدهما إلى الفعل لا يتعذر على الآخر القصد إلى فعله، لكن ليس تقدم قصد أحدهما على الآخر أولى من العكس، فإذا يستحيل أن يصير أحدهما مانعا للآخر من القصد وصحة المخالفة
Issue: God is one.
For if we suppose two gods, either it is possible for one of them to do an act contrary to the other, or it is not possible. If it is possible, let us consider that, because what is not impossible does not entail an impossibility when its occurrence is assumed; otherwise it would be impossible and not possible for us. When that difference occurs: either their desire is realized, so the one body would be moving and stationary, which is impossible; or their desire does not occur, which is also impossible, because the impediment to the occurrence of the desire of each is the occurrence of the desire of the other, so the impossibility of the desire of each is dependent on the occurrence of the desire of the other. If both were impossible, they would both exist, which is impossible. Or the desire of one occurs without the desire of the other, which is also impossible, because each of them is capable of infinite things, so neither has priority for preponderance; and because the one whose desire occurs would be unable. His inability: if it is eternal, it is impossible, because inability is only conceivable regarding what is possible of existence, and an eternal created being is impossible, so inability regarding it eternally is impossible. If it is temporal, it is impossible, because this is only conceivable if he was capable in eternity then his capability ceased, which requires the non-existence of the eternal, which is impossible.
As for if disagreement is impossible: this is false. Because if each of them is capable of all possibles, and the capable one validly performs his possible, then it would be valid for one to perform motion were it not for the other, and for the other to perform rest were it not for the first. So as long as one of them does not intend the act, the other's intention to his act is not prevented. But the priority of one's intention over the other is not more likely than the opposite. Thus it is impossible that one of them becomes a preventer for the other from intention and from the validity of disagreement.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage is a rational proof (burhan) for the oneness of God. Al-Razi argues by supposing two gods and examining all possibilities of whether they can act against each other or not. The proof is structured as a dilemma: either they can differ or they cannot. If they can differ, that leads to three subcases: both wills fulfilled (contradiction), neither fulfilled (mutual blockage leading to both impossible which implies both exist—absurd), or one fulfilled and not the other (which requires a reason for preponderance, but both are equally powerful and infinite, so no reason; also the failure of one implies his inability, which is impossible either as eternal or temporal). If they cannot differ, then they are both omnipotent and can potentially act in opposite ways, but since neither has precedence in intention, neither could prevent the other from intending, so disagreement becomes possible again. Thus any scenario leads to impossibility or falsehood, proving that two gods cannot exist.

Key Terms
إلهان
المخالفة
المراد
العجز
القديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument only works if the two gods are unequal in power.
Correction: Al-Razi assumes equal power and infinite capability; even then, the third subcase shows impossibility due to lack of preponderance.

Checkpoint
In the first subcase of al-Razi's argument, what contradiction arises if both gods' desires are fulfilled?
A) The body would be both moving and stationary.
B) The gods would lack power.
C) The universe would be created multiple times.
D) The desires cancel out.

Key Takeaway
If two gods could act against each other, every possible outcome leads to an absurdity, thus the first branch of the dilemma is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that God is one because any other possibility leads to absurdity, how does that conviction affect your daily reliance on Him alone? Is there any area of your life where you are acting as if there is another power you fear or depend on besides God?
?
The argument shows that any division in will is impossible for God. Do you ever find yourself doubting that God's will is fully sovereign over your circumstances? What does that reveal about your trust in His oneness?
?
Al-Razi's proof ends with the absurdity of an eternal being losing power. If you truly internalize that God's power is unchanging and eternal, how should that change your attitude when you feel powerless in your life?

The Second Horn: If Two Gods Cannot Disagree
Al-Razi refutes the second branch of his dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's refutation of the second branch of his dilemma, proving that two gods cannot exist because even if they cannot disagree, the possibility of disagreement remains and leads to impossibility.

Core Idea
If two gods cannot disagree, they would each be omnipotent but unable to prevent the other's intention, making disagreement possible again.

After eliminating the possibility that two gods could disagree, al-Razi now turns to the alternative: that they cannot disagree at all. He argues that this is false because if both are omnipotent over all possibles, then each validly perform their acts in the absence of the other. But since there is no priority of intention, one cannot prevent the other from intending the opposite act. Thus, the assumption that disagreement is impossible leads back to the possibility of disagreement, making the entire scenario impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the second horn. Notice how al-Razi builds the argument.
2
Understand the assumption
Al-Razi assumes both gods are omnipotent over all possible acts. What does that imply?
Each can validly perform any act if not prevented.
3
Follow the reasoning
Since neither intends first, there is no prevention. So both could intend opposite acts, meaning disagreement is possible after all.
4
Conclude
Thus the second branch reduces to the first, which has already been shown impossible. So the supposition of two gods is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : الإله تعالى واحد
لانا لو قدرنا إلهين لمكان إما أن يصح من أحدهما أن يفعل فعلا على خلاف الآخر أو لا يصح، فإن صح فلنظر ذلك لأن ما ليس يمتنع لا يلزم من فرض وقوعه محال، وإلا لكان ممتنعا لا يمكننا، وعند وقوع ذلك الاختلاف فإما أن يحصل مرادهما فيكون الجسم الواحد متحركا ساكنا وهو محال، أو لا يحصل مرادهما وهو أيضاً محال، لأن المانع من وجود مراد كل واحد منهما وجود مراد الآخر فامتناع مراد كل واحد منهما متوقف على حصول مراد الآخر، فلو امتنعا معا لوجدا معا وهو محال، أو يحصل مراد أحدهما دون مراد الثاني وهو أيضا محال لأن كل واحد منهما قادر على مالا نهاية له فلا يكون أحدهما أولى بالرجحان، ولأن الذي يحصل مراده يكون عاجزا فاعجزيته إن كانت أزلية فهو محال، لأن العجز إنما يعقل عما يصح وجوده، ووجود المخلوق الأزلي محال، فالعجز عنه أزلا محال، وإن كانت حادثة فهو محال، لأن هذا إنما يعقل لو كان قادرا في الأزل ثم زالت قادريته وذلك يقتضي عدم القديم وهو محال، وأما إن امتنعت المخالفة فهذا باطل، لأنه إذا كان كل واحد منهما قادرا على جميع المقدورات والقادر يصح منه فعل مقدوره فحينئذ يصح من فعل الحركة لولا الآخر ومن الآخر فعل السكون لولا هذا، فما لم يقصد أحدهما إلى الفعل لا يتعذر على الآخر القصد إلى فعله، لكن ليس تقدم قصد أحدهما على الآخر أولى من العكس، فإذا يستحيل أن يصير أحدهما مانعا للآخر من القصد وصحة المخالفة
Issue: God is one.
For if we suppose two gods, either it is possible for one of them to do an act contrary to the other, or it is not possible. If it is possible, let us consider that, because what is not impossible does not entail an impossibility when its occurrence is assumed; otherwise it would be impossible and not possible for us. When that difference occurs: either their desire is realized, so the one body would be moving and stationary, which is impossible; or their desire does not occur, which is also impossible, because the impediment to the occurrence of the desire of each is the occurrence of the desire of the other, so the impossibility of the desire of each is dependent on the occurrence of the desire of the other. If both were impossible, they would both exist, which is impossible. Or the desire of one occurs without the desire of the other, which is also impossible, because each of them is capable of infinite things, so neither has priority for preponderance; and because the one whose desire occurs would be unable. His inability: if it is eternal, it is impossible, because inability is only conceivable regarding what is possible of existence, and an eternal created being is impossible, so inability regarding it eternally is impossible. If it is temporal, it is impossible, because this is only conceivable if he was capable in eternity then his capability ceased, which requires the non-existence of the eternal, which is impossible.
As for if disagreement is impossible: this is false. Because if each of them is capable of all possibles, and the capable one validly performs his possible, then it would be valid for one to perform motion were it not for the other, and for the other to perform rest were it not for the first. So as long as one of them does not intend the act, the other's intention to his act is not prevented. But the priority of one's intention over the other is not more likely than the opposite. Thus it is impossible that one of them becomes a preventer for the other from intention and from the validity of disagreement.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage is a rational proof (burhan) for the oneness of God. Al-Razi argues by supposing two gods and examining all possibilities of whether they can act against each other or not. The proof is structured as a dilemma: either they can differ or they cannot. If they can differ, that leads to three subcases: both wills fulfilled (contradiction), neither fulfilled (mutual blockage leading to both impossible which implies both exist—absurd), or one fulfilled and not the other (which requires a reason for preponderance, but both are equally powerful and infinite, so no reason; also the failure of one implies his inability, which is impossible either as eternal or temporal). If they cannot differ, then they are both omnipotent and can potentially act in opposite ways, but since neither has precedence in intention, neither could prevent the other from intending, so disagreement becomes possible again. Thus any scenario leads to impossibility or falsehood, proving that two gods cannot exist.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If the two gods are perfectly harmonious, they would never disagree, so the second branch is plausible.
Correction: Even if they do not actually disagree, their omnipotence makes disagreement possible, which reintroduces the problems of the first branch.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi, why is the assumption that two gods cannot disagree false?
A) Because they are both omnipotent and can potentially perform opposite acts.
B) Because they would both be unable to act.
C) Because they would need a third god to decide.
D) Because disagreement is impossible by definition.

Key Takeaway
The second horn of the dilemma collapses because the assumption that two gods cannot disagree is inconsistent with their omnipotence.

?

?
Al-Razi’s argument shows that even the assumption of perfect harmony between two gods is self-contradictory. Are there areas in your life where you unconsciously act as if there are competing authorities? What would it mean for you to consciously surrender to the One whose will cannot be opposed?
?
This proof demonstrates that only Allah can be truly omnipotent. When you feel pressured by the expectations or demands of others, does this logical certainty give you the courage to prioritize His command above all else? What specific commitment will you make today to live out this conviction?

Objection and Answer on Divine Unity: The Motivator (Da'i) Analysis
Al-Razi answers the objection that two wise gods would agree on the best course.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi refutes the objection that two wise gods would necessarily agree, by analyzing the role of the motivator (da'i) in human choice and divine action.

Core Idea
Divine wisdom does not force agreement; the motivator (da'i) breaks the chain.

Al-Razi presents a classic objection to the proof of divine oneness: if two gods were both wise, they would always agree on the best course of action, so no conflict. In his answer, he dissects the nature of action and motivation, showing why agreement is not necessary. This passage sharpens our understanding of divine attributes and human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the Objection and Answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: 'If it is said...' and 'We say...'
The objection is a common one against tawhid proofs.
2
Identify the Two Scenarios
Al-Razi splits his answer based on whether acts depend on a motivator (da'i) or not. Find each scenario.
The first scenario: acts depend on da'i. The second: acts do not.
3
Trace the Logic of the First Scenario
If acts depend on da'i, then human choice of evil requires God to create the evil motivator, but that doesn't make God's act evil under their definition.
Be careful: this does not endorse the objector's moral framework.
4
Trace the Logic of the Second Scenario
If acts don't depend on da'i, both gods can choose opposite actions of equal moral weight, leading to disagreement.
This scenario shows that even without moral constraints, disagreement is possible.
5
Connect to the Main Proof
Understand how this answer defends the impossibility of two gods by showing that the objection does not prove necessary agreement.
The objection fails to undermine the proof of tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: لم لا يجوز أن يقال أيهما لكونهما حكيمين لا يريدان إلا الصلاح واحدا فلا جرم يجب توافقهما. قلنا : الفعل إما أن يتوقف على الداعي أولا يتوقف، فإن توقف على الداعي لا مجال من العبد أن يختار الفعل القبيح إلا إذا خلق الله فيه داعيا يدعوه إليه ، وإذا كان الداعي إلى القبيح موجبا للقبح كان قبيحا ، وإذا كان الفاعل لذلك الداعي هو الله تعالى لم يجب أن يكون فعل الله حسنا بالتفسير الذي تريدونه ، فلم يلزم اتفاق الإلهين على الفعل الواحد فصحت المخالفة بينهما ، وإن لم يتوقف الفعل على الداعي جاز في الضدين المتساويين في الحسن و القبح أن يختار أحد الإلهين إيجاد أحدهما والإله الآخر إيجاد الآخر وحينئذ تحصل المخالفة بينهما
If it is said: Why is it not permissible to say that each of them, since they are wise, do not intend anything but what is the best and one, so they must necessarily agree? We say: The act either depends on the motivator (da'i) or does not depend. If it depends on the motivator, there is no way for the servant to choose the ugly act unless God creates in him a motivator that calls him to it. And if the motivator to the ugly necessitates ugliness, it becomes ugly. And if the doer of that motivator is God, it does not have to be that the act of God is good according to the interpretation you intend. Thus agreement of the two gods on one act is not necessary, and disagreement between them becomes valid. And if the act does not depend on the motivator, then in the case of two opposites equal in goodness and ugliness, it is permissible for one god to choose to bring about one of them and the other god to bring about the other, and then disagreement occurs between them.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer (sual wa jawab) against the proof of divine oneness. The objector claims that two wise gods would always agree on the best course, so no conflict occurs. Al-Razi replies by considering whether acts depend on a motivator (da'i) or not. If they do depend, then human choice of evil occurs only if God creates a motivator to evil; but that would make God the agent of the evil motivator, which the objector would consider ugly, but al-Razi does not accept that their standard of good applies to God. Thus agreement is not inevitable. If acts do not depend on a motivator, then both gods can freely choose opposite actions of equal moral weight, leading to disagreement. Hence the objection fails.

Key Terms
الحكيم
الداعي
الصلاح
القبيح
الحسن
المخالفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that divine wisdom forces God to always choose the best and thus two gods would necessarily agree.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that their concept of 'best' is relative, and divine actions are not bound by human ethical standards; even if they were, the two scenarios allow for disagreement.

Checkpoint
What is the first scenario al-Razi considers in his answer to the objection?
A) The act depends on the motivator.
B) The act is eternal.
C) The acts are identical.
D) The gods cannot act.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi shows that regardless of whether acts depend on a motivator or not, two gods are not forced to agree, thus the objection fails.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own choices that go against what you know is right, do you attribute the motivator (da'i) to yourself or to God? How does al-Razi's framing of the da'i challenge your assumption of complete autonomy? What would change if you truly believed that every motivator to evil is created by God?
?
If you believe that God is wise and only does the best, does that force you to justify every evil in the world? Al-Razi's argument shows that 'best' is not determined by human standards. Is your heart content with a God whose wisdom is beyond your understanding? What do you need to submit in your mind to accept that?
?
Imagine two beings, each claiming divinity, acting in opposite ways. The objection says they would agree. But al-Razi says disagreement is possible. How does this affect your conviction in the oneness of God? Are you relying on human logic or divine revelation to affirm tawheed?

The Limitation of the Tawhid Proof
A marginal note on the scope of al-Razi's tawhid proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Razi's proof only refutes one type of polytheism and why a deeper argument about necessary existence is required.

Core Idea
Al-Razi's proof works for equal gods but not for hierarchical ones.

Al-Razi's proof of tawhid seems powerful, but this gloss by a commentator points out its scope. While the proof shows that two equal gods cannot exist, it does not address a more common form of polytheism where gods are ranked in power. This lesson explores that distinction and shows why a different argument is required.

Guided Path
1
Read the gloss carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the two parts: the mention of a stronger proof, and the limitation of the current proof.
2
Identify the two types of polytheism
The gloss contrasts 'equal gods' (الإلهان المتساويان) with 'hierarchical gods' (الآلهة المترتبة). Understand the difference: in the latter, one god can prevent the other's actions.
Most polytheists believed in hierarchy, not equality.
3
Connect to the deeper argument
The gloss refers to the earlier proof of the impossibility of two necessary existents. Recognize that this is the stronger argument that refutes all forms of polytheism.
This earlier proof is more fundamental.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول : قد مر امتناع وجود واجب الوجودين اثنين وذلك يكفي في إثبات هذا المطلوب ، وأما هذا الدليل فيدل على امتناع كون الإلهين متساويين من كل الوجوه ولا يدل على امتناع كون آلهة مترتبة يقدر العالي منها يمنع السافل مما يريده من غير عكس، ومذهب أكثر المشركين هو هذا
I say: The impossibility of the existence of two necessary existents has already been mentioned, and that suffices to prove this required point. As for this proof, it indicates the impossibility of two gods being equal in every respect, but it does not indicate the impossibility of gods being hierarchical where the higher among them can prevent the lower from what it wills without the reverse, and the doctrine of most polytheists is this.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage is a critical gloss (ta'liq) by a commentator on the preceding proof of tawhid. The commentator notes that the proof offered by al-Razi (the one in Lesson 3) only refutes the possibility of two gods equal in every respect, but does not address the possibility of a hierarchy of gods, where one is superior and can block the other. Most polytheists (like those who believed in a supreme god and subordinate deities) actually believed in such a hierarchy. The more fundamental refutation, he says, is the earlier argument about the impossibility of two necessary existents (wajib al-wujud), which is a stronger proof. Thus the lesson is that the given proof is limited and another proof is needed to fully establish tawhid.

Key Terms
واجب الوجود
الإلهان المتساويان
الآلهة المترتبة
المشركون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Razi's proof of tawhid fully establishes tawhid and refutes all forms of polytheism.
Correction: The proof only refutes polytheism with equal gods; it does not refute hierarchical polytheism, which was more common. A deeper argument about necessary existence is needed.

Checkpoint
According to this gloss, what kind of polytheism does al-Razi's proof not refute?
A) Polytheism with two equal gods.
B) Polytheism with hierarchical gods where the higher can prevent the lower.
C) Polytheism with many independent gods.
D) Polytheism with a single god and many demiurges.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's proof only refutes equal gods; a stronger argument about necessary existence is needed to refute hierarchical polytheism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever, in your understanding of tawhid, rely on an incomplete argument? Are you satisfied with a shallow proof, or do you seek the deeper reasoning that refutes even the most subtle forms of shirk? What will you do to strengthen your foundation?
?
The passage says most polytheists believed in a hierarchy of gods, with a supreme god over subordinates. In your own life, are there any 'subordinate gods'—things you prioritize, fear, or love above Allah? Name them honestly and decide how to demote them.
?
Al-Razi's proof was insufficient, and a commentator corrected it. Are you open to having your own beliefs and arguments corrected? When was the last time you admitted a flaw in your understanding and changed? How can you cultivate humility in seeking truth?

Why Human Ignorance Proves God as the Sole Creator
Imam Razi presents a proof against human creation of actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's second argument against Mu'tazila, which demonstrates that human lack of detailed knowledge of their own actions proves they cannot be the creators of those actions.

Core Idea
Creation demands detailed knowledge; our ignorance proves we are not creators.

Imam Razi presents a logical argument that strikes at the heart of the Mu'tazilite claim that humans create their own actions. He shows that creating something requires detailed knowledge of its particulars, which humans clearly lack. This lesson walks through the steps of that argument, drawing on examples and even the Mu'tazilites' own positions to establish that the servant cannot be the creator of his actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main claim
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Razi is proving that the servant is not the creator of his actions. Note the conditional structure: if servant were creator, he would have detailed knowledge.
2
Trace the logical chain: universal vs particular intention
Follow the argument that universal intention cannot determine which particular occurs. Razi shows particular intention is needed, which requires particular knowledge.
This is a key conceptual move.
3
Examine the three pieces of evidence
Razi gives three reasons: (1) the world's complexity, (2) slow movement example, (3) the Mu'tazilites' own view on causation. Consider each in light of the argument.
These examples ground the argument in observable reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني. لو كان العبد موجدا لأفعال نفسه لكان عالما بتفاصيلها، إذ لو جوزنا الإيجاد من غير علم بطل دليل إثبات عالمية الله تعالى، لأن القصد الكلي لا يكفي في حصول الجزئي، لأن نسبة الكلي إلى جميع الجزئيات على السواء فليس حصول بعضها أولى من حصول الباقي فثبت أنه لا بد من القصد الجزئي وهو مشروط بالعلم الجزئي فثبت أنه لو كان موجدا لأفعال نفسه لكان عالما بتفاصيلها، لكنه غير عالم بتفاصيلها أولا ففي حق العالم، وأما ثانيا فلأن الفاعل للحركة البطيئة قد فعل السكون في بعض الأحياز والحركة في بعضها مع لا شعور له بالسكون، وأما ثالثا فلأن عند أبي علي وأبي هاشم مقدور العبد ليس نفس التحصيل في الخير لعلة ذلك التحصيل مع أنه لا شعور لأكثر الخلق بتلك العلة لا جملة ولا تفصيلا.
Second: If the servant were the creator of his own actions, he would be knowledgeable of their details. For if we allowed bringing into existence without knowledge, the proof of establishing God's omniscience would be invalidated. Because the universal intention is not sufficient for the occurrence of the particular, since the relation of the universal to all particulars is equal, so the occurrence of some of them is not more rightful than the occurrence of the rest. Thus it is established that particular intention is necessary, and it is conditioned by particular knowledge. Thus it is established that if he were the creator of his own actions, he would be knowledgeable of their details. But he is not knowledgeable of their details—firstly, with respect to the world; secondly, because the doer of a slow movement has performed rest in some spaces and movement in others while having no awareness of the rest; thirdly, because according to Abu Ali and Abu Hashim, what is within the servant's power is not the actual acquisition itself but the cause of that acquisition, yet most creatures have no awareness of that cause, either in general or in detail.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage is a proof (دليل) against the Mu'tazilite claim that humans create their own actions. Imam Razi argues that if a human were truly the originator (موجد) of his actions, he would need detailed particular knowledge of every aspect of those actions, because bringing something into existence without knowledge would undermine the proof for God's omniscience. The argument proceeds in steps: (1) a universal intention cannot determine which particular of a set occurs, so particular intention is required; (2) particular intention depends on particular knowledge; (3) therefore, a creator of his own acts must have detailed knowledge of them; (4) but humans clearly lack such knowledge, as shown by examples: a person performing a slow movement is unaware of the small rests between positions; and the Mu'tazilites themselves hold that the servant's power is over the cause of an act, not the act itself, yet most people are ignorant of that cause. Thus the conclusion that the servant is not the creator of his actions is established.

Key Terms
موجد
إيجاد
قصد كلي
قصد جزئي
علم جزئي
مقدور العبد
تحصيل
علة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A general intention is enough to produce a specific action.
Correction: Razi argues that universal intention relates equally to all particulars, so it cannot determine which particular occurs. Particular intention and knowledge are necessary.

Checkpoint
Why does Imam Razi argue that the servant cannot be the creator of his own actions?
A) Because God has more power.
B) Because creation requires particular knowledge, which the servant lacks.
C) Because the servant has no intention at all.
D) Because the Mu'tazilites deny human agency.

Key Takeaway
The second argument establishes that human ignorance of the details of their actions disproves their ability to create those actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you are not the creator of your own actions, what does that mean for the moments you feel most in control? Will you honestly examine an action you performed today and admit you did not know all its details? How does that change your reliance on Allah?
?
The proof shows that creation requires exhaustive knowledge. Are you willing to let go of the illusion that you fully understand even your simplest deeds? Will you acknowledge your ignorance and turn to Allah as the only one who truly knows and creates?
?
Consider a recent action you believed was entirely your own doing. In light of Razi's argument about unawareness of the cause, can you continue to claim ownership without feeling a need to attribute it entirely to Allah? How will you cultivate a deeper sense of gratitude for His sole creative power?

First Proof of the Self's Non-Bodily Nature: The Indivisibility of Knowledge of God
Proof of the self's non-bodily nature via indivisibility of knowledge of God, with objection and response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that knowledge of God is indivisible, why this entails that the self is not a body, and how the objection based on the indivisible atom is addressed.

Core Idea
If knowledge of God is indivisible, its locus cannot be a divisible body.

This lesson explores a key argument used by philosophers, Mu'ammar, and al-Ghazali to prove that the human self is not a body. The proof hinges on the nature of knowledge of God: if that knowledge is indivisible, then the self that possesses it must be indivisible as well. The author presents the argument, considers a potential objection, and offers a brief response.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim and its proponents
Identify who advanced this proof: philosophers, Mu'ammar, and al-Ghazali. They argued that the self is not a body.
This is a standard argument in philosophical theology.
2
Follow the indivisibility argument
Walk through the hypothetical division of knowledge of God. If divisible, each part would either be knowledge or not, leading to absurdities.
The argument uses a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Derive the conclusion about the locus
Since knowledge of God is indivisible, its locus (the self) must also be indivisible. Because every extended body is divisible, the self cannot be a body.
This relies on the premise that no indivisible atom exists.
4
Consider the objection and the answer
The objector denies the premise that the state in a divisible is divisible, citing counterexamples like point, unity, relation, existence. The answer reaffirms the existence of the indivisible atom (جوهر فرد).
This shows the debate hinges on atomism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الذين قالوا إنه غير جسم وهم الفلاسفة ومن المعتزلة معمر ومنهم الإمام الغزالي، والحجة القوية المثبتة من وجهين: الأول: أن العلم بالله تعالى غير منقسم، إذ لو انقسم لأمكن أن يكون كل واحد من أجزائه علما أو لا يكون، فإن كان علما فإما أن يكون علما بكل ذلك المعلوم فيكون الجزء مساويا للكل وهذا خلف، وإن لم يكن علما بذلك المعلوم فعند اجتماع تلك الأجزاء إن لم يحصل هيئة زائدة لم يحصل العلم بالله تعالى وهذا خلف، وإن حصلت هيئة زائدة فإن انقسمت عاد التقسيم وإلا لحصل المقصود. وإذا ثبت ذلك وجب أن لا يكون محله منقسما لأن الحال في المنقسم منقسم وكل متحيز منقسم بناء على نفي الجوهر الفرد، فمحل العلم بالله تعالى غير متحيز ولا حال فيه. وجوابه: أنا بينا إثبات الجوهر الفرد. ثم إن قوله: الحال في المنقسم منقسم باطل بالنقطة والوحدة والإضافة والوجود.
As for those who said that it is not a body – and they are the philosophers, and among the Mu'tazila: Mu'ammar, and among them Imam al-Ghazali – the strong proof for its establishment is from two aspects: First: that knowledge of God Most High is indivisible. For if it were divisible, it would be possible that each of its parts is knowledge or not. If it is knowledge, then either it is knowledge of all that known object, so the part would equal the whole, which is absurd; or it is not knowledge of that known object, then upon combining those parts, if no additional quality arises, knowledge of God would not be obtained, which is absurd; and if an additional quality arises, then if it is divisible, the division recurs, and if not, the purpose is achieved. And if this is established, then it necessarily follows that its locus is not divisible, because the state in a divisible is divisible, and every extended thing (mutable) is divisible based on the denial of the indivisible atom. So the locus of knowledge of God is neither extended nor does it have a state in it. Its answer: we have already proven the existence of the indivisible atom. Further, his saying 'the state in the divisible is divisible' is invalid by (the concepts of) point, unity, relation, and existence.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof for the claim that the self is not a body, attributed to philosophers, Mu'ammar, and al-Ghazali. The proof is based on the nature of knowledge of God. Knowledge of God is indivisible; the author argues by considering division: if it were divisible, each part would either be knowledge or not. If each part is knowledge, it would have to know the entire object, making the part equal to the whole – impossible. If the parts are not knowledge individually, then their combination would either produce a new quality (which if divisible recurs, if not divisible then the goal is reached) or not produce knowledge – also impossible. Therefore knowledge of God is indivisible, and its locus (the self) must also be indivisible. Since every extended body is divisible (assuming no atom), the self cannot be extended nor possess states (like being in a body). The objection is answered by reaffirming the existence of the indivisible atom (جوهر فرد). The author also dismisses the objection that a state in a divisible is divisible, citing examples like point, unity, relation, and existence which are in some way indivisible.

Key Terms
غير منقسم
الحال في المنقسم
الجوهر الفرد
متحيز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge of God can be divided into parts that each know part of God.
Correction: If each part knows only part, then combined they still wouldn't know God unless a new quality emerges; if they know the whole, part equals whole, impossible. Thus knowledge of God must be indivisible.

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion of the first proof regarding the locus of knowledge of God?
A) It is divisible but extended.
B) It is a subtle body.
C) It is not extended and has no state in it.
D) It is a composite of atoms.

Key Takeaway
The indivisibility of knowledge of God leads to the conclusion that the self is not a body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your knowledge of God is truly indivisible, then it must permeate every aspect of your life. Is there any area—your work, your relationships, your private thoughts—where you act as if that knowledge does not exist? What would it take for you to make that knowledge whole?

The Philosophers' Proof of the Soul's Incorruptibility
Philosophers' proof of soul's incorruptibility with objections and responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that the soul cannot be annihilated, and the key objections and responses in that debate.

Core Idea
The soul's immateriality proves its immortality.

In this passage, the great scholar Fakhr al-Din al-Razi presents the philosophers' proof for the incorruptibility of the soul, along with critical objections and their rebuttals. This debate touches on the nature of existence, possibility, and the relationship between matter and form. As we read, pay attention to how the argument hinges on whether possibility is a positive attribute requiring a substrate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage once
Read the entire passage to get the overall flow of the argument and its dialectical structure.
The text includes a proof, an objection, a response, another objection, and a final response.
2
Identify the main proof
The philosophers argue that if the soul could be annihilated, it would need matter, but it is immaterial, so annihilation is impossible.
Notice the key premise: 'everything upon which non-existence can occur has matter'.
3
Analyze the first objection and response
The objection states that possibility is not a positive attribute, so it doesn't require a locus. The response is implicit that possibility is a real attribute for the contingent.
This touches on the nature of possibility in kalam.
4
Examine the second objection and response
Another objection: if the soul had a material part, that part would remain, so the soul remains. The response: the composite does not remain by the remaining of one part; the soul's happiness/misery requires the form, not just matter.
This clarifies the purpose of proving the soul's survival.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : اتفقت الفلاسفة على امتناع عدم الأرواح. واحتجوا بأن العدم لو صح عليها لكان إمكان العدم مقدما لا محالة على العدم، وذلك الإمكان يستدعى محلا ، ويجب أن يكون المحل بافيا عند ذلك العدم، لأن القابل واجب الحصول عند المقبول والشيء لا يبقى عند عدمه ، فإذا كل ما يصح عليه العدم فله مادة ، فلو صح المدم على النفس لكانت مركبة من المادة والصورة لكن ذلك باطل لما بينا أنها ليست مجسم، ولأنا على هذا التقدير إذا نظرنا إلى الجزء المادى لم يكن قابلا للمدم، وإلا لافتقر إلى مادة أخرى ولا محالة ينتهى إلى مادة له ، فيكون ذلك الشيء غير قابل للفساد والاعتراض لا نعلم أن الإمكان أمر ثبوتى وعلى هذا التقدير لا يستدعى محلا ، وأيضاً فالنفس حادثة فكونها مسبوقة بالإمكان السابق لما لم يوجب كونها مادية ، فذلك إمكان فسادها . سلمنا أنها لو قبلت العدم لكانت مادية ، فلم لا يجوز قولد كل مادی جسم قلنا : لا تسلم بل مذهبكم أن كل مادى والموجبة الكلية لا تنعكس كنفسها وكيف وهى تحت جنس الجوهر فتكون مركبة . قوله : إذا نظرنا إلى الجزء المادى وجب أن يكون باقيا ، هب أنه مجب بقاء مادة النفس لكن لا يلزم من بقاء مادة النفس بقاء النفس ، لأن المركب لا يبقى ببقاء أحد أجزائه . وتحقيقه أن المقصود من إثبات بقاء النفس إثبات سعادتها وشقاوتها وذلك غير حاصل على هذا التقدير ، نه على تقدر بقاء مادتها دون صورتها لا يمكن القطع ببقاء كمانتها لإمكان توقف إمكان تلك الكمالات على حصول الجزء الصوري الثابت
Question: The philosophers agreed on the impossibility of the non-existence of souls. They argued that if non-existence were to occur to them, then the possibility of non-existence would necessarily precede non-existence, and that possibility requires a locus, and the locus must be existing at the time of non-existence, because the receptacle must be present when the received exists, and a thing does not remain at its non-existence. So everything upon which non-existence can occur has matter. So if non-existence were to occur to the soul, it would be composed of matter and form, but that is false because we have shown it is not a body. And also on this supposition, if we look at the material part, it would not be receptive to non-existence, otherwise it would need another matter, and inevitably it would end at a matter that is not susceptible to corruption. And the objection: we do not know that possibility is a positive attribute; on this supposition it does not require a locus. Also, the soul is originated, so being preceded by prior possibility does not necessitate that it is material; that is the possibility of its corruption. We grant that if it accepted non-existence it would be material, so why is it not said that every material thing is a body? We say: we do not grant that; rather your doctrine is that every material thing is [a body]? The universal affirmative does not convert, especially since it is under the genus of substance, so it would be composite. He said: When we look at the material part, it must remain. Granted that the matter of the soul must remain, but from the remaining of the matter of the soul, the remaining of the soul does not follow, because the composite does not remain by the remaining of one of its parts. And its verification: The purpose of proving the soul's remaining is to prove its happiness and misery, and that is not attained on this supposition, because on the supposition of the remaining of its matter without its form, one cannot be certain of the remaining of its perfections, because it is possible that those perfections depend on the attainment of the constant form.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' proof that souls cannot be annihilated, followed by objections and responses. The proof: If the soul could cease to exist, its possibility of non-existence would require a material substrate (matter + form). But the soul is not a body (as previously shown), so it cannot have matter; therefore it cannot be annihilated. An objection: Possibility is not a positive attribute, so it doesn't need a locus. Also, the soul is originated, so its prior possibility doesn't make it material. The response: even if we accept that non-existence requires matter, why isn't every material thing a body? The reply: the universal affirmative does not convert; being under the genus of substance does not make it corporeal. Another objection: if the soul had a material part, that part would remain, so the soul remains. The answer: the remaining of the matter does not entail the remaining of the soul (the composite), because the composite can dissolve. The passage ends with a verification: the purpose of proving the soul's survival is to guarantee its eternal happiness or misery; if only the matter remains without the form (the soul itself), then its perfections are not guaranteed.

Key Terms
عدم (adam)
إمكان (imkan)
مادة (madda)
صورة (sura)
النفس (al-nafs)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Possibility is not a real attribute and does not require a locus.
Correction: In kalam, possibility is a real attribute for contingent beings, thus requiring a substrate.

Checkpoint
What is the main conclusion of the philosophers' proof in this passage?
A) The soul is a body composed of matter and form.
B) The soul cannot be annihilated because it is not material.
C) The soul is originated and therefore mortal.
D) The soul is a substance that can be destroyed.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that the soul's immateriality entails its incorruptibility, and the debate focuses on whether possibility requires a substrate and whether the soul's survival of matter ensures its survival.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your soul is truly immortal and its final state depends on your actions, what are you actively doing today that will shape your soul's eternal happiness or misery? Name one concrete change you need to make.
?
The passage emphasizes that the soul's perfections require its form (identity) to remain. What is the 'form' of your soul right now? Is it defined by worldly pursuits or by devotion to Allah? Be honest: where do your worries lie?
?
The philosophers argued that the soul cannot cease because it is immaterial. Do you live as though your soul is temporary or eternal? When you face trials, do you respond as if only this life matters, or do you act with the conviction that your soul will endure forever?

The Ash'ari Proof for the Return of the Non-Existent
al-Razi states the Ash'ari position and gives a proof for the possibility of returning the non-existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's core proof that returning the non-existent is possible because its impossibility is contingent on an accidental property (non-existence) that can be removed.

Core Idea
The impossibility of a non-existent thing is accidental, not essential.

Al-Razi begins by stating the Ash'ari position on physical resurrection: the return of the non-existent is possible. He then provides a concise proof that distinguishes between essential and accidental impossibility. This argument forms the foundation for defending the doctrine against rational objections.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic and English to grasp al-Razi's succinct argument.
Notice the logical structure: if impossible essentially, then every like is impossible; if accidental, then removal of accident removes impossibility.
2
Identify the two cases
Al-Razi presents two conditional scenarios: impossibility due to essence vs. due to an accident. Understand each.
3
Connect to the overall debate
Reflect on why this proof is needed: to counter claims that the return is rationally impossible.
This proof shifts the burden to opponents to show impossibility is essential.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولنتكلم الآن في المعاد البدني. مسألة إعادة المعدوم عند أصحابنا جائزة خلافا للفلاسفة والكرامية وأبي الحسين البصري من المعتزلة. لنا أنه بعد العدم إن كان ممتنعا لماهيته أو لشيء من لوازمها وجب امتناع مثله، وإن كان الأمر غير لازم فعند زوال ذلك العارض يزول ذلك الامتناع. لا يقال: الحكم عليه بأنه ممتنع لذاته أو لغيره لا يصح لأن الحكم على الشيء يستدعي امتياز المحكوم عن غيره والامتياز يستدعي الثبوت وهو مناف للعدم، لأنا نقول الحكم عليه بأنه لا يصح الحكم عليه حكم فيكون مناقضا. واحتج المخالف بأمور. أحدها: أن الشيء بعد عدمه في محض العدم ولم تبق هويته أصلا فلا يصح الحكم عليه بصحة العود، لأن المحكوم عليه متميز عن غيره والمتميز ثابت. وثانيها: أنه بتقدير الوقوع لا يتميز عن مثله، وما يفضي إلى أن لا يتميز عن مثله كان باطلا. وثالثها: أنه لو أعيد لأعيد وقته الأول معه يلزم أن يكون مبتدأ مع أنه معاد وذلك تناقض.
Let us now speak about the physical resurrection. Issue: The return of the non-existent is permissible according to our companions, contrary to the philosophers, the Karamiyya, and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri from the Mu'tazila. For us: after non-existence, if it is impossible in its essence or due to something of its concomitants, then the impossibility of its like is necessary. But if the matter is not necessary, then when that accident ceases, that impossibility ceases. It cannot be said: The judgment that it is impossible in itself or due to another is not valid because judgment on a thing requires the distinctness of the judged from other things, and distinctness requires existence, which contradicts non-existence. For we say: The judgment that it is not valid to judge it is itself a judgment upon it, so it is contradictory. And the opponent argued with several matters. First: that after non-existence, the thing is in pure non-existence and its identity does not remain at all, so judgment on the validity of its return is not valid because the judged must be distinct from others, and the distinct is existent. Second: that if it occurs, it would not be distinguishable from its like, and what leads to indistinguishability from its like is invalid. Third: that if it were returned, its first time would have to be returned with it, which would mean it is both initial and returned, and that is a contradiction.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage is a proof and defense of the Ash'ari position that returning the non-existent is possible. al-Razi first states the position and lists opponents. He gives a proof: if something becomes impossible after non-existence due to its essence, then its like would also be impossible; but the impossibility is due to an accident (non-existence), so removing that accident removes the impossibility. He then anticipates an objection: one cannot judge a non-existent because judgment requires distinctness, which requires existence. He refutes this by noting that the objection itself is a judgment on the non-existent, thus self-contradictory. Next, he presents three objections from opponents: (1) the non-existent lacks identity for judgment; (2) if returned, it would be indistinguishable from a similar existent; (3) returning would entail the return of its first time, leading to contradiction. These are not refuted here but set up for further discussion.

Key Terms
إعادة المعدوم
جائز
ممتنع
عارض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that impossibility is always an essential property.
Correction: Al-Razi clarifies that impossibility can be accidental, as in the case of a non-existent thing; the impossibility is due to the lack of existence, not an inherent quality.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi's proof, what determines whether a non-existent thing can return?
A) Its essence
B) Whether its impossibility is essential or accidental
C) The will of God
D) The opinion of philosophers

Key Takeaway
The return of the non-existent is possible because its impossibility is accidental, not essential.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe that Allah has power over all things, but deep down, do you carry the assumption that certain losses are permanent and irrecoverable? What would your life look like if you truly aligned your heart with the Ash'ari proof that nothing is impossible for Allah to return? Will you today consciously refuse to label anything as 'lost forever'?
?
If the impossibility of a non-existent thing is merely accidental, then what does that say about your despair over something you have lost? Are you treating your loss as though it is eternally impossible to return, thus implicitly limiting Allah's power? What step can you take right now to replace that despair with hope in the Returner?
?
The philosophers and some Mu'tazila argued that the return of the non-existent is impossible because non-existence is a necessary state. You may not be a philosopher, but do you live as though your past mistakes or missed opportunities are essentially impossible to redeem? How does this lesson confront that assumption, and what small act of repentance or hope can you commit to today?

Allah's Existence
intermediate
4 min
Refuting the Objection: One Can Judge the Non-Existent
al-Razi refutes the objection that one cannot judge the non-existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's refutation of the objection that judgment cannot be made on a non-existent thing, using the principle of self-refutation.

Core Idea
The objection itself makes a judgment about the non-existent, contradicting its own premise.

After presenting his proof, al-Razi anticipates an objection: how can we judge that a non-existent thing is impossible? Judgment requires an existing subject. Al-Razi cleverly refutes this by pointing out that the objection itself is a judgment on the non-existent, thus self-defeating.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and refutation
Understand the skeptic's argument: judgment requires distinctness and existence. Then read al-Razi's response.
2
Analyze the self-refutation
Notice that al-Razi points out that the statement 'it is not valid to judge it' is itself a judgment on the non-existent.
This is a classic logical maneuver: refusing to judge is still a judgment.
3
Relate to the broader argument
This refutation defends the proof by removing a skeptical meta-objection.
Now the proof stands unchallenged from this angle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولنتكلم الآن في المعاد البدني. مسألة إعادة المعدوم عند أصحابنا جائزة خلافا للفلاسفة والكرامية وأبي الحسين البصري من المعتزلة. لنا أنه بعد العدم إن كان ممتنعا لماهيته أو لشيء من لوازمها وجب امتناع مثله، وإن كان الأمر غير لازم فعند زوال ذلك العارض يزول ذلك الامتناع. لا يقال: الحكم عليه بأنه ممتنع لذاته أو لغيره لا يصح لأن الحكم على الشيء يستدعي امتياز المحكوم عن غيره والامتياز يستدعي الثبوت وهو مناف للعدم، لأنا نقول الحكم عليه بأنه لا يصح الحكم عليه حكم فيكون مناقضا. واحتج المخالف بأمور. أحدها: أن الشيء بعد عدمه في محض العدم ولم تبق هويته أصلا فلا يصح الحكم عليه بصحة العود، لأن المحكوم عليه متميز عن غيره والمتميز ثابت. وثانيها: أنه بتقدير الوقوع لا يتميز عن مثله، وما يفضي إلى أن لا يتميز عن مثله كان باطلا. وثالثها: أنه لو أعيد لأعيد وقته الأول معه يلزم أن يكون مبتدأ مع أنه معاد وذلك تناقض.
Let us now speak about the physical resurrection. Issue: The return of the non-existent is permissible according to our companions, contrary to the philosophers, the Karamiyya, and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri from the Mu'tazila. For us: after non-existence, if it is impossible in its essence or due to something of its concomitants, then the impossibility of its like is necessary. But if the matter is not necessary, then when that accident ceases, that impossibility ceases. It cannot be said: The judgment that it is impossible in itself or due to another is not valid because judgment on a thing requires the distinctness of the judged from other things, and distinctness requires existence, which contradicts non-existence. For we say: The judgment that it is not valid to judge it is itself a judgment upon it, so it is contradictory. And the opponent argued with several matters. First: that after non-existence, the thing is in pure non-existence and its identity does not remain at all, so judgment on the validity of its return is not valid because the judged must be distinct from others, and the distinct is existent. Second: that if it occurs, it would not be distinguishable from its like, and what leads to indistinguishability from its like is invalid. Third: that if it were returned, its first time would have to be returned with it, which would mean it is both initial and returned, and that is a contradiction.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage is a proof and defense of the Ash'ari position that returning the non-existent is possible. al-Razi first states the position and lists opponents. He gives a proof: if something becomes impossible after non-existence due to its essence, then its like would also be impossible; but the impossibility is due to an accident (non-existence), so removing that accident removes the impossibility. He then anticipates an objection: one cannot judge a non-existent because judgment requires distinctness, which requires existence. He refutes this by noting that the objection itself is a judgment on the non-existent, thus self-contradictory. Next, he presents three objections from opponents: (1) the non-existent lacks identity for judgment; (2) if returned, it would be indistinguishable from a similar existent; (3) returning would entail the return of its first time, leading to contradiction. These are not refuted here but set up for further discussion.

Key Terms
حكم
امتياز
ثبوت
مناقض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It is impossible to speak meaningfully about non-existent entities.
Correction: Al-Razi shows we can and do make judgments about non-existents, as the objection itself demonstrates.

Checkpoint
How does al-Razi refute the objection that judgment cannot be made on a non-existent?
A) He says the non-existent can be distinct
B) He says the objection is itself a judgment on the non-existent, so it is self-refuting
C) He says existence is not required for judgment
D) He ignores the objection

Key Takeaway
The objection that one cannot judge the non-existent is self-refuting because it itself is a judgment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you argue against something you don't fully understand, might your own words contain the same flaw you are attacking? Take one belief you oppose and write down your objection; then check if it holds itself to the same standard.
?
Is there a time you dismissed a spiritual truth because you felt you couldn't 'judge' the unseen? How does al-Razi's logical move free you to affirm what revelation teaches, even about non-existence?
?
What claim do you make about Allah's existence that, if examined, relies on assumptions you have not tested? Will you commit to examining your own presuppositions for hidden contradictions today?
